THE RED PAGE.
AS MY GONDOLA GLIDES
JOHN TODHUNTER (b. Dublin, 1839)
has written much and pleasantly in
verse, and one does not know that in
verse anything can be said of him but
that he has written much and pleasantly.
His style is careful, his expression cultured ;
but his pulse, even with the force of Irish
legend behind him, is nearly always too faint
to thrill us. And above all we want to be
thrilled, to have and hold emotion. In his
new little book of Sounds and Sweet Airs
(Lond., Elkin Mathews; Is. net) Todhunter
weds his talent to music in seeking to express
musical moods and musical masterpieces.
Musicians will find interesting his poetical
transcriptions of the meaning of Schubert,
Tchaikovsky, Beethoven, and others; and
the booklet is charming and cheap. As verse,
one prefers the pleasure of quoting portion
ol tile sincerely-felt and happily-turned
IN A GONDOLA.
Suggested by “A Song Without Words." —"Some
Venice of the heart.”
In Venice !—this night so delicious, its air
Full of moonlight and passionate snatches of song,
And quick cries, a/nd perfume of romances which
throng
To my brain as I steal down this marble sea-stair,
And my gondola comes:
And I hear the slow, rhythmical sweep of the oar
Drawing near and more near, and the noise of the
prow,
And the sharp sudden splash of her stoppage. And
now
I step in ; we a/re off o’er the street’s heaving floor,
As my gondola glides —
Away, past these palaces silent and dark,
Looming ghostly and grim o’er their bases, where
clings
Rank seaweed that gleams, flecked with light, as it
swings
To the splash of the waves where they reach the tide-
mark
On the porphyry blocks—with a song full of dole,
A forlorn barcarole,
As my gondola glides l
'
And the wind seems to sigh through that lattice
rust-gnawn
A low dirge for the past—the sweet past, when it
played
In the pearl-braided hair of some beauty, who
stayed
But one shrinking half-minute, her mantle close-
drawn
Round the swell of her bosom, and cheeks passion-
pale,
Ere her lover came by, and they kissed. They are
clay,
Those fire-hearted men with the regal pulse-play ;
“ They are dust! ” sighs the wind with its whisper
of ivails,
“Those women snow-fair, flower-sweet, passion-
pale ! ”
And the waves make reply with their song full of
dole,
Their forlorn barcarole.
As my gondola glides.
Dust—those lovers! But love ever lives, ever new,
Still the same. So we shoot into bustle and light,
And lamps from the festal casinos gleam, bright
On the ripples ; and here's the Rialto in view 1
And black gondolas, ghostlike, slide eerily past.
And the gondoliers cry to each other : a song
Far away, from sweet voices in tune, dies along
The zoaters moon-silvered. So on to the vast
Shadowed span of an arch where the oar-echoes leap
Through chill gloom from the marble ; then moon-
light once more,
And laughter and strum of guitars from the shore,
And sonorous bass music of bells booming deep
From SI. Mai-k’s. Still those waves with their song
full of dole.
Their forlorn barcarole,
As my gondola glides.
Then the silent lagune stretched away through the
night,
And the stars, and the fairy-like City behind,
Domes and towers rising spectral and dim, till the
mind
Is entranced in a vague subtle maze of delight;
And I float in a dream, lose the present—or seem
To have lived it before. Then a sense of deep bliss
Just to breathe, to exist, in a night such as this —
Just to feel what I feel, drowns all else. But the
gleam
Of the lights as we turn to the City once more,
And the music and clangour of bells booming slow
And that vision of visions, St. Mark’s, the star-
glow
For its crown, I shall see l The great moment
draws near
As we glide ; and delight, like an exquisite fear,
Lays a hand on my heart, as I step to the shore—
The Piazzetta ! My life-dream accomplished at last,
As my gondola goes:
I am here, here alone with the ghost of the past!
But the waves still break in with their song full of '
dole,
Their forlorn barcarole,
As my gondola goes :
And the pulse of the oar, swept through silvery
spray,
Dies away in the gloom, dies away—dies away—
dies away !
THE GOSPEL OF PROTECTION
FROM time to time one is asked to indi-
dicate a book on tariff questions
written with special reference to Aus-
tralia. Preferential Trade, by Ben-
jamin Hoare, recently published by Geo.
Robertson, Melbourne (3s. 6 d.) is the book
that applicants want. It looks like the book
of a life-time, the book in which a man has
concentrated all his knowledge and all his
labour. The title is to some extent a mis-
nomer, explained in the preface :
National Trade is Preferential Trade, and the trade of
every nation within itself is the first consideration for
statesmen to aim at. Imperial Preferences are good, but
not as good as domestic Preference. My work is there-
fore for Preferential Trade in its widest signification of a
national policy of Protection.
The signification is a little arbitrary, and one
would have preferred it given as a sub-title. The
book should really have been called National Pro-
tection for Australia. As a treatise on that sub-
ject it is well worth a thoughtful man’s money.
je
Mr. Hoare writes as an advocate of Protection.
Well, we are all Protectionists now. It is quite
clear that Australia can only prosper as a self-
dependent community. To depend upon even a
friendly nation for the essentials of civilised exist-
ence is to nourish weakness that may be fatal.
Australia in British eyes is still “one of the
colonies, ” a British market. The ideal for Aus-
tralians is Australia her own market, with as
much of other markets as our energy and resources
can compass. Trade independence may even
decide the question of this community’s existence ;
and every industrial development is a fresh root
thrown into vital soil. We want foreign trade—
yes; but before foreign trade we want home in-
dustry.
The policy of Protection for Australia is fixed.
There is no longer question of Why ? —but only of
How i and How Much ? The principle stands;
but the extent of the application of the principle
may well depend upon time, and place, and cir-
cumstances. Industry—the rivalry of nations, the
interest of individuals—is not a pitched battle,
but a running fight that never ends. The value
of positions varies : the key to victory is found
now in one and now in another.
A*
Mr. Hoare, like so many of his predecessors,
would fain give us an ideal policy and hold it flaw-
less and speckless. This yearning for a panacea
is a very human yearning. Prom the inventor of
every shibboleth his votaries desire a blessing on
all their ends and sanctification of all means to
gain them. Adam Smith is only Procrustes writ
large, as Benjamin Hoare is writ larger. It is
sufficient to say that Protection is good for Aus-
tralia, and will be good for many a year to come.
It is unnecessary to say that Protection was, is,
and will be good for all the world without end,
Amen. Mr. Hoare does not say this, but his zeal
hints it. If his book had proved less it would
have proved a great deal more.
The plan of the book is to take leading questions
of controversy and hammer them into the shape
desired. Starting with a consideration of the
state of economic science, Mr. Hoare proceeds to
discuss the doctrine of cheapness, the values of
competition and co-operation, and the nature of
trade. Then he passes in review England, Ireland,
India, Prance, America, Turkey, Russia, Ger-
many, the Netherlands, and Australia —and
declares the world’s experience of Pree-trade
unsatisfactory. Free-trade being cast into outer
darkness, in the last section of the book blessed
Protection is invited to come into life eternal.
There is something to debate on almost every
page; but withal such a prodigious amount of
thought and industry has gone to make the book
that it well deserves close study.
—* ♦
The Cingalee case interests Australians. Capt.
Fraser wrote a play. The Hanjiahn , and offered
it to George Edwardes, a leading Eng. theatre-
manager. Edwardes declined The Hanjiahn , and
presently produced The Cingalee, which Fraser
alleged was based on the ideas of his play. A
London jury believed Fraser, and awarded him
30Q0Z. damages.—-*-The point is that all over
Australia innocent men and women are brooding
over literary and dramatic ideas, presently to be
written down and despatched to London. Some
of them get printed; some of them get “lost” ;
and how many of them are absorbed by unscru-
pulous publishers, or agents, or managers, and pre-
sently given to the world in another form, with
another named as author ? A good many, London
gossip declares; and The Cingalee brings apt con-
firmation of London gossip.
“ A statue ” (bust ?) of W. E. Henley, made by
Rodin from that extant by the same sculptor, is
to be erected in Westminster Abbey. One
wonders if it is the Poet they would honour? or
the Jingo ? Possibly both.
The vote for compulsory Greek at Cambridge
(in March last) seems a legitimate expression of
University opinion. If Greek is to be studied at
all, the obvious place for study is a University.
But the field of modern thought is so wide,
and the pressure of modern thought is so
great, that it has become impossible for any
individual to make the best of both worlds.
The Greek grammarian’s funeral can be deferred
only at the cost of a “modern side” dead
from the waist down. Cambridge pays the price,
and perhaps it is as well that somebody should
prefer Plato and Socrates to Veritas. Veritas
is such a vague personality.
In April Steele Rudd’s Magazine , A. Meston, in
the course of an article on “The Evolution of the
Boomerang,” declares that the Egyptian and
Dravidian boomerangs (so-called) are not boome-
rangs at all, and that the “return” boomerang
was originated in Central Australia, travelling
thence to the coastal tribes. This boomerang, he
says, was unknown to the blacks of at least one-
half the continent.

RECENT BOOKS
The Purple Land, by W. H. Hudson (Lond.,
Duckworth; 2s. 6 d. ) is a chapter of the I rue
Romance, and one may love it well: better even
than the same author’s Green Mansions, this
book is the younger, fuller of life and good spirits,
reflecting joyous peregrinations in the Banda
Orientale of South America. For the matter,
it is almost Gil Bias without cynicism ;
for the style, it is almost Maurice Hewlett
without preciosity: indeed it seems fitted tb be
classic. Humour the writer has, and adventures
run to meet him the adventurous, and “Ah,
l’oarystis, la” derniere “maitresse ! ” he will cry
with engaging frequency. And shocking : he will
have it that the free and easy “Orientals
live better lives than some of their “civi-
lised” critics. “We do not live by bread
alone, and British occupation does not give
to the heart all the things it craves. Bless-
ings may even become curses when the gigantic
power that bestows them on us scares from our
midst the shv spirits of Beauty and Poesy.”
Hudson dares to quote with approbation the scan-
dalous sentiments of John Carrickfergus, who sold
his Scotch birthright for a mess of Oriental
chickens:
“ Edinburgh was my home. Had enough reading when
I was a boy ; heard enough psalm-singing’, saw enough
scrubbing and scouring to last me my lifetime. My
father was a bookseller in the High Street, near the Cow-
gate—you know ! Mother, she was pious—they were all
pious. Uncle, a minister, lived with us. That was all
worse than purgatory td me. I was educated at the High
School—intended for the ministry, ha, ha ! My only
pleasure was to get a book of travels in some savage
country, skulk into my room, throw off my boots,
light a pipe, and lie on the floor reading-
locked up from everyone. Sundays just the same.
They called me a sinner, said I was going to the devil —
fast. It was my nature. They didn’t understand —kept
on ding-donging in my ears. Always scrubbing, scouring
—you might have eaten your dinner off the floor ; always
singing psalms—praying—scolding. Couldn’t hear it ;
ran away at fifteen, and have never heard a word from
home since. What happened ? I came here, worked,
saved,. bought land, cattle ; married a wife, lived as I
liked to live—am happy. There’s my wife—mother of six
children —you have seen her yourself, a woman for a man
to be proud of. No ding-donging, black looks, scouring
from Monday to Saturday—you could n’t eat your dinner oft
my kitchen floor. There are my children, six of ’em, all told,
hoys and girls, healthy, dirty as they like to be, happy as the
day’s long; and here am I, John Carrickfei’gus—Don
Juan all the country over, my surname no native can pro-
nounce —respected, feared, loved ; a man his neighbour
can rely on to do him a good turn ; one who neyer hesi-
tates about putting a bullet in any vulture, wild cat, or
assassin that crosses his path. Now you know all.”
“ An extraordinary history,” I said, “ but Isupposeyou
teach your children something?”
“ Tfeach ’em nothing,” he returned with emphasis.
“ All we think about in the old country are hooks, cleanli-
ness, clothes ; what ’s good for soul, brain, stomach, and
we make ’em miserable. Liberty for everyone—that’s my
rule. Dirty children are healthy, happy children.”
One needs such a counterblast to “ the New
Syllabus.”
The Stepping Stone, by Helen Colvill (Lond.,
Constable; 2s. Qd.) looks like the old-fashioned
Family Herald novel brought up to the date of the
Newest Woman. There was a good deal to be
said for the mixture of sloppy sentiment and tall
talk that we call the Family Herald novel. As
melodrama, it was absurdly inartistic ; but it did
urge the old familiar Instincts every time. Even
Art cannot afford to dispense with Instincts; though
some artists try to make it. Hall Caine knows
better, and it is only to be desired that he would
become the artist that Hugo was (occasionally).
“ In the beginning was the Word,” says Scripture.
No ; in the beginning was Humanity. In the end
is the Word ; and literary art consists in setting
the twain partners to a country-dance, “hands
joined and down the middle.” The Word bows
for subtilising Man of course : the Woman curtsies
for primal Human Instincts. Helen Colvill pipes
a curious tune for them, with breaks and lapses
that are part of her inexperience or (more likely)
part of her nature. There are enough ingenuity
and novelty in the story to make it readable.
The Seething Pot, by George A. Birmingham
(Lond., Arnold ; 2s. 6d.), will interest every Irish-
man and Irish-Australian. It is practically a
sketch of Irish political and social forces
at the present day, with characters and in-
cidents taken from the history of the past
twenty years. Parnell figures as “ John O’Neill,”
and the picture of him is vivid. His struggle
with the Church is described with a change of
crisis : the Church appears as the selfish enemy of
Irish national aspirations, yet as the humane
helper necessary to individual Irishmen ; —and the
author confronts the dilemma despairingly. It is
apparently George Moore that is satirised as
Dennis Browne, and the satire is not creditable.
Moore may be a fool as a practical politician, but as
a literary artist he is a rare bird in Irish latitudes :
the island of to-day cannot show a brighter one.
The book touches Australia in bringing the central
character thence to inherit an Irish estate : he is
shown as the Austral-born son of an expatriated
“rebel,” and is not in the least like what such a
figure might be expected to be. If he were an
Irish-American he could scarcely seem more
unreal to us. This falsity makes the book seem to
begin badly : it improves as it goes, and ends by
leaving the impression that the author is honest
but intellectually limited, truthful and occasion-
ally mistaken.
J*
It must be admitted that Mrs. Humphry Ward
is writing better. She no longer preaches at us
on religious questions ; and her moral purpose, if
still felt, is deliberately evaded as far as possible.
What is surprising is to find her book sometimes
nearly naughty in the endeavour to be in-
teresting. One grieves that Mrs. Ward should
abandon the side of the angels: what, oh what
would Mr. Gladstone say if he could see !
The Marriage (Lond., Macmillan ; 2s. 6 d.) pro-
ceeds on the lines of Lady Rose’s Daughter.
Having realised that her unsupported imagination
is worth little, Mrs. Ward takes actual personages
with well-marked traits of life and on _ the
foundation of these erects her story. Lady Kitty,
the new heroine, becomes thus a real and fascina-
ting character. She is contrasted in her French
brightness and charm with a sensible English
maiden, and (very badly for the Moral) is per-
mitted to pick up both the men of the book while
the sensible Englishwoman starves (and cheats !)
for love. These men do not impress one, hut the
first half of the story is interesting. Then, when
the characters have been exposed and the plot
has to be finished, it becomes rather wearisome.
The sense of strain that one feels in all
Mrs. Ward’s books —as if she were trying to get a
little more out of her talent than her talent is
worth —accompanies one again through The Mar-
riage ; but the strain is so far efficacious that the
new book cannot be called dull. The generous
publishers give us Albert Sterner’s charming iTi'is-
trations for the mere half-crown. as illustra-
tions they do not bring conviction, but the deli-
cacy of Sterner’s brush is a delight.
Arthur Adams has some verses descriptive of
Bond-street in March Monthly Review. You can
tell an Adams poem in the dark by the glittering
exclamation-points that hedge his lines. He
scorns Bond-street as “ a little lane of useless
things ” decorated with languid women, champing
horses, and bloated pugs.
And, a suave carven god of jade, j
By some enthralled old Asian made,
With that thin scorn still on his lips,
Waits, in a window-front displaced:
The hurrying, streaming croivds he sees,
With the same smile he ivatehes these
As from his temple-dusk he saw
The passing of the centuries !
The exclamation-marks are bad form, of course;
since a reader, like a nag, may justly resent being
spurred all the time.
Adams, by the way, is returning to Maoriland
via Sydney next month : “I want some sun-
shine.” He has done pretty well with his talent,
and may reckon upon a moderate Eng. market for
the future. America has rather taken to him
(as America should).
In March Contemporary A. Kinnear relates the
ruin of Eng Parliamentary reporting. In the
good old Gladstonian days first-flight politicians
got “a verbatim note.” Few nowadays get even
“ a full report.” The public won’t read long
speeches, and the newspapers are glad to escape
paying the tax on space and reporting staff. The
Times alone preserves a semblance of the old
tradition; and only The Daily _ Telegraph,
and Morning Post besides maintain special
staffs in ‘‘the Gallery.” The rest are served by
fiercely-competing news agencies, which will supply
a nightly report at sgs. per week or 120 gs. for the
session ; while a special staff may cost 1440g5. per
session. There are nearly 26p “ Gallery men ”at
present, drawing as reporters about 6gs. per week
each, or as sketch and leader writers from 8 to
15gs. Special reports can be got as low as 10s.
per col.; and a big daily can have all its general
Britisli news for as little as 500 1. per annum.
From St. James’s Budget, Lond., April 7 :
Russia Shocked.— The Novoe Vremya has been devoting
a good deal ot attention to the Western Australian
Government’s inquiry into the reported ill-usage of the
aboriginals by the settlers. “in the name of our common
humanity,” it says, “should not Russia make some pro-
test against this continued infliction of unnameable tor-
tures on the remnants of an unfortunate aboriginal
community ? ”
They manage some things pretty well in Paris.
Here is Lepine, prefect of police, confronted with
a Latin quarter riot and...joining the riot ! At
least, he quells it by enrolling himself a permanent
member of the Students’ Association : which is as
if the local Commissioner, to calm a rowdy Uni.
procession, posed as an angel of peace on a trolly.
E. Lucas, quoted the other day as a humourist,
has had a curious encounter with the English law
of copyright. Mr. Lucas is known as a devotee
and editor of Charles Lamb, and is now watching
through press a new edition of the works of
Charles and Mary Lamb, which he cannot
make complete for the reason that many
letters are held as copyright by different pub-
lishers. The law of letters is this : the paper and
ink, and the substance in other words, belong to
the recipient; but the copyright, the right of
publishing copies of a letter, belongs to the writer.
Many of Charles Lamb’s letters have been printed
within the last few years, and each, as printed,
started on its separate career of 42 years’ copy-
right from the time of publication. Suppose that
a letter of Sbakespear’s were found. The pro-
perty in the letter would be independent of the
copyright, which would apparently belong to
. Shakespear’s heirs, if discoverable ; and copyright
would commence whenever the letter was pub-
lished, no matter how long after Shakespear’s
death.
A.G.S.

NERVOUS PROSTRATION
A Remarkable Cure in Redfern.
The Case of Mrs. M. J. BUDD.
(By a Special Reporter.)
The time has arrived when it is im-.
peculive that the afflicted members of
the community should take steps to
protect themselves against the evils
which tend to render their lives un-
happy ; and as so many go on suffering
year after year through lack of ser-
viceable information, it is deemed ad-
visable to place before the public the
story of Mrs. Mary Jane Budd, of 38
young-street, Redfern, so that a salu-
tary lesson may be learned. What Mrs.
Budd said to our special was this
“It is now eight years since my hus-
band died, a calamity that took place
when we had been living in this house
nine years, so you see 1 have been re-
siding here a good while.”
“Yes,” assented the reporter. I be-
lieve you are an old resident of these
parts ?"
“1 was born in Bourke-street, and
have lived in this locality all my life.
But to go back to my story. It was
not long after my great bereavement
that I became a total wreck. The blow
nearly killed me, and for a good while
I was really stupefied with grief. Tor
some considerable time my memory
was completely lost, and it was im-
possible for" me to concentrate my
mind for a few minutes upon any sub-
ject. When my dazed condition passed
away it was then that I realised how
terribly my whole system had suffered,
a fact which dawned upon me as I
stood at the table one day feeling so
ill that l did not think 1 would live
till the morrow. For my own part I
did not care what happened, but I
thought of my three little children,
and said to myself : ‘Oh, God ! What
ever can I take to do me good for
their sakes ?’ ”
“Was that the lirst time you thought
of taking anything ?”
“It was the first time I thought of
acting on my own judgment, the rea-
son being that 1 was under a doctor
for some length of time, and his treat-
ment hadn’t benefited me In the
least.”
“What did your medical attendant
say was wrong with you ?”
“He told me i had taken my afi lo-
tions inwardly, and that my nervous
system was in a very weak state. I
knew that well enough, unfortunately,
for T could not even bear to hear the
children talking, their voices got on my
nerves so, and if a tap came to the
door i nearly fainted with fright. I
hadn’t the least desire for food, and I
think one of my greatest afflictions
was my inability to get to sleep. For
four years I did not know what it was
to sleep properly for one night. It was
usual for me to remain awake till
about three or four O'clock in the
morning, and then doze off till half-
past five, when I always awoke with a
start, and would get no more sleep
till next morning. And the dreams I
had each time I slept—they were aw-
ful. But my mind was always distort-
ed with hideous fancies for that mat-
ter. When 1 put the light out at
night, 1 imagined I coukd see some-
body sitting on a chair in front of
me. The form would then stealthily
approach me, and for a time I could
neither speak or move. After a great
struggle, however, I would manage to
gasp, ‘Who’s there ?’ and whilst I lay
there awaiting an answer my feelings
of pent-up terror were dreadful. Many
of the neighbours could tell you how
often I have mentioned to them the
things I imagined I coul'd see, and
how, when I thought somebody was
sitting by mv side, I screamed aloud
for help. And after those frights I
was very bad indeed—so exhausted
that I could scarcely move; in fact/'
“You must have lost weight during
your illness ?”
“Oh ! I was ghastly. My bones
were almost protruding through tn\.
shin, and anybody seeing me ■ conic
not have thought otherwise than that
1 would never get better again. i
really thought I was doomed to die.
and feel sure I would have been dead
long ago only for Clements Tonic,
which was the last medicine of all
that I took. A friend often used to
say, ‘Why don’t you try some Clem-
ents Tonic ?’ but 1 always postponed
.doing so ; but at last 1 thought that
as I had heard so much about it, -and
nothing else could do me any good, I
would give Clements Tonic a trial.
This was the decision that I came to
the day that I stood at the table
feeling that I could not last another
twenty-four hours, so w 7 hat better rea-
son than that could I have for saying
that Clements Tonic saved my life ?
You must understand that I was quite
incapable of doing any work, and that
it was a common thing for me to be
in bed a fortnight at a time, utterly
unable to attend to anything that re-
quired seeing to, so when Clements
Tonic began to put a little life and
strength into me you can imagine how
thankful I was for it. And weren’t
the neighbours surprised to hear that
] had taken a turn for the better ?
JVlv word, it was a matter that aston-
ished nearly the. whole of Redferfe. But
what a relief it was to have my head-
aches ease off after suffering so long
with them ! , And wasn’t I glad to
feel that Clements Tonic was also do-
ing good to my, nervous system ?
Wny, I had only been taking: that
remedy a few weeks when I could stand
noises' of any kind without wincing,
and already I was getting more sleep
than I had had for years.’’ ■
“Bid you feel mold refreshed after it
than formerly ?’’
“Oh ! yes ; much. The reason why,
I suppose, was that I did not dream
at all, so both my brain and body
were resting all the time, and when I
awoke I felt so fresh and light heart-
ed—no nasty taste in the mouth as
before, a clean tongue, and, my word,
what an appetite for breakfast 1 had !
Clements Tonic did make me eat rnd
no mistake, and my nerves were get-
ting stronger and stronger as the days
went by. Eventually my old fears
and imaginations died right away,
nothing ever troubling me, and all
this wonderful change was entirely due
to Clements Tonic. Oh !_ I have
thanked God thousands of times that
I Was guided to take that remedy, and
I am so anxious that other sufferers
may regain hope by hearing of my
cure. But Clements Tonic served me
well in other ways. It put an end to
the giddy feelings I used to have, re-
moved the swollen feeling from mv
eyes and the suffocating sensations
from my chest, then, finally, built up
my strength so perfectly that every-
one could see my health was thor-
oug-hly regained.’’
“May 1 publish your remarks ?'’
“I gladly give you permission to
publish them in any way.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, MARY JANE BUDD, ol No. 38 Young-
street, Redfern, in the State of New
South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing' of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of icy illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements —which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment : and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
/3
- of
Subscribed and declared at Redfern this
twenty-seventh day of August, one thousand nin<
hundred and four, before me,
THOS. CADDY, J.T.

Various Verses.
A Vagabond.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
When eagerly I sought the road
There gleamed before my sight
The city of Great Hopes Attained—
A vision of delight !
Alluring flowers were in vain
To tempt me from the track ;
My eyes were all in front, nor looked
To either side or back.
The road branched off on every side
And twisted in and out,
And finding it no single way
I first began to doubt.
For to my anxious-toned request
I found each road was right—
I had a hundred eager guides,
Each with the only light.
So, pausing in perplexity,
The wayside flowers’ scent
Appealed in beauty to my soul
And filled me with content.
Now, idling, mock I those who mock :
“ It is not there—or there,”
They say : while I, far wiser, ask
“ Why is it any whore ? ”
N.S.W.
HAMER.
To-day.
The grass hath drawn its tide across thy grave,
And, like a pearl within its heart of wave,
A daisy lifts its head ;
While, sweeter than the double reeds of Pan,
Or violin of some soul-trembling man,
The river sings a-bed.
And, in the fragrant, blossom-spotted hedge,
Or where the clover ripples in the sedge,
Glister the wings of bees.
0 heart o’ me, O heart V me, my love,
Canst thou not hear the cooing, milk-white dove
Between the crested trees !
I have forgiv’n thee—And thine error, too,
I have forgot; O dear one, let me woo
Thee, as thou art, in Death.
I will thicket thy grave with roses red
And gold—with thorns to guard and buds to
spread
Jewels at ev’ry breath.
N.s.w,
HUGH M'CRAE.
Cre o.
I do believe, although I may not know,
That Something stands behind this passing
show—
Something omnipotent, aloof, benign—
A God, it may be, knowing me and mine,
Weighing and sifting, winnowing perfectly,
Our wheat and chaff. It is enough for me.
In my Lord’s hands I am content to rest,
Asking no favors. If He knoweth best,
Why should I prompt him amateurishly ?
If He knows not, nor cares, as well may be,
lam content. I whimper not nor pray.
For me the chances of each golden day,
The moment’s joy hard-won in Fate’s despite,
The splendid solaces of fragrant night,
The prizes wrenched against o’erwhelming odds
From out the very bosoms of the gods.
I love my world in Carnival or Lent.
God’s in his Heaven ? Perhaps. lam content.
I do believe that manhood makes the man ;
And I love men, and hate the puling clan
Of sophists and ascetics dank and sere
Who poison all the sweetness of the year.
To do and dare, to seize all life can give,
To live exuberantly while we live ;
To gather in the purple and the gold
With both hands eagerly, to grasp and hold
The very kernel ®f each moment’s joy ;
To keep sensation pure from base alloy ;
To think our secret thoughts at need out loud ;
To make no base concession to the crowd ;
To love the sun and breathe no borrowed breat..;
To keep our Ego stalwart te the death —
All this is to live honestly and well,
True to one’s self. All else is squalid hell
Of inanition. Better waste one’s prime
In wild attempts beyond the stars to climb
Than rot respectably before one’s time.
I naturally love all womankind,
And yield them fealty of heart and mind.
So long as they are women staunch and true,
So long as they are women through and through,
So long as they are women body and blood,
They must be sweet and in their essence good.
The other sort, the millinered pretence,
With neither pulse nor purpose, sex nor sense ;
Morally hipless, futile as a worm . . .
(One needs an adjective to make her squirm)—
Our Perfect Lady of the Poison-Brews.
Instinctively I hate your squawking shrews
And smirking spinsters of suburban routs . .
Still . . . Phyllis turns her head away and pouts
The coy refusal that demands a kiss.
The Queen still guards the pearly gates of bliss,
Where we have passed with Lalage and Tess,
And Inez, and that exquisite lithe Bess,
Whose eyes condoned audacities they spurned,
And sweet, warm Kate, who’d blush at half a
word,
And yet eclipsed Columbus in her realm . . .
“ Youth at the prow and Pleasure at the helrr.”
(The phrase seems somehow twisted as I quotj) . .
Fair Hebe’s dimples and bluff Pan’s shrill not-. . .
These things make life worth while, when all .3
said ;
And, these things lacking, one were Letter dead.
TAS.
FAANK MORTON.

the lining membrane of the
nose and adjoining passages.
If this inflammation is not
arrested it invades the pas-
sages which lead from the
nose to the head, ears, throat
and lungs. It injures the
sight and hearing, destroys
the sense of taste and smell,
renders the breath offensive,
breaks down the affected tis-
sues, consumes the nasal car-
tileges, and rots away the
small frontal bones of the
skull. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and sto-
mach, causes dyspepsia, also
consumption. Do you want
relief and cure? If so, try
our great remedy.

The N. S. W. Railway
Commissioners select
RUBEROID
ROOFING.
The supreme test of the practical quality and
durability of a roofing material is on an engine-
house. Every durable quality it possesses is
tested, and sometimes simultaneously. The roofing
must be able to permanently resist
1. Acids, alkalies, smoke, gases and fumes.
2. Intense heat or varying temperatures.
3. Vibratory strains, expansion & contraction.
The N.5.W. Railway Commissioners have proved
that RUBEROID will resist all these conditions,
and a large number of railway buildings are
row being roofed with RUBEROID.

Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director .
How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin 'will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. ivill be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not, responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 18, 1905.
EMPIRE DAY.
May 24 is to be. “Empire Day ”in Australia. To
mark this official feast-day of St. Jingo, the children
in the public schools will gather together to sing
hymns of blood and battle, in glory of a country
which is not their own ; and be thus taught, in-
directly, to neglect and despise the land which is
their heritage and trust. Identify patriotism
with “ the Empire ” in the mind of the Australian
child—the Empire of Jingo ideals—and patriotism
is at once dissociated from a love for Australia.
For there is nothing more certain than that the
British Imperialism of to-day—the Imperialism
of the Boer war, of the Chinese slave-trade, and
of the Japanese alliance —is utterly antagonistic
to white Australian ideals. The fact does not
lack recognition from Australian Imperialists. It
is, indeed, often the sole reason why they are Im-
perialists. Australian nationalism stands for de-
mocratic equality; for the fostering of home
industries ; for such extension of State Socialism
as will give to the people the control
of great national services and monopolies; for
making the hire worthy of the laborer ; for white-
race purity. Therefore, there are ranged against
Australian nationalism all the weak-minded snobs
who can see no other hope of standing out a little
from the ruck than by securing an Imperial bauble :
all the Freetrade importers to whom an Australian
industry represents an injury, and who look upon
this country as a satrapy of London, to be ex-
ploited for the extension of its markets; all the
foes to liberal industrial legislation, who see in
“the Empire” the best hope of the sweater; all
the cheap-labor advocates, who hope to see
this continent given over to the coolies and
their slave-drivers. From these, the Tories, the
snobs and the sweaters, are the ranks of the
Jingo-Imperialists recruited. With them are a
few sincere men, who honestly believe that the
world-interests of the Anglo-Saxon race are best
to be served by Australia limping after England
in apeish imitation, and trying to conform to a
type which is foreign to its natural conditions.
Their sincerity calls for some respect, even if their
folly earns contempt. But such folk are few.
The leading spirits of Imperialism in Australia
are Imperial because they are anti-Australian.
There is nothing hostile to England in Australian
Nationalism : it indeed holds out the hope of a
protector and an asylum when, as must happen in
the ordinary course of events, the balance of Euro-
pean power swings adverse to Britain. There is
< verything hostile to Australia in the British
Imperialism of to-day, with its ideals of war, con-
quest, pillage, and servile labor.
It is, perhaps, mainly the hope of securing' ser-
vile labor that makes the Fat man in Australia
nowadays so ardent an Imperialist. He sees the
result of Imperial success in S’Africa in the hordes
of Chinese slaves packed in the coolie-ships for the
Rand, and hopes for a like happy result here. As
he sings of the “dear old hag,” in his thoughts
it is waving over a slave-ship, and the “mother-
country ” is the country which can take the rule
of Australia out of the hands of Australian
citizens, and give him cheap Japanese coolies for
coal-mine, gold-mine, factory and field. Whilst
Australia is self-governing and independent, he
knows Australia will be white. Let Australia be
induced to give up her self-government and be-
come a partner in, and obedient to, a Council of
Empire, which is mostly nigger Empire, and he
hopes that cheap colored labor will flow to these
shores. Nor is the Tory capitalist without some
grounds for believing that devotion to the Empire
is likely to be a good thing for dividends. London
does something to encourage that view. Mark
this, from the London correspondent of a Scotch
paper, the Stonehaven News :
Another matter which will follow the cessation of
hostilities is the question of the colonisation of Australia.
It has, however, been remarked by a great authority that
it was with the purpose of finding room for her surplus
population that Japan made the now famous alliance
with Great Britain. Australia is on the verge of bank-
ruptcy, and the Labor Party show every sign of making
the condition of the country much worse than at present.
A poll-tax of £lOO per head is levied on the incoming
Oriental by the Commonwealth Government. Japan will
make strenuous efforts to secure free entry to the Aus-
tralian continent, and there can be little doubt that the
continent will be much improved thereby. With Japan
paramount in the Far Fast and having a big interest in
Australia, Britain and Japan would dominate the world.
That hint is sufficient to make Australian capi-
talism as ardently Imperialistic as was the Hebrew
capitalism of Johannesburg. For capitalism knows
no nation. Cash is its country and gold its god.
* * * *
The Australian democracy has of late come to
a fair understanding of this, and shows its judg-
ment of Imperialism by dooming the fall of almost
every politician who accepts an Imperial decora-
tion. But if Australian children, whilst their
minds are plastic to sentimental impressions and
little receptive of logical ideas, can be taught to
be little Imperialists, to bawl with their infant
trebles for blood and conquest and Asiatic alliances,
there is some hope for Jingo-Imperialism in the
future. A large proportion of voting citizens,
even in a specially intelligent community such as
Australia, never checks its early impressions by
its later-acquired reasoning powers. Teach the
children to be Imperialists and a certain propor-
tion of them will remain so, even when a power of
reasoning has been developed that would, if
exercised, show the inconsistency of their ideas
with Australian conditions and aspirations. There
is thus an evil subtlety in concentrating the effort
of Empire Day in corrupting the young. If the
twigs can be bent crooked many will grow up
crooked, and the future will see a party in Aus-
tralia ready to put the fate of this continent in a
pool to be a counter in some calculations of policy
between England and Japan.
* * * st
The Bulletin, therefore, in all seriousness,
urges those Australian parents who are Austra-
lians first—who are earnest in the belief that the
one sure duty of the people holding this lonely
outpost in the Asiatic seas is to keep it free, and
clean, and white—-to gather their children round
them on May 24, and tell them of their own land.
Tell them how, by the destiny of Providence, from
a bad and tainted beginning, a little free people
grew up here, washed by the clear seas, purified
by the breath of the gum forests. How, with the
knowledge abroad of great open fields here ; where
no man was landlord or master, and neither wood
nor wood game had been divinely set apart
for squire or parson, and hats were touched to
none except in genuine chivalry and respect; the
best of the men from England, Scotland, Ireland,
and many another European land came to Aus-
tralia, seeking freedom and scope for manly self-
respect. How with the gold discoveries the old
world again bled its veins of some of its best blood
to populate this continent with the most vigorous,
the most enterprising, those most scornful of mas-
ters and kings. How, as these people built and
ploughed and forged, growing free as their great
gum-trees, but careless of public responsibilities,
the lesser men among them set up Parliaments
and laws, and bowed in the house of Rimmon,
and made Australian politics a thing apart
from and lower than Australian life. How the
stain of servile politics is still on the land, but that
the Australian folk are coming gradually to know
of this, and have learned that it is not all to build
a home, but that a people must, too, build strong
the bulwarks of law and freedom around that home,
and put its mind somewhat to the business of
politics. How it is the duty of every Australian
child to grow up to love his own land, where life is
free and no man is born duke or lord, and any may
look to rule who has the power within him. How
of all things it is needful to keep the white blood
pure, else will Australia come down to the despised
ranks of the outcasts. How he should hold fast
to the thought that there is nothing of good that
his country is not able to do, and nothing of evil
that it should ever consent to do.
* * * *
So much surely the Australian parent may tell
his child without disloyalty to any Empire idea
that is worth an honest man’s thought. But so
much he cannot say without the deepest hostility
to the ideas of those who will assemble Australian
children in the schools on May 24th to celebrate
Jingo-Imperialism.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Strike of Judges.
N.S. Wales Supreme Court Judges are still on
strike; and the Attorney-General, who is sup-
posed to represent their employers, the State,
instead of taking steps to compel them to do the
work which they had agreed to do, cordially winks
at their conduct. For this is no ordinary strike.
The J udgeshad contracted to do certain work—viz.,
to administer the laws of the State. According
to the Government’s report, they have declined to
do part of the work—viz., administer the Arbi-
tration Act. And instead of simply calling upon
them to do their duty, the Government claps
its hands and says “ They won’t.” But it doesn’t
make any move to stop the pay of the Judges on
strike. What Justice Prynnge, for example,
would say if a miner were brought before him in
similar circumstances, one can pretty easily ima-
gine. Probably something like this
Prisoner at the bar, your conduct admits of no excuse
whatever. You agreed to do certain work—unpleasant
work it might turn out to be ; but all that was taken
into account when the conditions were fixed, and you
accepted those conditions. Now, when work which is
distasteful to you turns up, you refuse to do it. It is
no excuse that it is distasteful: if you were oniy expected
to do agreeable work the conditions of your employment
would have been made very different. You say
that it is not your work any more than it is
the work of your fellow-workmen. But you all
agreed to do it, and you are all equally liable for your re-
fusal to do it. And not only that, but with an effrontery
that would be difficult to parallel, you not only refuse to
do the disagreeable part of the work which you have con-
tracted to do—you have not only gone on strike after
binding yourself to perforin this service, but you present
yourself for your wages,as though there had been no de-
fault on your part. And I deeply regret to observe that
your employer’s agent had, and still has, so small a regard
for his principals’ interests that he paid you this money.
Then his Honor would pass sentence, and inci-
dentally express the opinion that the agent should
make good the money he had paid away. Practi-
cally this is the position with regard to the
Judges and the Arbitration Court. It is un-
thinkable that it can remain at that. If
Judges can go on strike against the administration
of one law, they can hang up any law ; and that
means that the government passes from Parliament
and the people to the Judiciary, which is appointed
not for its political opinions at all. Years ago, S.
Australia found itself in a somewhat similar diffi-
culty. Justice Boothby, newly im ported, thought
as little of “colonial” Acts as the N.S.W. Supreme
Court Bench evidently thinks of the Arbitration
law. Grand juries had long been abolished in the
province; Boothby disagreed with that course,
and went on strike against trying accused persons
who had not passed through the hands of a grand
jury. Prisoners were therefore kept waiting for
trial. But, happily for S. Australia, it had an
Attorney-General who fulfilled his obligations to
the people. The Judge was charged with obstruct-
ing the administration of j ustice; and under an Act
of George 111., the recalcitrant was “amoved.”
The precedent is worth remembering at this junc-
ture. And, even if there were no precedent, it is
an occasion that calls for the establishment of one.

Again Arbitration.
According to the testimony of Lord Ranfurly,
ex-Governor of Maoriland, who will be accepted
even by the Tories as a witness of truth :
In Maoriland the Arbitration Act had been generally
successful. It had resulted in the practical abolition of
strikes, and even of serious industrial disagreements.
The M.L. Act is practically the same in principle
and detail as the N.S.W. Act. Except that the
N.S.W. Act allows railway servants to take
advantage of its courts, the two laws are almost
identical. Both allow a “common rule” and
“preference to unionists.” Why is it that in
M.L. the Act is an assured success, and in N.S.W.
its fate now trembles in the balance with Attor-
ney-General Wade keeping a strangle-hold on its
throat ? It is easy to answer the question by
a method of exhaustive analysis. Compare M.L.
conditions with those of N.S.W. until points of
difference arise, and among those points will
be found the reason. Is the Maorilander
naturally more law-abiding than the Waler, more
obedient as an employee, more reasonable as an
employer ? Does the climate make the differ-
ence? Is the M.L. Judge more trusted
than the Judge in N.S.W. ? All such attempts at
explanation are inherently absurd. The true
reason is undoubtedly to be found in the fact that
in N.S.W. the Act has from the first been the
subject of venomous press attack. The Tory
press in this State has lied the Act down to such
an extent that now an unsympathetic Govern-
ment holds it prostrate and prepares its strangu-
lation. The position recalls again the need that
exists for the democracy to protect itself against
the press. If The Bulletin says that
John Smith is a scoundrel it must be
prepared to prove the truth, and the public
advantage of publishing the truth, of its words or
pay damages. If The Bulletin were to say, as
another paper said the other day, that the
Arbitration Court is a dangerous institution,
which was stifling the progress of the State, which
was the official slave of the trades-unions and the
torture-chamber of employers, it should be
equally liable to punishment if unable to prove
its statements. Free, nay violent, discussion of
political principles—even if incorrect in statement
and argument—comes within legitimate press
liberty, but it is absurd to allow a newspaper to
recklessly and falsely defame Parliament, the
Supreme Court, the Arbitration Court, or any
other public institution without penalty. If the
Carruthers Government succeeds in strangling
the Arbitration Act, it will be because lying
newspapers have smoothed the way to the crime.
Our Export Trade in Lies.
When Australians realise fully their responsi-
bilities they will do something with the people
who send lying cablegrams to the London papers.
The Daily Mail printed a cable from Sydney at
the time of the trouble over the Orient Co.’s mail,
“that the Customs had arrested two Lascars on a
trumped-up charge about walking-sticks, to pre-
vent the P. and 0. mail steamer sailing.” That
was a lie in every detail. From one Australian
office the cables carry on a systematic and
persistent misrepresentation. The work of
this correspondent is lately to show that
Melbourne is against a “Made in Australia”
policy. A month ago two very good Melbourne
citizens stood for the vacancy M‘Eacharn left in
the Melbourne City Council. The contest was a
purely personal one, carried out in the friendliest
spirit, and no one in Melbourne had any notion
that any political question was being fought out.
Mr. Frank Davies beat Mr. Charles Atkins.
The cable faker at this end cabled to London that
Mr. Atkins, “aleaderof the ‘Made in Australia’
movement,” was beaten. The matter of the elec-
tion was not, politically, worth the cost of a wire
to Collingwood, much less to London, and the in-
ference meant to be drawn in London is plain; and
it is a lie. But the cable man deserves thanks for
the phrase, a “ Made in Australia” man. That is
the sort of man wanted ; and in future we might
do worse than put the mad e-in-Australia man'
always in front, even in municipal elections. ’ ‘

Closer Settlement.
The Myall Creek farms offered by the Govern-
ment not having moved off so freely as was ex-
pected, it is now understood that the N.S.W.
Government will hold its hand in the matter of
resumption of private estates, “as there is not so
much demand as was represented for closer settle-
ment.” The decision, if come to, will be an ex-
cellent one; but the reason for it, if it is '
the one alleged, will be absurd. There
is a pressing need for closer settlement,
and a keen demand for small farm areas.
But to buy private lands, necessarily at a
high price, cut them up, and offer the smaller areas
to the settlers, again necessarily at a high price, is
not the method which promises success. Nor is it
in any way a just method. The good land of the
State, that is to a large extent locked up now by
monopolists waiting to be bought out, can be
thrown open to the people by a much simpler,
more equitable, less expensive and more effective
method. The only just title to land is its use.
Tax the land until the monopolist lets go
his grip, and the small settler can enter
with his fertilising plough. That method
will call for no expensive purchase, will
bring no business to the land-shark waiting
in his unwholesome den to levy on the
man with the earth-hunger, will save scandal
as well as expense. And it will break no right,
human or divine, will enable sanctity to hover
over a great many more homes, and make the
marriage-tie possible to a great many more lads and
lasses. Closer settlement by purchase will always
be a peddling business, sometimes a corrupt busi-
ness. Closer settlement by adequate land taxation
is the sensible and just system.
Immaculate England!
There is some explanation for England’s unpopu-
larity abroad, which causes so much virtuous
surprise to the Englishman, in a speech from
Lord Lansdowne, in the House of Lords, last
week. Referring to the report of Dr. Roth on
the ill-treatment of Westralian natives, the British
Minister for Foreign Affairs
confessed, with indignation and humiliation, that such
things could happen under the British flag. A mortifying
feature was that the report was calculated in the future
to deprive our remonstrances to other Powers on the
treatment of aborigines under their protectorate of a
great deal of the force which those remonstrances had
hitherto commanded.
All the inferences from, and some of the direct
statements in, that speech show a deplorable
Pharisaism. It represents in its worst form the
traditional English attitude, “ I thank God I am
not as others are.” The facts as to the ill-treat-
ment of the Westralian blacks are horrible, and
constitute a grave reflection on the administration
of one Australian State; but they give no shadow
of justification for this hypocritical English atti-
tude—this sorrow that the honor of England,
immaculate England, is for the first time stained
by one of her children, to such an extent that in
future she cannot do the heavy hypocrite in
foreign councils. Let us examine the facts.
The worst of the crimes in Westralia were
committed when that State was a Crown
colony, and was therefore directly governed by
England. So soon as the State received respon-
sible government there began a betterment,
though the fact that the men and the ideas of the
Crown colony era still survived made that,
betterment slow. But genuine reform came when
at last the Westralian Government cleared the
old English gang out, and Parliament reflected,
the views not of Downing-st., but of Westralia.
And now the worst surviving horrors are due to -
the fact that England stood in the way when.
Australia wished to bar the Asiatic absolutely
from the Commonwealth, and insisted that her
friends, the Jap, the Malay and the Indian, should
have at least some chance of entry. Take away
the Asiatic element from Westralia, and the most.
repulsive of the horrors which Dr. Roth de-
scribed would vanish. England must take •
the bulk of the disgrace of Westralia; and
that will not be a novel stain on an
immaculate reputation. In the other States of
Australia, whilst the Crown Colony’’ system sur-
vived and England was the direct ruler, the
blacks were flogged, tortured, poisoned, hanged
and shot in wholesale fashion. So soon as re-
sponsible government came and Australian ideas
began to take shape, the era of humanity dawned.
The Australian treatment of the aborigines has
not been just or humane. Our record is a black
one, and many grave sins against the unfortunate
autochthons cry out for vengeance. But it has at ,
least been immeasurably whiter, cleaner, more
merciful, than that of Eugland. We had always
some elementary notions of mercy ; have now
some genuine desire to be just; in the era of Eng-
lish rule of Australia there was neither mercy nor.
justice for the blacks.

Agricultural Science.
Victoria is doing more good things wrong-end
first. Realising that the farmer must have more
knowledge to compete with the world, it proposes
to educate him into scientific methods by estab-
lishing courses of Agricultural Science at Mel-
bourne University. The University may therefore
soon be expected to turn out its Bachelors of Agri-
cultural Science—young men with some practical
experience (the course provides for that) and tech-
nical knowledge enough to make them the finest of
farmers. But there is no move to ensure that
these men will have chances of using their know-
ledge. Here and there, a newspaper may
find room for one of these agricultural ex-
perts; one or two more may find Government
billets ; and the rest may sit still and speculate
on the glorious things they could do if thev
only had the land. But they can’t get that till
Parliament puts on a stiff land-tax, with, if
necessary, provision for the compulsory resumption
of estates. It might go further and give pre-
ferential conditions of lease to holders- of uni-
versity degrees and diplomas, and students of
agricultural colleges. No reason, in. fact, why the
best man at the examinations should, not be given
a grant or a lease of land in lieu.of a cash scholar-
ship. At present, a big of Agricultural
College men, unable to get Und s . drift into clerical
employment. Several are op the country press,
using their agricultural, Ipipwledge in reporting
shire and city council meetings, and describing
what Mr. Teatpui.ler’s girls wore at the annual
hospital hop.

Maoriland Municipal Socialism.
“Galvo’s” observations
After living, for .twenty years, in Australia, a (fierce
years sojourn In Maoriland has impressed on me for-
cibly the wonderful difference in the public spirit of tfw
latter people. Perhaps it is that Maoriland was first fn
so-called “ Socialistic” legislation, and so has had time to
get over the senseless scares which the.capitalistic press
always rakes up whenever an innovation tending towards
progress is proposed. Monopolies in M.L., such as tram-
ways, gas, water supply, sewerage and railways, are now
viewed as essentially the property of the people themselves.
Certainly there are still many, public enterprises privately
owned, such as the Auckland Gas-works, .the Auckland
tramways, the Wellington Electric Light, etc., but
these are mostly survivals of past indiscreet legislation, and
are bitterly regretted. Absolutely the most public-spirited
town is Dunedin, where the Scotchmen live, and which they
promise to transform into a second Glasgow. There, not -
one private monopoly,opens its mouth. The pity owns the
gas works, and turns out very good gas ; the finest tram-
way system in Australasia bar, none, the abattoirs, the
water supply, the public reserves, and the sewerage system
(now under construction) ; and controls harbor manage-
ment. In addition a big water power scheme for the supply
of current for light and power is now in hand, and when
completed will enable Dunedin to supply its manu-
facturers with power at a price far lower than any other
place in Australasia. It should lead to the establishment
of great works. It wall particularly benefit the woollen
industry, which is just now having a rather rough time,
thanks to the competition of imported shoddy, upon which
a much higher tariff is required. Striking, too, is the
difference between the treatment of employees b_v public -■
bodies and private companies. Arbitration has tended to 1
level matters up, and compel juster treatment from the
latter, but it is still possible to skin an arbitration award
and give concessions in such a way as to practically rob
them of their value. By public bodies, however, the arbi-
tration awards are usually given a liberal interpretation
and the men profit thereby. In Dunedin, Corporation
employees get particularly fair treatment, and the tram-
way men work under perhaps the most liberal award ever
given. As it is impossible to employ a tramway man for
regular hours, owing to the exigencies of traffic, it was
decided that their work should-be divided into fortnightly


periods of 104 hours each. Any regular man is paid for
104 hours even if he only works 50, whilst, if he work 1-0
he gets time and a half for the 16 hours overtime. He
gets also two days holiday per quarter on full pay,*
day per fortnight without pay. In addition, the men
are provided with warm uniforms and overcoats at the
citizens’ expense, whilst the ends of cars are glassed m as
a protection against the weather. In spite of this leckless
regard for the men’s comfort and health, and the fact that
fares are absurdly low, the trams are able to show' a profit.
In the hands of a private co. this profit would un-
doubtedly be much larger, but. it would be at the expense
of the convenience of the public as a whole, and the wel-
fare of the employees in particular. When I lived in
Australia and read the shrieks of alarm which
proceeded from such papers as the Tbpegraph,
iand Sydney’s Morning Granny, I thought there
really must be some foundation for the statements
that public ownership of monopolies was mischievous and
retrogressive, since there could surely not be so much
smoke without some fire. But when I get these same
papers a week old, and read the same old articles in the
light of experience, and with the results of Socialistic
legislation before my eyes in every direction, it makes me
fairly weep for the ignorance and selfishness which must
have inspired them.

Fish—and Bricks.
Melbourne Argus, the other day, became excited
over a matter of fish, common fish, barracouta at
that. It seems that the Victorian Co-operative
Fishermen’s Association had, during the past 11
months, destroyed 592 baskets of fish because they
did not realise the reserve of 3s. a basket fixed by
the Association. The Association is only a com-
paratively small concern, which competes with the
agents who sell on commission, and the fishermen
themselves have fixed the reserve price—i:t is at
absolutely the lowest figure that gives them any
return. They were acting in strict accordance with
the Argus axiom that a man has the right to do as
he likes with his own. But these persons were
common fishermen, and that made a difference.
After giving a full column to explain in detail
the iniquity of their acts, tl.e Argus argues:—
Surely it would be better, in the interests of ths
fishermen themselves, that fish should be supplied at ae
low a rate and as abundantly as possible, in order to
create a demand for it. Yet we ar,e told the demand fell
off. What could be the reason, except that it was too
dear for the mass of the people to buy ?,, , What would
be said if the farmers made a ring to keep the price of
potatoes up to £6 a ton [3s. a basket for fish gives a return
equal to about 10s. a ton for spuds, but that is a trifle the
Argus overlooked], and destroyed all they could not sell
at that rate ?
Now read that over again, and put “bricks” in
the place of “ fish ” :
What would be said if brickmakers combined to keep
up the price of bricks to £2 per thousand, and bought up
other brickworks in order to keep them idle, so as .to keep
up the price to that rate ?
Well, the Argus has so fair said nothingin fact,
it seems to enthusiastically approve of the pro-
ceeding. Again, the Argus :
Some of the old laws against combinations made in
restraint of trade were founded on a proper appreciation
of what public interest demands. Is the fish (brick)
supply to be regulated and restricted in the interests of a
few men working the fishing (brickmaking) grounds?
Surely not. . . . Experience teaches that it is hest in the
long run to give trade free play. If the demand for fish
(bricks) were enlarged by the lowering of prices to the
ordinary level the fishermen (brick-makers) might be the
first to benefit.
Truth to tell, the fish trouble is not with the
fishermen or their agents, the association. Three
shillings a basket gives the fisherman an average
weekly return of barely 305.; and there is, in the
case of barracouta, the cheapest fish, an average
of 12 good-sized fish in each basket —3d. apiece
wholesale. Yet not even on the hawkers’ barrows
on Prince’s Bridge can you buy one barracouta
for less than Gd. In the suburbs it will cost you
:9d. or Is., and out at Doncaster, Burwood or
Fern-tree Gully you will have to pay from Is. 3d.
to Is. 6d. for the ’couta that costs the distributor
:3d. That is where the robbery lies. That is
where “the supply is restricted in the interests of
a few.” It is again the old story of the woollen
manufacturer and the tailor, the hatmaker and
tthe hatter. The producer sells reasonably enough,
shut the consumer has to pay the distributor an
•extortionate price for carting the goods across the
road. That the retail price of fish is too high is
admitted, but what is chief!)’ needed to correct
that is not the acceptance by the fishermen of Is.
or 2s. a basket, which would make little difference
to the consumer, though it would kill the pro-
ducer. but a guaranteed regularity of both supply
and demand, with a comprehensive and properly
organised system of distribution. Nothing but a
co-operative distributing agency can do that. The
Argus’ idea of a remedy for dear fish is merely to
sweat the fishermen.

Prison Reform.
Ip Mr. Bent or anybody else is in earnest about
prison reform, and if he himself is neither a
professor of the science of crank-penology, nor
the holder of a billet in the penal department, the
ways and means are plain and easy. Reform in
the way of finance, and reform in the way of
common justice, moreover, will go conveniently
together. Eight per cent, of the prisoners
who pass through the chief gaols are held
in default of fines. Folly and injustice, begin
together with those prisoners. It costs just as
much to hold and keep a man who does seven days
because he can’t raise 20s. as to hold and keep a
burglar who is as dangerous as a loose tiger. And
the one is permitted to earn just about as
much as the other. In Victoria it averages
£4 Bs. 2d. per annum, and the cost is £43 11s. 4d.
Now, if natural justice and common-sense
were furnished with waddies, and permitted
to knock crank-penology on the head, they
might from that good deed go on to such good
works as these. First of all, justice, tackling the
courts first, would enact that every man and every
woman fined (vagrants and utter impossibles
excepted) should have time to pay the fine. Seven
days or twenty shillings, with a month to pay as
a matter of course, and if neither fine nor party
turned up at the month’s end, six months certain.
A good deal of cross-eyed injustice might be
quashed in that way ; a few thousands annually
in gaol costs saved, a few hundreds in fines, now
lost, recovered. That done, the same twin
brethren might surely assert together that, if an
offence can be atoned by a fine, all we are
entitled to inflict on the person guilty of
that offence, is compulsion, if he cannot pay, to
work it out. This done, three-fourths of the gaol
accommodation might be transformed into old age
barracks. A barbed-wire fenced paddock would
hold all the prisoners engaged in working out
fines, and ten warders handle four hundred of
them easily enough. But then what a lot of gold-
starred red-braided warders would find themselves
without billets? Also, what should we do with
professors of the science of crank-penology ?

Land Monopoly in Maoriland.
The Maoriland reactionaries, who are bent on
undoing the best there is in the State s land-
policy, know the value of plausible phrases in
fooling the multitude. Though they demand the
freehold, they say they favor a “limited free-
diold” as a “safeguard against the aggregation of
large estates.” But the limitation of area offers
no security whatever against that evil. A small
farm would be a bloated estate in urban or
suburban property, in connection with both of
which the curse of landlordry is apparent enough.
And as to country land, consider. what is
taking place in M.L. The district of
Taranaki is pre-eminently a community of
small freehold farms, for most of the land there
was sold at auction years before the M'Kexzie
land policy came into operation. Yet there is no
part of Maoriland where the evils of land-
monopoly are more in evidence, and black and
bitter evils they are. Taranaki, in fact, is
rapidly becoming a district of landlords and
tenants, and soon but few dairy-farmers will own
the land they work, and it looks as though many
of them will even have to lease their cows. It is
already a common thing for the freehold pro-
prietor of, say, 200 acres, which is a large area for
a dairy farm, to rent it to a tenant for 20s. or 255.
per acre. That means that a tenant of a 200-
acre farm will have to find £2OO or £250
for his landlord before he has a shilling
for himself; and the most pitiable feature of the
whole business is that his children have to suffer.
In jnuch the same plight as the wretched tenant
farmers are those nominal freeholders who have
bought land at spurious prices with the aid of
money-lenders, numbers of whom infest Taranaki,
and wax rich by lending on usurious conditions.
Many men have bought land at £3O per acre on
the instalment system, and words are inadequate
to describe the wretched lot of |th© children of
such men. Not a few of th,e landlords are no.w
able to live comfortably on their rents in th,e
larger centres ; and in opposing land reform and
labor legislation, these men are as unrelenting and
merciless as the rnost purse-proud squatter. When
one sees the evils which now scourge Taranaki —
the usury, the child-slavery, the neglected educa-
tion, the arrogance and greed—it is possible to
picture what the country would come to if the
reactionaries had their way —those who are wire-
pulling in favor of •“ the limited freehold.”

Concerning Things that Wear Out.
A few weeks ago the South-Eastern and Chatham
Railway Co., which pushes itself out froiq the fogs
of London, sacked a number of its por.ters and told
others that in future they would get no wages, but
would be required ito make a lamentable living out
of travellers’ tips. This is in .consequence of the
march of the motor, and the cutting-out of the
railway. Railway men in England acknowledge
that they must write down the value of their plant,
which means in many cases also their .capital. And
Australia, which will ha y ? i*° f ac . e ,the S*W® •<?**
eunistances, has been living in the foolish
belief that nothing could affect the value of
•the plant, &c„ into which it has put its
loans.; wherefore it hasn’t seriously undertaken a
sinking fund, and hasn’t made its railways pay
anything for wear and tear; often, in fact, when
one engine built with borrowed money lias been
worn out, the State has bought another with bor-
rowed money, so that the one engine has to earn
interest for two. Now the time is probably
coming when even the one engine ought to be
superseded • but if it is superseded, the new con-
trivance, under present conditions, will have .to
struggle through carrying three loads of interest.
Every private concern knows that its plant will
wear out or become obsolete, and that the co. will
have either to get new plant or be forced out of
business by more up-to-date competitors ; where-
fore it wipes something off the capital cost every
year—in other words, it compels its business,to pay
something for wear and tear just as regularly as it
compels it to pay wages. Tal;e the case qf ,the
Silverton Tramway Co., the biggest private rail-
way concern in Australia. Run on business lines,
that co. last year not only paid £25,235 out of
revenue for renewals, but also wrote off £5OOO for
general depreciation. If that concern were run
by the State a large part of the £25,235 would
have been charged to capital account (that is, paid
out of loans instead of out of revenue), while the
other item, amounting ,to p.c. on the capital,
would not have appeared at all. If only 1£ per
cent, (half the Silverton Co.’s .charge) were pro-
vided, the N.S.W. railways would have to show
an extra £528, GOO a year, or taxation would have
to make up the amount; and that seems to
be the very least amount which should be set
aside as a sinking fund on the railway debt. The
Electric Light Department of the Melbourne
City Council, which is mostly run by business
men on business lines, started with a 2,1 per cent,
sinking fund ; but experience showed that that
was not enough, and now it provides 3 per
cent. The State cannot buy imperishable ma-
chinery any more than the City Council; and yet
it sets aside absolutely nothing, . To say that the
State cannot afford to have a sinking fund is to say
that the State cannot afford to be honest. It will
not allow that excuse in others; how can it
alloAV it in itself ? And, anyway, the matter
will have to be faced some time; and
the further we drift without facing it,
the bigger the trouble will be. . If we can’t pay
anything off the debt on one engine, it isn’t going
to "be easier to pay off the debt on three, when two
of them are on the scrap-heap. It will take the
remaining engine all its time to pay the interest;
and, to help "things along a bit, the management
(if it is that sort of management) will probably tell
the porters (as the English co. has done in similar
circumstances) that for the future they must live
on tips. To that issue, at any rate, the present
drift tends.

This is the comfort which one Indian Baboo paper
takes from the Jap victories as likely to curb the
insolence of Australia :
Australia is a new country, and has recently been
granted the power by England to rule itself, and to have
its own Parliament. ‘ All new lands are generally peopled
by the dregs of all societies, and Australia is not an
exception. . . . The first act of their newly-formed Parlia-
ment was to pass laws to keep the Asiatic nations out of
their country. Relying fully on the strength of England
to protect them from unpleasant complications with
other countries, they have most arrogantly declared
before the world that they shall not have anything to do
with the black, and even the mail steamers having Indian
lascar crew are not to be patronised by them. India is a
subject country, and will calmly submit to all sorts of
insults that may be heaped against her, hut this nation qf
mean and short-sighted people has not seen that there is
alreadv a nation in Asia which has the power to fully
retaliate the insults it has been put to. Japan is surely
strong’ enough already to teach this foolish, vain, and
infant nation the usual courtesies which should exist
between nations, and in time it is expected that China
will also be strong enough to bring Australia to reason.

The Crow-eaters invented the stripper. That is
a matter beyond all doubt. Half a dozen Austra-
lians had a hand in developing the stripper har-
vester, all of whom seem to have been singularly
neglectful of patent rights. Eor Americans
pirated the whole lot, Yankee-doodlised them,
and now send back the stripper harvester made
in their shops, and land it invoiced at £26. Cus-
toms here put that £26 up to £3B, on which the
importers have to pay 12 \ per cent., which amounts
to £4 15s. And that, added on to the invoice
price of £26, makes £3O 15s. Australians say they
can’t make and sell a machine under £Bl or £B5.
And “ the imported machines are sold to the farmer
at about the same price.” Thus Mr. James Moobe,
manager of T. Robinson and Co., before the Tariff
Commission. There ought to be enough in that to
set even a farmer thinking. Every Freetrader
is preaching to him of the benefit he
derives from every step towards the goal of
Freetrade. Yet here, on the sworn evidence
of a manufacturer and distributor, is proof
that the farmer gets no advantage at all. The
stripper harvester can be stored, duty paid, in
any Australian port for £34 155., but, despite
that, the farmer pays eighty odd pounds for it
delivered at his shed.
