Thk anti-Socialistic cranks are yelling in the
Argus and elsewhere that the increase of deposits
in Australian banks and the shrinkage of advances
is further proof that “ Socialistic legislation ” is
killing enterprise. One half the explanation is
that enterprise isn’t dead at all, but that people
are extending operations out of the profits of two
extra-good seasons (which is a mighty lot better
than extending them with borrowed money) ; the
other half of the explanation is that where, in
manufacture, enterprise is slack, it is because the
low-tariff enemies of Australia are allowing the
foreign factory to strangle the Australian industry
even before it is born. There is a fat lot of in-
ducement for a man to borrow money to put into,
say, a woollen mill when two-thirds of the present
mills can’t pay even 3 per cent, dividends because
the Reid party encourages the foreign miller to
flood the land with cheap shoddy.
*
Australian shipping suffers from some compli-
cations. Generally speaking, coastal freights are
yery high ; butter, it is alleged, can be shipped to
London for less than it cpsts to bring it from the
North Coast to Sydney. These high rates affect
many Australian industries—Queensland sugar-
growing, for instance. The yarn that the white
man can’t work in the North means that
they won’t pay white men’s wages; and one
reason ydiy white men’s wages look stiff is that
freight to the Australian markets takes out so
big a bite. And while Australia is being
prejudiced by high coastal profits, it is
also, in one way, prejudiced by very low ocean
freights. Thus it has been drained of practically
the whole of the once highly profitable tin-
smelting business ; a large proportion of our lead
goes away in crude concentrates ; it doesn’t even
matter much whether we wash the dirt out of our
wool or not. The ocean liner is subsidised, and
it carries these things for very little ; the coastal
liner isn’t subsidised. Thus the policy that would
promote Australian development is completely
up-ended. Melbourne is encouraged to get its
coffee frqm the other end of the earth rather than
from Queensland. Queensland is helped ,to get
its cheese from Canada instead of from the
N.S. W. South Coast. There is something besides
the subsidies at work, top. The ocean liner
expects (anyway, it only mak;es) 2 or 3 per cent.,
the coastal line is made to pay 8 or 10 per cent.
In other words, when we want to tracle with
Germany, or Brummagem, or other foreign parts,
we can do it on a 2 per cent, basis so far as the
middleman shipper is concerned ; when we want
,to ; trac}e with sorqe other part of the Common-
wealth, jt must l}e on alO per cent, basis. Seeing
.that itje Coppm on wealth is a large place, whose
'rigKt apd varied development depends upon
,o}ieap interchange of products, this business of
'coastal stopping will have to get a lot of attention.
For one .thing the Commonwealth will have to see
(whether high sugar freights, for instance, don’t
Stand in a White Australia’s way—and that’s only
one branch of a large subject.
A Sane Imperialist*
The first impression frpm Richard Jebb’s “Studies
in Colonial Nationalism ” is that, if he represents
Imperialism, there is .little hope for the anti-
imperialists—for those of us who, groping among
the lessons of history, can see nothing but disaster
in a policy pf expansion, conquest and “ Empire; ”
who, plodding carefully from fact to fact of present
conditions, reject as unsound the theory that one
centralised government can rule better than a
series of independent governments over a group
of democratic peoples spread oyer a vast area,
haying and jealously preserving well-marked
divergencies of character; who, cherishing
fondly our Australian characteristics, would
set up an obstacle of sentiment—were all
reasons confounded—against surrendering one
atom of our self-governing power. For Mr.
Jebb is an Imperialist with a difference. He is
utterly antipathetic to the Imperialist of Austra-
lian experience, the ignorant mouther of sophis-
tries and slanders, the pleader of a mean cause of
national abasement. He has a power of seeing
facts, of valuing them at their true worth, and of
making from them logical deductions. He has
imagination, and can understand that other people
have imagination, and that, in a national
cause, the sentimental has often ‘as much weight
as the practical. He sees clearly, argues cor-
rectly, and has the historical faculty of
getting to the motives behind the facts, even
when the motive is not the practical one of £ s. d.,
but the more elusive one of sentiment or idealism.
It is not too much to say that at first sight, before
Mr. Jebb’s case for Imperialism, it appears that
the anti-imperialist must give up his cause. That
impression, however, fades so soon as the question
occurs, “ Is Mr. Jebb an Imperialist at all in the
general acceptation of the term here ? ” —and is
answered with a firm “No.”
Mr. Jebb is Imperialist in this sense—that he
has a passionate faith in the future of the Anglo-
Saxon race. He is firmly convinced that the
greatest good is bound up with the existence of
the closest possible sympathy between the branches
of that race in Britain, Australia, Canada, and
South Africa. So far most Australians can follow
him. Nor do they find reason for taking a differ-
ent road as Mr. Jebb amplifies his principles with
details. In the very beginning the author shows
clearness and candor.
The assumption which underlies such phrases as “ the
expansion of England” or “Greater Britain,” and sug-
gests the familiar principle of Federation as the logical
form of closer union, is not justified by the tendency
either of instinctive sentiment or of actual developments
in Canada and Australia. So far as generalisation is
possible, it may be said that there is not, in fact, any
growing consciousness of a common nationality, but
exactly the reverse. In other words, the basis of Imperial
Federation instead of expanding and solidifying is melting
away.
That is a good beginning. Fair and free again is
the statement —
It is possible to respect the prejudice against “ Im-
perialism ” when the anti-imperialists put forward a self-
respecting national policy upon the basis of separation.
As a still more unmistakable confession of this
Imperialist’s faith —
Premising that the national self-assertion now charac-
teristic of Canada and Australia is neither accidental
nor temporary, but signifies the inevitable mastery of a
racial and geographical over a political situation, it is
safe to forecast the future development of a similar
tendency also in Maoriland and South Africa.
As affecting the problem of Imperial union, the
nationalist movement, led by Canada and Australia, is
defining rapidly the limitations which any such union
must respect. The popular habit of alluding to “ the
British nation ” in a sense inclusive of colonial peoples
already is an anachronism. It has became evident that
the fundamental principle of any real advance must be
the frank recognition of independent national instincts in
the four principal self-governing colonies, in varying
stages of evolution. But that recognition involves the
rejection of the Imperialist conception which hitherto has
been current in the mother country. In general it is
clear that the union, if any, must resemble an alliance of
independent nations rather than a federation of scattered
States inspired by the idea'of a common nationality.
The objects and organisation of the alliance must be such
as to satisfy nations jealous of their independence.
This clear sight and sound judgment follow the
author in almost every conclusion. He sees the
inherent selfishness and unrcliableness of the
foreign policy of the United States, and with a
courage rare among English publicists is willing
to announce it. He condemns the forces which
were used against the Commonwealth to preserve
some right of appeal to the Privy Council front
the High Court; and estimates at exactly its true
value the support which Australian daily papers
and Australian Judges gave to that movement:
The newspapers in question included several which had
treated Federation from the Commercial rather than the
National point of view: and generally had been con-
ventional in dealing with Imperial topics . .. It was
evident to those more in touch with the national move-
ment that the editors were expressing views out of
harmony with the popular and deep-seated convictions.
...Mr. Chamberlain learnt further that the Chief
Justices of the Colonies were ranged on his side. In
Australia that fact was estimated, humanly, in connection
with the prospect of handsome positions in the capital of
the Empire. , ,
On the question of naval defence, Mr. Jebb s
attitude is that of The Bulletin. Incidentally
he writes a mournful epitaph on the political
career of Mr. Edmund Barton, who, at the
Colonial Conference, committed himself to sup-
porting the present Naval Tribute :
Landing once more on Australian shores, Sir Edmund
Barton exerted all his ample gifts of eloquence on behalf
of the naval agreement, to which he had pledged his
honor and sacrificed his past.
Mr. Jebb has really an uncanny gift of clear-sight.
How admirably he appraises Mr. Reid ! In regard
to the naval tribute :
On the present occasion the leader of the Federal Oppo-
sition W’as faithful to the tradition which has earned for
him the popular title of “ Yes-No,” for he announced his
intention of supporting the Bill while adducing the most
urgent reasons for rejecting it.
Again, on the preferential trade question :
Of the men who have hitherto been at the helm of the
Commonwealth only Mr. Reid, the Freetrader and former
anti-nationalist, has spoken with an uncertain voice. At
the present time it is common to hear him quoted by
English controversialists upon both sides of the question.
In Australia, however, such confusion is not likely to
occasion surprise, since, it is often said, there never yet
was an important Australian i,ssue upon which that poli-
tician committed himself irretrievably to one side or the
other.
Mr. Jebb would have none of India, as an equal
partner in any Imperial union. Its inclusion, he
argues, would “reduce any scheme to an ab-
surdity.” His Empire would be a white Empire.
And he foresees that if there were “Imperial
Federation ” (which he does not support) the day
would come soon when England’s place in the
council of that Federation would be “superior
only to New Zealand’s.”
Enough has been quoted to show alike Mr.
Jebb’s power of clear judgment and his idea of
Empire. He would have Australia, Canada,
S’Afriea develop their national entities, accepting
the responsibilities of their own defence, linked to
their mother land by the ties of race and affection,
and by trade relations which should be now
fostered by a Preference scheme. (On this point of
Preferential Trade, Mr. Jebb’s work will be dis-
cussed again.)
It is impossible, even in this paper, to ignore
the author’s judgment of The Bulletin. Through-
out his work Mr. Jebb constantly refers, sometimes
with praise, sometimes with severe blame, to its
views. One whole chapter is devoted to a dis-
cussion of-what he styles “The National Aus-
tralian Paper,” and he quotes several of its articles
in full. His apology for this is interesting :
It is absurd to affect indifference nowadays towards a
journal which has become nothing less than an imperial
institution. For it has attained a circulation which is
Australasian and more. To the remotest limits of settle-
ment in every Australian State ragged back numbers of
The Bulletin form the literature of the shearers’ hut and
the miners’ camp. The pink cover is no less familiar in
New Zealand, and catches the traveller’s eye upon the
bookstalls of Manila and Hongkong, Singapore and
Colombo. Even further afield, it seems to find a demand
which testifies to its unique position amongst colonial
journals. . . . Whatever the explanation, students of the
Empire cannot afford to ignore a unique journalistic
influence which has expanded over the two Southern
Continents and along the margin of the Pacific.
Undoubtedly the extraordinary popularity of The
Bulletin may be attributed largely to the variety of its
contents. . . . The cartoons and illustrations hold their
own with the similar productions of any other country
for cleverness. . . . The “ Society ” page and “ A Woman’s
Letter ” describe the doings of Government House and
neighborhood with a vulgar familiarity, pandering to all the
snobbish instincts which simultaneously are denounced ih
the editorial columns as an evil result of British connection.
... A considerable space is devoted to the encouragement
of Australian literature... But, after, all, it is the poli-
tical activity of the Australian national newspaper that
gives it its unique individuality, and must account for
the greater part of its popularity... It seems para-
doxical, if not suggestive of insult added to injury, to
saddle The Bulletin itself with the credit, or the respon-
sibility, for the enthusiasm displayed by Australia in the
S’African war. And yet the truest generalisation about
that enthusiasm is to explain it as the expression of the
very national sentiment which The Bulletin had set
itself to create. .. The Bulletin does reveal the genuine
consciousness of national self-respect.
These extracts, picked at random from various
parts of the work, give some idea of the trend of
a lengthy chapter, which (unusual as it may
seem to devote so much space in an historical
treatise to a newspaper) is yet not the least useful
in giving an insight into the author’s conclusions.
To a “ Policy for the Commonwealth,” by Mr.
James Edmond, Mr. Jebb also gives notice as
“foreshadowing the actual lines followed by the
Federation.” In this respect as in others through-
out the work, the author shows an unhesitating
readiness to accept facts. The Bulletin gener-
ally displeases, sometimes enrages, him. But ho
has the historian’s gift of accepting facts and
giving them their value, however much they run
counter to his own views.
To follow Mr. Jebb’s “ Imperialism,” Australia
would need to cultivate with 3’et more pride its
national characteristics and independence; treat
with yet more scorn its K.C.M.Gr-eese class, which
is servile to foreign ideals and regardless of Aus-
tralian aspirations; it would need to declare un-
hesitatingly for its own navy, its own military
system, and its own unappealable High Court.
Synchronising with national development, Mr.
Jebb would have us, by the cultivation of closer
trade relations and by giving full value to the
blood tie, come closer in affection to the Empire.
It is a sane Imperialism.
♦“Studies in Colonial Nationalism,” by Rickard Jebb.
London : Edward Arnold.

On a second look-in at “The Cingalee,”
that subject of much contention and many lawyers’
fees seems just about what it did at first—scarcely
reaching a very high level anywhere, yet never in
any respect falling below the middle-line. The
most captivating things happen in the last half-
hour j then the Chedder Bhoy’s harem, which was
a gorgeous enough harem before, sparkles with a
sudden illumination, and a picturesque ballet
keeps it company. But, for that matter, the ballets
are all good ; and Miss Ivy Scott, the leader of the
tea-girl romps, who mostly constitute the ballet,
sings a song that was good enough to mention last
week—the “ Tea in the Morning, Tea in the
Evening” song that almost sings itself. Also, you
can relish better, at a second taste, the philosophy
of the babooo who has been successful in failing at
an English University examination, and who, as a
result of his travels, makes numerous pungent
observations upon English manners. Both Miss
Margaret Thomas and Haigh Jackson, who have
the pick of the lyrics, continue in excellent voice,
and score heavily.
*# * *
These moonlit nights Little Tich is a
humorous lamplighter ; and the “ turn ” is one of
his best. _ Most other star comedians of the’alls
twinkle in but a single song or two ; Tich seems to
have dozens, and all of them packed with carefully
polished “business.” He leaves the Tivoli and
Sydney on May 20 ; Melbourne will then have 10
more nights of the merry little fellow. After
that, the gloom of London, which needs him more.
Alice Guerra, from the French Tivs., is another
sparkling item these times. She uses her voice,
which is a pity; but her Spanish dances are
among the most seductive things seen lately. Miss
Pierce, Max Milian, and Nat Travers are other
features of a particularly strong bill.
#* * *
Some are born criminals, others achieve
criminality, but Miss Eugenie Duggan has of late
had iniquity thrust upon her. In his desire to let
Sydney know how some people other than Govern-
ment House folk and corner-grocers live, Mr.
William Anderson, on Saturday night, produced
at the Palace “The Female Swindler” with his
leading lady as the star criminal. The lady
steals diamonds with industry and perseverance,
looking the while as innocent as the flowers
that bloom in the spring, and always giving
the impression that like them she has nothing
to do with the case. A detective, pallid as
Cuyler Hastings, is loved by the fair burglar, and,
while he is working to secure her wrists for the
handcuffs, she is trying to noose his heart. In the
last act both he and she succeed in their mutual
designs, and all is no doubt love and harmony and
sitting on one (stolen) chair. But meantime,
before the playing of the Wedding March, so
to speak, this cement-complexioned detective
does many crazy and blood-curdling things,
such as breaking into a house through the
skylight. Skeleton keys and cold chisels are
prosaic and used by detectives of no dramatic
instinct. He goes unarmed into the presence of
rogues, who carry a small-arms factory about their
Kersons. He tackles the Bill Sykes breed bare-
anded, and always he is fearless and uncon-
querable as an earthquake. Anyone who likes
improbabilities and really strenuous thieving, and
beautiful scenery, will find all three in the new
gay. Mr. Willard, as the detective, and Mr.
unbar, as the male accomplice of th# female
rogue, do their work very neatly.
#* * #
Mr. Julius Knight’s illness has developed
into typhoid fever, and this Theatre Royal season
will probably not see him again. His absence
was unfortunate for the revival of “ The Eternal
City ’’—emphatically the weakest piece of the co.’s
repertory—for his acting was always magnetic to
at least the feminine half of an audience, even in
such a rdle as that of David Rossi. It would not
be fair to judge Mr. Harry Plimmer’s capa-
city as an actor by his rendition of so poor
and so utterly unnatural a part, but it may
be said for him that, though lacking the
romantic air of Mr. Knight, he does very well-
indeed. “The Eternal City” is just a crude,
improbable melodrama, which struggles over a
great space like a crowd of annoyed ants. Instead
of a comic pieman as “relief” to its succession of
dismal improbabilities it has a Pope. This Pope’s
standard of intellectual merit is pretty low, for
when the police spy suggests that he should
denounce his son as a murderer in order to do him
a good turn, instead of throwing a pie at the
villain, to the loud applause of the gallery, he
approves the notion. Splendid setting and a
strong cast, headed by Misss Jeffries, and sub-
stantially the same as that which presented the
piece first in Sydney, give some attractions to
“The Eternal City.” But it is good news that,,
by Saturday, it will be replaced with “ The Silver
King ” —the best of all melodramas.
Some very Superior Person has said ‘ The
comic is that which is out of time and place with-
out danger.” According to this standard, “ The
Rose of the Riviera,” revived at the Criterion on
Saturday night, is very comic. In fact, it is
immensely amusing even without any authority
backing it as such. The tradesman Brisket mas-
querading as a duke, the variety dancer per-
sonating a duchess, are as out of place as
mules in a boudoir, but they are consider-
ably more humorous than the real personages m
palaces. Miss May Beatty, as the Rose lapsed
into trousers, cuts a pretty figure ; and when she
sings the “Egypt” song, with spectacular back-
ground, the audience tells itself that it is glad it
came. To keep track of the story is like pursuing
a rabbit through a forest of saplings. But the
rush and whirl of skirts and dancers, and the lift-
ing of voices in song are all right. Mr. Edward
Lauri and Miss Roland Watts Phillips _ are
amongst the most valuable of the farcical mirth-
makers.

The fact that there are only three nights
more on which to gaze upon Aerie standing
upright on a flying trapeze ought to cause a mild
rush to Fitzgeralds’ circus. In a very little while
the place where the Weatherleys extract melody
from a hatpin and buttonhole will be left to the
vagaries of the push that usually holds up the
desolate railway station while it chews its cigar-
ette. It is a pleasing show ; and when its lights
have been extinguished Sydney will miss its white
horses and pink women.
* * * *
Mr. Watkin Mills’ English concert party
opens in Sydney early in June. At present it is
making music in W.A. The two ladies, Miss
Edith Kirkwood, soprano of the Royal Italian
Opera Co., London, and Miss Gertrude Lonsdale,
contralto, are said by fellow-passengers arriving
here ahead of them, to have voices that we shall
be very glad to hear. Mr. Harold Wilde, a tenor,
of whom it is written, “ he is a London favorite,”
aids the co. Mr. Mills needs no introduction, nor
does his master of the ivories, M. Edouard Parlo-
vitz. First half of the Mills’ concert programmes
will consist of the regular concert numbers, and
the second will take the form of song cycles—
“ The Persian Garden,” for instance, with its
musings on wine, love, and death.

College girls and suburban ladies who
don’t go out at night will presently become
indebted to Mr. George Boyle, the pianist, for
a series of matinees at the Y.M.C.A. hall,
lasting for about an hour and ten minutes. Vocal
music and the music of the violin and piano will
be included.
* * * *
Theatrical managers, at least some of
them, continue to argue that the abolition of the
queue means a return to chaos. Now, it has
never been necessary in Sydney to form a queue
at the doors of the dress circle or reserved stalls.
Not that these places are never crowded, but
simply because seats may be engaged there in
advance, and because there are not two prices of
admission to them. Even when a play is drawing
big houses, only a few gather round the doors to
the cheaper seats prior to seven o’clock, and even
up to 7.15 there are not enough gathered to
obstruct the thoroughfare. It is surely obvious
that if all the doors were thrown open at seven
there never would be any crowds, and therefore
no need for the queue. Indeed, the Sydney
Criterion has proved this by abolishing all early
door fees, and by throwing open all its doors at
seven.
* * * *
With the conclusion of the present season
at Sydney Criterion, a musical comedy co., under
the pilotage of Mr. JohnF. Sheridan, will take the
boards. The co. has some fine pieces, opening with
“ King Dodo” and going from that to “The Earl
and the Girl ” ; and it will be strengthened for
the Sydney season by the inclusion of Miss Maud
Amber and Mr. Wilfred Blake—well known to the
public here.
* * * *
A brief revival of “Sunday” repeats, at,the
Princess’s, the success which Miss Tittell Brune
scored at Her Majesty’s nine months ago, when
the cleverness of her free and hearty acting came
as a surprise. Probably the Wild Western gal
who bursts into notice with a swear-word on her
lips is a little more convincing these nights than
she was last year, but she was always an admir-
able Sunday in her various moods and manners.
Seeing her again in her first Australian part, one
is again impressed, by the fact that Miss Brune
has to work hard for her effects. A born actress of
the restless sort rather than an artist in repose,
neither her personality nor her somewhat sombre
voice is a first-class asset, yet she makes herself
always interesting and forcible in a part where
many another actress of greater experience could
be more picturesque and—less real. There are
changes from the previous cast of “ Sunday.”
Greenaway is the low-spirited young man who
murders Gaston Mervale in the first act and hides
the corpse in a lonely spot, for the good of all
parties concerned. Cuyler Hastings was great
in the murder episode when he played this
character. Greenaway hasn’t the impressive
voice and deadly stillness of the other man,
but he is a satisfactory manslaughterer, also
he has a younger bearing, and the contrast
between Jacky and his hairy older mates is better
marked than in the previous production. Jeffrey
Williams is a new Towser, and Maurice Dudley’s
imitation of Edmund Gwenn in the senile
character of Lively is more than a good imitation.
In its quieter way it is even an improvement on
the original.
** * *
The question as to who was Dan Maguire
when the light went out will be finally disposed of
at Her Majesty’s on Friday night. “The Way
to Kenmare,” although a sufficiently popular Irish
drama for Andrew Mack to talk, and sing, and
move and have his being in, is not calculated to
crowd a moighty large theatre for longer than
twelve nights and two matinees, perhaps. Any-
how, Friday will be the last occasion when
Maguire aforesaid will discover the proofs of
his haughty lineage by the light of a thunder-
storm. Touching Dan Maguire’s relation-
ship to the reigning Earl of Kenmare,
it is only fair to say that he doesn’t
wish to be a future earl for his own sake, but for
the sake of his late mother. Aspersions have been
cast on the memory of his mother, and Dan under-
takes to prove her respectability, even though the
effort earns him a title and estates. Otherwise he
would have scorned his birthright and continued
to wear a leather belt and a democratic billycock
hat. On Saturday a revival of Boucicault’s
“ Arrah-Na-Pogue ” will present Andrew Mack
and co. as Irishmen who are mostly agin the Gov-
ernment. A good many years have passed over
Erin’s head since Melbourne got a handsome, full-
flavored production of “ Arrah.”
** * *
Bland. Holt, still harping on military
matters, intends to revive “One of the Best” on
Saturday. This is the well-knit drama that
reflects a lot of unjust suspicion upon Walter
Baker in the second act, but restores him to
public confidence at the finish. Baker’s regiment
plays the name part with much pomp and
circumstance, and vigorous exeroise of wind
instruments.

At the Opera House the Magic Kettle is
spouting vapor for the last times. The Kettle is
an unsatisfactory utensil which occasionally goes
on strike, owing to its lack of “liquid air”
materials, or because it has exploded the operator
or something. At time of writing the Kettle was
expected to be on the boil for the remainder of its
fleeting season. The musical dancing Figaros,
likewise Taft, Leo Dryden, Winton and others
will support the Kettle to its bitter end, and on
Saturday they will support a less laboratorious
attraction. At the Bijou a low-priced edition of
“ A Message from Mars ” is about to be issued by
Walter Sandford.

Adelaide Royal, promised by Manager
Pollock the best of everything in Australia during
the coming winter, is taking a heavy dose of melo-
drama meanwhile. The Anderson Co. has gone
out and the Billie Howarde Co. has come in, but
the hero and heroine still struggle with unmerited
misfortune, and the casual corpse still falls to the
boards with a dull thud at uncertain intervals. A
big chance was lost by not having some lighter
show in Adelaide for Oakbank and Cup time.
The Wild Cat Column.
Though the proposed iron bonus would do
something for Australia, more is wanted. Australia
produces pig-lead now ; but still it imports nearly every
leaden article it uses—it imports even much of its sheets
and pipes. It is almost certain the same thing will
happen with iron and steel. Without substantial Cus-
toms duties on iron machinery, ironware, galvanised
iron, etc., Australia will simply export its steel billets—
and, after all, it won’t be worth much to Australia to
merely make and export billets, which are only one
remove or so from the raw product, and let the foreigner
turn them into foreign goods for us. Dodge and wriggle
as we may, Protective duties are unavoidable. We can’t
firmly secure the trade any other way.
* * * *
Two thousand Victorian State tenants are
£120,000 in arrears ; and though a good many of them
come in and pay up the moment it is plain the landlord
means business, a big and pitiful percentage simply can’t
doit. Forty-seven Birchip farmers who faced the music
declared that most of the selectors are heavily in debt.
They paid 10 per cent, on p.n.’s, and 7 to 10 per cent, on
mortgages, and didn’t go to the Credit Foncier either
because the institution wouldn’t have them, or because
their private Cohens forbade that course, or by reason of
the impossibility of getting through the Credit Foncier’s
red-tape entanglements. Consequently those who hold
on are working in hobbles, and those who have
succumbed to 10 per cent, compound interest are
humping bluey. The look-out is bad, although a fair
sprinkling of real rogue-farmers are rounded up. It
might make things a bit better if the name of the Credit
Foncier were abruptly changed. Plenty of selectors
don’t know what a Credit Foncier is; but they would
have some idea of what an Agricultural Bank, or a
Farmers’ Bank, is when they heard of it. As things are,
the most helpless of them drift into the jaws of the
private shark. But even a State Land Bank wouldn’t
save selectors in the far-out wilderness ; nothing, in fact,
can take the place of regular rainfall and handy markets ;
and it will take a land tax, not a land bank, to give them
those two things.

A striking- feature is the amount of deposits not carrying
interest. Against this money a large reserve has to be
maintained ; .yet counting in public securities, remit-
tances in transit, and all other more or less liquid assets,
they total less than the deposits not bearing interest; in
other words, in addition to using for advances the whole
of the fixed deposits, and the capital, and reserves, the
bank uses also a considerable amount of money for which
it doesn’t have to pay anything. It’s a lucky bank while
things last like that.
• • « *
Vic. Parliament for next session is promised a
bill prohibiting solicitors from acting also as trustees.
Which somehow recalls the comment of a bookmaker on
Justice Pring’s anti-sporting and gambling remarks when
he sentenced Slattery—" The turf be hanged ! It wasn’t
the turf —it was his lurid legal training that did it! ”
* * * *
The Queensland banks decline to fall in with
the others and reduce their deposit rates. There isn’t the
utmost cordiality between one or so of the northern
banks and the southern concerns. The former, restricted
to Queensland, with its scattered population and high
expenses, have desired more than once to arrange some-
thing in the nature of a minimum lending rate for remote
parts ; but the southern banks haven’t rushed the pro-
posal. Hence the absence of any warm affection. And,
anyway, money isn’t even now at all plentiful in Queens-
land, which suffers very severely from the absentee
trouble. Even when a man “ makes a punch ”at mining,
he shifts on to Sydney and draws his divs. from there.
The marvellous thing, in these circumstances, is that any
big Queensland section should want to intensify the
trouble with the help of nigger or yellow labor—for, of
course, that would mean more absenteeism. Queens-
land’s hope lies in encouraging a big population of small
white settlers who will not only earn but also spend their
money in the State.
• * * •
Bank of N.S.W. will shortly ask Parliament
for the right to issue notes for another 21 years. This
provides an opportunity to insist on some changes in the
original charter. Bank-notes returned to the office in a
frayed and filthy condition should not be re-issued for
another tour of Australia’s trouser-pockets. In that
respect the Wales is a long way from the worst; but a
start must be made somewhere.

Unlike the other American offices doing business in Aus-
tralia, the New York Life does not show in its balance-
sheet the variations in the market value of its securities.
For some years past there had been a shrinkage, following
upon the Pierpont Morgan inflation ; but things are
moving the other way again ; and whereas market values
at the end of 1903 were reckoned at about half-a-million
sterling ahead of book (or cost) values, the market value
of securities at the end of 1904 was reckoned at £1,489,238
in excess of book value—a gain of nearly a million ster-
ling during the year. In other words, the co. has what
is practically a reserve of £1,489,238 against depreciation
of assets. These are big figures—more than twice as
great as those of the aggregate fluctuation reserve of all
the Australasian offices combined. But, then, in this
matter of reserves (apart from assurance funds) the Aus-
tralasian offices have been singularly lax ; it is the one
fault, in fact, which can be found with practically the
whole of them. And in addition to this informal reserve,
the American office under notice has a contingency re-
serve of £1,738,761. The two make an aggregate of
£3,227,999. It isn’t pleasant to reflect that in spite of the
magnificent results some of them have achieved, the total
investment fluctuation and other reserves (apart from
assurance funds) of all Australasian offices was, on last
year’s figures, £602,824. Certainly Australasian offices
may also have hidden reserves in the shape of excess
market value of assets over the value at which they stand
in the books ; but there isn’t much room to hope that it
is so. It is quite as likely that the securities of some at
least of the offices are over-valued. But that isn’t so
much the fault of the offices as of the fool-legislators of
Australia, who won’t adopt that thorough-going Pro-
tection which, in building up American prosperity, has
also naturally added steadily to the value of American
securities. And that these additions are real enough is
proved by the fact that last year the New York Life made
a profit of £102,679 on securities sold ; and even in 1903,
in spite of the market slump in that year, the net amount
realised over cost price was £56,397.
The expense rate in proportion to total income
is still high—far ahead of the Australian average. The
A.M.P. shows a rate of £8 16s. 7d. per cent, on total
income ; the Citizens’ (ordinary) £l3 ss. lid., and under-
takes to make it £lO on premium income next year;
the National Mutual, £lB Is. 5d.; the M.L. State,
£l3 19s. 4d. per cent.; and here are the New York Life’s
averages:—

The other American offices show up in about the same
way. On premium income alone the N.Y. Life had a rate
of £23 12s. Bd. per cent., while, notwithstanding the
appalling rate shown by some of the smaller Australian
offices, the average rate for all ordinary business by Aus-
tralasian offices is £l9 12s. per cent. In this comparison
even the average Australian office thus shows up ahead
of the American. On the other hand, the Australian
office, with its cheaper methods, could never hope to show-
such glittering turnover figures note the 10 years
increase in income from £7,796,763 to £19,909,847. The
American doesn’t aim at cheapness. It isn’t part of his
country’s policy—in the first place, at any rate. If cheap-
ness comes in the end, it is through bigness.

Interest earnings are pretty good. Assets in

£4 3s. Bd. p.c. This is nearly ss. p.c. below the average
Australasian rate. But the American office hardly touches
that branch of lending business—on policies—which the
Australian office finds particularly profitable. Even the
mortgage business is neglected. This comparison seems
interesting and significant—it shows the disposition of

* * * *
Maoriland insurance co.’s continue to chop off
their nose to spite the State Insurance Department.
Recently the farmers complained that they were being
over-charged by the co.’s. The Farmers’ Union proposed
a scheme of mutual fire insurance associations, and the
co.’s tried to bludgeon the new- idea, at its inception, by
refusing to re-insure in the case of an Otago and Canter-
bury association. The State Department immediately
came to the rescue, and offered to take the excess lines—
a fact which so pleased the farmers that, should the
mutual associations scheme not mature, all future busi-
ness will probably be placed with the State concern.

The formal reserve fund, which stood at £ll,OOO, was

These assets consist principally of amounts owing by

£122,254 ; 12 months ago the total was £138,042, and
most of the difference seems to be the result of the
revaluation. At the same time, deposits held by the
societj 7 have shrunk from £68,918 to £57,903 ; the office
held £ll,OOO of certain N.S.W. trust moneys which, under

apparently reckoned at something very near true value—
and that’s also something for a Queensland co. to feel
good about. Next year, in view of the writing-down
which has lately taken place, the return ought to show up
still better.

In addition to the amount shown as written off, £13,843
was spent on prospecting in the first period (7} months),
and was charged to profit and loss, £8717 in the next half
year, and £13,030 in the term lately closed. These
amounts might perhaps be justifiably added to profits
and then written off ; but on the whole, as prospecting is
just as necessary to mining as ore-breaking is, matters are
best left as they are shown above. There is, however,
another reserve of a special sort. When Mt. Lyell 00.
was reformed in August, 1903, in order to effect amalga-
mation between Lyell and North Lyell, £140,000 of
debentures were issued in place of North Lyell’s little
load. Under the trust deed, repurchase of these bonds
has been steadily proceeding (North Lyell had failed to
take up £20,000 worth in its last term, and this obligation
was passed on to the new co.). With the profits made
from repurchase at a discount, a speoial reserve (Deben-
ture Redemption Fund) is being built up. Matters in
this respect have moved as follows :
Debentures Retired Special
Outstanding. During Term. Reserve.
Aug., 1903 .. £140,000
Mar., 1904 .. 120,000 £20,000 £lOOO
Sept., 1904 .. 97,750 22,250 1556
Mar., 1905 .. 89,760 7,990 1899
Shareholders’ capital remains at £1,200,000. Hitherto
there has been no formal reserve fund, and practically the
whole of the surplus has been used in the business, except
as to £23,790 of Vic. 3 per cents, (face value £26,030). It is
now proposed to create a definite reserve fund of £25,000,
apparently using this investment as a basis. In an
erratic business like copper-mining, in connection with
which new plant is likely to be called for just when cur-
rent profits have dwindled, it it hardly necessary to say
that the decision is one of the best. And the decision
ought to be kept in good working order so long as the
co.’s present run of prosperity continues. Copper has
averaged £66 during past half-year against £57 in present
co.’s first term; so that on the half-year’s 3950 tons of
metal, the market has put £35,550 into the co.’s purse.
As even £57 wasn’t a low price, co. hasn’t done anything
very heroic in the matter of a nest-egg by having £25,000,
seeing that nearly all of it was virtually put aside before.

This meant a total supply in April, 1903, of 1,613,198 tons
of Mt. Lyell ore averaging '66 per cent, copper, l'9oz.
silver and 0'0490z. gold, available for treatment. This ore
is only valuable as flux and fuel. Co., therefore, has to
rely for divs. on North Lyell ore. Latter has been beaten
out at the rate of 1000 tons per week ; or, since the esti-
mate, over 100,000 tons have gone, out of a total of 170,000
tons. Thus there is about nine months’ supply in sight
above 500 ft. of 6 per cent. ore. Working this in, during the
past six months Sticht’s average panned out at 2T7 per
cent, copper as against 2'41 per cent, for the pre-
ceding six months. With copper £9 per ton higher
than during the September term, Sticht could afford to
take low-grade stuff all round, and so he may have left
some of the better grade Mt. Lyell pyrite ore. But the
weakness of the situation is emphatically the poorness
of development at the 600 ft. If the ore there
totals 30,000 tons the co. will be lucky. It is
a pity that there is such a contraction, for the
work done by Sticht is that of a top-notcher. His
costs show a drop from 14s. B'2sd. to 13s. o'39d. per ton ; but
this saving is lost through the decreased value of the Mt.
Lyell ore treated. Oo.’s superphosphate works have run
away with over £30,000, and a little interest is felt as to
whether there is any arsenic in the ore to worry those in
charge. Surplus liquid assets amount to £305,979 against
£223,780 twelve months back ; but co. has still £89,000
worth of North Lyell debentures to redeem, so it needs to
have a bit up its sleeve.

It has been a broken half-year, and the figures are not
much of a guide as to the new mill’s capacity; so that
a supplementary report, which will probably be available
at the half-yearly meeting, will be of more value to in-
vestors. Go. had an overdraft of £26,550 on Sept. 30th ;
it was back to £14,880 at the end of March, though £7450
had been spent in plant during the half-year. Profits,
shown above, have not, however, been charged with the
full amount spent on the new shaft. By the end of cur-
rent month the overdraft should have been wiped out or
thereabouts; and then divs. may be looked for again.
Even on last half-year’s basis, profits were at the rate of
nearly 7s. per ton of ore ; and present run averages about
3100 tons weekly. B.H. Prop, made last half-year just
upon 10s. per ton, and B.H. South still more ; and Block
10 ought to pass the B.H. Prop.’s figure easily enough
while metals keep present price. Market values the mine
at £252,000. It has often valued a mine higher with less
cause.
• • * *
Seems to be some uncertainty about assays from
B.H. South Blocks and other prospecting shows, which
are not subject to mill tests. Junction North lately gave
fire assays from the 1137 level, but additional and higher
values for lead contents by the wet process. There is
enough “discretion” used, as the manager of Block 10
pointed out, in sampling ore, without using any in assay-
ing. The method of assays should be stated.
«** ■ *
First four months of this year B.H. Block 14’s
have been the best (or worst) gamble among stocks
quoted on all exchanges. They went from 3s. sd. to 13s.
101 d., and flopped back to 9s. 7d. Junctions were close
up, from 4s. 3d. to 10s. lOd. Off the Barrier, Smith’s
Creek, the N.Q. tin show on which Adelaide once went
nap, played between 3s. Bd. and 7s. lid. ; but they stick
around 7s. A heap of money was dropped on Hainaults
and Lake Views ; latter touched 295. and 16s. 3d. Alto-
gether, the gamblers can’t growl that they haven’t had
plenty of excitement.
* * * »
Cobar Copper Mine hasn’t got quite everything
it expected from the Chesney deal. Copper value is
all right, but Chesney’s mission was to furnish enough
silica to flux the basic ore of the copper mine. For this
purpose Chesney has failed, and Syndicate is again buying
quartz locally. Oobar Gold Mines has a huge supply of
clean silica and an undecided policy, therefore there are
possibilities that Syndicate’s grasp may stretch out that
Way * , ' .
Queen Bee, Cobar (N.S.W.), often referred to
as rather better than the ordinary run of copper shows,
has lately emphasised the unwisdom of giving an option
over portion of its shares. Said option was a smart move
by a Sydney man, who lost no time in hustling his expert
down the mine to say if 1000 shares were worth 235. 6d.
Of course they weren’t (how could they be when market
quoted buyers scarce at 20s ? And expert’s decision that
smelting was altogether ahead of developments was only
to be expected. Something had to be said ; and as there
was nothing wrong with the mine or the ore going to the
mill, derogatory remarks applied themselves to ore that
wasn’t in sight through tardy development. As a matter
of fact, surface indications at Queen Bee, with but a few
tons of ore at grass, don’t suggest an overwhelming
development of ore body ; but so far as things have gone
this isn’t the fault of the mine, it’s the fault of the policy.
Anyhow, with expert Daly’s exit, question arises, whose
shares went into the option ? And who authorised the
examination ?
« • * *
Occidental’s new Moore slimes process is in
course of introduction. Occidental has 85,000 tons of
tailings, going 2dwt. ; said that new process will treat
at 2s. 9d. a ton. If so, tailings heap should send a fair
cake of gold to the bank during current year. Inter-
esting to note that directors have at last seen the wisdom
of a reserve fund. It may, for a while, interfere with
divs., but it will make them more regular by and bye.
* * * *
Burra copper show, near Mt. Boppy (N.S.W.),
is to be given another chance. In bygone days of ex-
pensive treatment Burra furnished some pretty rich stuff,
mostly from pockets at upper levels ; but there isn’t
much surface indication now of past workings, except on
uninviting mullock tips and the remains of a furnace.
J ust before closing down mine looked to have a chance of
lode opening up at lower levels. Just then water rushed
in, and the pumps never got there to explain things.
Burra has lately been worked in a little way, on tribute,
at a profit, and as it is only four miles from Mt. Boppy
mine, and skirting the same granite belt, development of
lower levels is worth while.
* * * *
Golden Fleece, Wyalong (N.S.W.), treated 20
tons at Cockle Creek for Boz. per ton. Stone was raised
from the 500 ft. level on the western reef. It looks per-
manent. At True Blue levels are being run at 800 ft. on
sulphide ore with satisfactory results. Important work
to be taken in hand shortly will be connecting True Blue
with Brilliant at the 800 ft. Both properties are owned
by E. E. Tyler, on behalf of an English co. A property
that shows signs of coming again is Operator, where
tributors are putting down a shaft to cut the reef in
ground hitherto untouched. There is a large decrease in
the output of free milling ore compared with the field’s
first three years. Owing to the scarcity of material, it is
intended to temporarily close down Channon’s cyanide
works ; and Nicolas’ battery and the Huntingdon mill are
idle.
• * • *
The Star of the East crowd who took over the
management from the old crowd are not making the hair
fly. People have had too much of Sebastopol Plateau
stocks, and if Ballarat wants them to jig it must provide
the funds itself to pay the piper. The Consols lode at
2200 ft. is what it has been for hundreds of feet above—a
good body of quartz minus payable gold ; while the down-
ward continuation of the Guiding Star lode isn’t worth
the little flutter that was attempted last week, when it
was said that gold was showing.
* * * *
A phrase from a manager’s report set the Mt.
Tarrangower, Maldon (Vic.), share racket going
“Battery started, concentrates will be heavy.” It
was a sort of “Up guards and at ’em ” signal,
for the heavy brigade swung into line, and ever
since have been gobbling up spec.-selling differences.
So far, the mine is an ordinary little show ; it has pro-
duced between 200 and 300 tons of quartz, which no one
locally expects to average lOdwt. There is about as much
evidence that the co. has struck the Lisle shoot of stone
as there is that the co.’s prospects are rose-tinted. Until
some reserve of stone is opened up and its value proved,
to buy shares is to let people out who ought to be kept in.
However, the battery has started; and, as said concen-
trates are “heavy,” that is a ray of light on the wintry
sky. Perhaps the whole horizon will be suffused with
gold when the plates are cleaned. Poor speckers if it
should be so !
* * * *
With the Duke of Wellington, Meredith (Vic.),
out of the way as a dividend-payer, save to “ short ” sellers,
a side glance is being taken at the Duke of York. This is
the one-time hero of the market corner which brought
several brokers down. Mine has been out of sight almost
since settlements were made. For one thing, investors
hate stocks that have been made gambling counters;
secondly, gold has not been found in payable quantities ;
thirdly, there is the present silence of the directors over the
challenge thrown out by Holst, of Ballarat, to take the
allegations contained in his circular into the law court.
On broad lines it can be said of the mine that it has some
patches of good stone. If these were used with judg-
ment, two or three payable, perhaps good, crushings
might be possible. But bn the principle that one swallow
does not make a summer, so it will be wise to remember
that more than a few average returns are required to
make the Duke of York an investment.
* •■**....
Men who wish they hadn’t. Two members
of the Stock Exchange of Melbourne, forgetting the
divine precept to love one another as brothers, fell to
fighting like brothers. The bigger and bulkier led off,
but the smaller and more active not only got the first
whack in, but slipped his fellow-member’s head into
Chancery, and held it there till someone intervened. Up
to this stage it was a very ordinary row between two very
ordinary mortals over a very ordinary and insignificant
share sale. Then the committee lifted the dispute
into quite a higher realm. Solemn people sat to
adjudicate on the row. Finally the aggressor was fined
£2O and his adversary £lO. All of which shows that if
you want to have a “ scrap,” have it where the ordinary
pplice magistrate can adjudicate ; for then 20s. and a
caution will probably fit the case.
* ■* * *
The unwisdom of Ministers of the Crown re-
taining their positions on directorates of mining co.’s is
shown by the following extract, taken from the circular
report of John Hutchinson, an outside share-pushing
broker in London, and published by the Times. The ex-
tract closes a puff in respect to the Victorian Cornish
mine, Daylesford (Vic.): “A highly encouraging' feature
is that the chairman of the local board of directors is the
Hon. Donald M‘Leod, Minister of Mines of the colony of
Victoria.”
* * *
A sixpenny div., declared by Cock’s Pioneer
Co. (Eldorado), is the first reward of a long patience on
the part of the shareholders. The Pioneer, which is the
largest public co. sluicing in Victoria, was floated during
the dredging boom with £4200 cash to its credit, and
calling power. It began operations by letting a contract
for £5700 worth of steam engines and electric plant without
appointing a consulting engineer to prepare plans and
specifications and boss the show. The result was a
muddle that required much consultation of many en-
gineers in the thereafter, for nearly every bit of machinery
had to be altered, the remedying of one original evil
only serving to reveal fresh trouble. From first to last
nearly £20,000 of shareholders’ money was spent where,
perhaps, a wise expenditure of £lO,OOO would have
sufficed. Mr. J. S. Dane—Melbourne’s festive “Jack”
Dane —was the promoter of the co., and he seems to have
been the moving spirit of confidence who kept the share-
holders jogging along through the wilderness. All the
calls were fully paid up. Dane secured the services of a
sturdy Scot engineer, Hugh Falconer, by whom the plant
was finally put on a working basis. The field of opera-
tions is over 72ft. deep, and is worked by two centri-
fugal pumps, in series. The total height of lift is 130 ft.,
or thereabouts, and the hopes of the most sanguine in-
vestors have risen to quite a lofty altitude.
* dr * *
“Wamba”: There is trouble at Talbot (Vic.)
over dredging proposals, and petitions and counter-
petitions have been presented with regard to the granting
of dredging leases. One of the “ anti ’’-petitions was carted
round for signature by an old man. “ I don’t mind the
blasted thing myself,” he said; “ I’ll soon cross over ; but
what about the risin’ generation ? The dredges will ruin
the soil, for a few quid, and leave the place desolate.
And then what will the risin’ generation do? The land
isn’t too good now, but we can get a bit of crop and fruit
off it, but what’ll the risin’ ” I fled ; but I heard him
awhile after button-holing for a fresh signature, and the
only words I could catch were “the risin’generation.”
The leases have, however, been granted ; but, all the
same, there is force in the old man’s objections. Dredging
ruins the land dredged, and is a very shortsighted busi-
ness in most cases where the land is any good at all.
They have learned this in the western States of America,
where the “ risin’generation ” found its heritage turned
into mud and stones.
* * » *
Lake View Consols, Kalgoorlie, is still pushing
on developments eagerly, and will shortly start opening
out at 2000 ft. At 1200 ft. L.V. lode has been cut 230 ft.
east of shaft, and assays 9dwt. for 4ft. wide. This looks
poor, but then this mine’s ore reserves average only Bdwt.
Everything on field is gradually getting down to bedrock,
and days of ounce values and fireworks outputs are past.
Raven and party have erected a 5-head mill on Hannan’s
Reward North though the stuff is estimated at only 6dwt.
for 25ft. wide ; while Presgrave and party are erecting a
Huntingdon mill on Excelsior (late Hannan’s King Brown
Hill), whose lode is 30ft. wide and estimated at sdwt.
Both properties are within 10 minutes walk of Kalgoorlie
post office. Thornett Bros, have a 10-head mill running
on Mt. Ferrum, and are knocking out a good living from
stuff going less than 4dwt. over the plates, but cyaniding
produces double that value. The lode is quite 50ft.
wide. Eades has long run 15-head on Devon Consols on
4dwt. ore, including tailings.
* * * *
Great Coolgardie (W.A.), which fizzled in July,
1901, after an ignominious seven years, and one recon-
struction, in still in hands of a London Official Receiver.
Capt. Warne, oldest mine manager on the fields, ran the
show successfully on Bull’s bullion from its inception in
Sept., 1894, to July, 1901. Then Rosewarne came, and
insisted on a test crushing. Result, 200 tons panned out
only 2oz. ldwt. 6gr., valued at £7 Bs. 7d. This is world’s
record, nearest approach being a crushing from Meyer
and Leeb, Transvaal, result of which John Scott, the then
manager and erstwhile boss of Kanowna Consols, wore on
his watch-chain. It weighed about loz., but tonnage is
not recorded.
• * * *
Karridale, Burtville (W.A.), has treated 134
tons for 13400 z. by amalgamation, and there is 26dwt. in
tailings by assay. Total from mine to date 1500 tons for
3800 oz. There are many small rich shows in this district,
but reefs are small, water level is only 15 to 20ft. below
surface, and work is soon blocked. No co.’s operate,
and fossickers are left to their own resources.
• • • •
Princess Royal Central, Norseman (W.A.), is
forging ahead at a great rate. After sinking main shaft
to 975 ft., opening up was started at 950 ft. At first reef
was worthless, but in driving south, values of 6 to Bdwt.
were obtained, and mill tests confirmed assays. Manager
Henderson anticipates getting better ore further south,
and is pushing on. Another month or two will show
whether venture is to prove a success.
* * * *
South Kalgurli (W.A.) is making slow pro-
gress with new treatment plant, and it will be about a
month before it starts. Plant is designed to treat 4500
tons monthly,"and ore reserves are estimated at 100,000
tons, worth 10'8dwt., equal to nearly two years’ supply.
If costs can be brought down to, say 305., there should
be a fair margin of profit. Development is being prose-
cuted in Perseverance, or Morty, lode, hitherto neglected,
and it will shortly be tested at 1200 ft. It appears to be
better than the L.V. lode. A year ago a bore found ore
worth 4oz. for 14ft. of core, supposed to be 345 ft. north of
Perseverance, and 90ft. south of Hainault. But bore
holes are so unreliable.
* * * 4
Great Fingall, Day Dawn (W.A.),'continues to
look healthy at No. 11 north drive. Lode at furthest
point reached 700 to 750 ft.—ranges from 6 to 24dwt.
over a width of 7to Bft. Very little has been done at No. 12
beyond cutting a chamber, but work will be started there
shortly. An increase of treatment plant is mooted ; this
will make mine far the biggest producer in the State.
Quarterly divs. are assured for some time, as ore reserves
are quoted as over 500,000 tons, but this is not accepted
unhesitatinglv. With new plant 10s. quarterly is even
talked of. This would mean £500,000 a year—a pretty
tall order.
* # * #
A German expert named Roliler is inspecting
and sampling Great Fingall Consols, and it is said he will
report that ore reserves have been much over-stated. It
may be pointed out that March return panned out at
17dwt. only, against 23dwt. estimated value of reserves on
July 1 last. This shows either an extraction of 74 per
cent, only, or a possible over-estimate of gold contents.
Since estimate was published, 10 months ago, monthly
returns have averaged 18dwt. Tonnage treated since July,
about 150,000 tons.
* * • *
Fair Play, Kalgoorlie, about half a mile north
of Hidden Secret, has treated 34 tons for 2900 z. This was
won from an oxidised lode at 85ft. Like Hidden Secret,
it is part of the ground bottled up for years by Hannan’s
Prop. This co. held a square mile of territory, most of it
totally unprospected ; but recent success of Hidden
Secret and Fair Play is stimulating work. Fair Play was
recently put under offer to a Perth syndicate, but a
demand for a cash deposit of £2OO was too much for the
Perthites, and the deal fell through.
*•* , *
Monte Christo, Kalgoorlie, is being equipped
with a 10-head mill to treat alluvial soil and cement,
which covers the area to an average depth of about 6ft.
Kalgoorlie-Boulder railway intersects property, and when
railway was being built Government collared thousands
of tons of this ground to make embankment. The co.
which held the ground sued Government, and, after
tests, was awarded 30s. per ton, less estimated treatment
charges, stuff being valued at 7idwt. Since then dry-
blowers have been continuously at work turning over soil,
and have probably materially reduced the average.
Battery, in course of erection, is antediluvian, but
beggars can’t be choosers.

The Wilfley Slime Table which has been in
operation on the Ivanhoe Gold Mines for some months
past has been giving extremely good results. Mr. H. T
Brett, Metallurgist of the Ivanhoe Gold Corporation, in an
article contributed to the March number of the Chamber
of Mines Journal, W.A., gives particulars of the savings
made, and states that the introduction of the Wilfley
Slime Table will be of great service in treating the low-
grade sulphide ores of the field. Since the future of
Kalgoorlie is so intimately bound up with the most
economical solution of this sulphide problem, this
assurance from Mr. Brett is a most important matter.

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,

Oil Engines.—lnformation has just reached
us that the Government Award (Gold Medal) in this class,
at the present Capetown Exhibition, has been awarded to
the “ Hornsby ” Oil Engine. The success of this engine is
perhaps the most remarkable in engineering history.
There is no engineering speciality which can claim, as
the “ Hornsby ” oil engine does, to have obtained every
highest award in the world—viz., England, America,
Australian, France and Russia, &c. —and last year it
secured the British Government award of £lOOO. It is
made in various types of fixed, portable, traction and
marine, and in the latter it is made in suitable sizes for
small pleasure craft, and in gradations of h.p. up to 500
b.h.p., as supplied to the British Navy. R. Hornsby and
Sons, Ltd., Sydney and Melbourne.*

The Babcock Boiler seems to be favored by
mining engineers, over 50 mines in Australia using these
boilers. Mount Morgan has 20, Waihi Gold Mining Co.
24, Mount Lyell 12, Tasmanian Gold Mining Company 7,
and at Broken Hill there are 30 distributed amongst the
mines, water works, and electric supply, while in
Western Australia they are looked upon as a necessity,
there being now 68 in the principal mines and Kalgoorlie
Water Supply. It will be noted that coal is expensive at
Broken Hill, and wood fuel is expensive at W.A. These
figures speak for themselves. Evidently the ease of
transportation, the economy in fuel and maintenance and
the adaptability to any class of fuel, that are such con-
spicuous points in the Babcock Boiler, are much
appreciated in Australian mines. * *

Mt. Steadman, back from Maryborough and
Gympie (Q.), is in the liquidator’s hands; and mine,
plant, and a brand-new battery are up for sale. Co. was
formed in Glasgow about 2b years ago, and got to work in
a brisker way than that very dead district had known for
years. Work was laid out on a substantial scale, and a
10-head battery (which, at the time, was said to be the
first section of a 20-head mill) was put up. The mine had
less attention than the surface ; hardly anything was
done beyond cleaning out old workings ; and after the
battery had run only two shifts it was shut down,
no water being available. While the co. waited for
rain it got tired, and, after spending about £BOOO on
the property, let its affairs go into liquidation by the
court. The Bull co. is mostly a sticker; but
this co. made about the poorest showing on record. And
it had something to go on, too ; for years ago, after some
very rich stuff had been taken out and treated at Gympie, a
large body of crushing dirt was struck in the Venus
claim, at 110 ft., and 400 or 500 tons was put through for
16dwt. In workings from a tunnel in the mountain the
lode is a good deal broken, but in places there is sft. of
crushing stuff (from 8 to 18dwt.) available. There is
another reef, narrower but strong and rich in places, and
it looks as though the two might junction further down.
The whole thing will probably go for a song, and whoever
gets it will find plenty of justification for a bit of deeper
sinking.

WHISKY.
It is Harmsworth of the Daily Mail and
many other papers, the man who is 14 different
“ we’s,” the Napoleon of the Press combine, who
boasts in private that he is “going to see that
Australia borrows no more money in London.”
If he succeeds he will be doing Australia a good
service. Still, does it not impress the serious
danger to democracy of the modern movement
towards a Press Trust when one newspaper
“ boss ” can thus threaten to stick up a continent ?

In a pre-maternity case heard at Adelaide,
the other day, a 16-years-old plaintiff stated that
she first fell a victim to the wiles of defendant (a
grave-digger) when at the West Terrace cemetery
watering the flowers on her mother’s grave ! In
the midst of death men and women are in life.
* * * *
A casual Australian female was before two
North Melbourne magistrates, the other day,
charged with neglecting her infant child, five
weeks old. Evidence showed that, when arrested,
the mother was drunk in a room, where a man lay
on a bed drunk, and another woman, also drunk,
nursed the child, which had a beer-soaked rag in
its mouth, and was likewise drunk. The Bench
told the woman to clear out, and she left the court
with her infant. And the Bench was not drunk,
To remind Q. of the part her farm-
burners took in the Boer slaughter, the War
Office is sending out to Brisbane two field
guns and ten cases of rifles. On arrival they will
be handed over to (it makes one smile) Chief
Sec. Airey. So far, no arrangements have
been made as to their disposal. No one seems to
want them. At one time, while the country was
frantic with war fever, the Mateking rioters would
have rushed around to meet such a consignment
with roystering cheers and a band procession.
Now they are as last night’s cigar-stump and
empty bottle.

N.S.W. Presbyterians the other day held
an “antique service” of the type of the John
Knox days. The “kist of whustles” was
silenced, and good, old-fashioned hymns, with
plenty of Hell-fire in them, intoned with good
nasal vigor. Even the modern collection was
abrogated for a more ancient but less searching
method of harvesting the thrums; and when a
church tampers with the sacredness of the collec-
tion it is in dead earnest. But. alas, the antique
flavor was spoiled by the preaching. There was
not to be found in these modern, degenerate days
a good four-hours stayer, capable of pursuing a
subject to fourteenthly, with some last thoughts
before finally, and of weeping and howling artis-
tically in the pulpit. The hymns were ancient,
the collection was ancient, but the man in the
pulpit was modern. The gentle light of civilisa-
tion has exorcised the old-fashioned black Presby-
terianism from the parsons of this land.
Japan and Britain are supposed to be
allied to preserve the integrity of Korea and
Manchuria. Japan now wishes to talk about re-
viewing the alliance, but not on any integrity
basis—on a plain thieving platform of the absorp-
tion of those provinces by Japan.
The present short-sighted prison system
i n Australia is always shouting out for alteration,
It can ’ t dea l with the despicable beings that can
~,f , ° . ,
but won * work ’ except to keep themselves m beer
and skittles, and who throw on the State the task
of providing the necessaries of life for their
families. The problem is always before the
Benches. A father is hopelessly in arrears for
the maintenance of his youngsters, who are in the
care of the State. If he is sent to gaol—to which
he’d evidently not object much—the State simply
has to keep "the whole family. Prisons should
be labor, hard labor, and. profit-making labor
places, and then the problem is solved. The man
who won’t work outside to keep his family can
then be made to work inside.
Things are changing up north, where “the
white man can’t work.” Proserpine mill is
putting up decent quarters for the men in place
of the old abominations. Hitherto, in most of the
sugar districts, a man has been thrown into . a
pig-stye and expected not to be a pig. Some men
wouldn’t be pigs even
in a pig-stye, and in
that case they mostly
got out of it and
sought other work.
It wasn’t a question
of “can’t work;”
it was merely a
question of “won’t
work” till the con-
ditions were madefit.
Treasurer Kidston,
when he was up that
way lately, struck a
good sample of local
conditions. At Moss-
man’s he found about
a dozen pubs., but no
savings bank. The
men were paid by
cheque, and the pub.
was the place where
the cheque was melted.
It was a lovely scheme
for demoralising the
white, and helping the
argument of the nigger-
lover ; but Kidston
decided that things are
to change. For a start,
the men are to be paid
in cash, and a savings
bank will be opened.

The proposal to exterminate rabbits by
inoculating them with disease germs, if put into
practice, will probably cause the appearance of a
new human disorder. But it won’t extirpate the
pest. The bubonic plague was first communicated
to man by the rat, and it has been waging war
against rats sinee the world was young ; but the
rat increases faster than the plague microbe, And
the rabbit is even a quicker breeder than the rat.
* * * *
Maoriland, which a couple of years ago
gorged itself with Jingoism, is now suffering from •
nausea. The latest proof of the state of the
public’s moral stomach, as it were, comes from
Wellington school committee. The committee, in
its annual report:—
There is one other matter your committee tried to do,
which was to have the board make the sanitary arrange-
ments fit for a civilised country and fit for a self-respecting
child to use. This, however, the board considered such a
trifling matter compared to Navy League snobbishness
and sticking a flag on a pole, that it has entirely ignored
the committee’s recommendation, and consequently the
sanitary arrangements are as indecent and disgusting as
they were 12 months ago.
* * » •
Bent Thomas, of Victoria, is just now
arguing points with a far-seeing lady whose hus-
band was killed while on duty on the railways.
Instead of making the usual claim for compensa-
tion, she has offered the Brighton statesman the
option of paying compensation, or of providing
with permanent railway employment another
young man whom she proposes to make hubby
No. 2. Theoretically, there is but one way of
getting into the railway service—by examination—
but practically Bent appoints whom he pleases,
and if there is a chance of making a present saving
of a hundred or two by appointing the widow’s
new mate to a billet, Bent is the man to do it,
and blow the Law !

The North Australian syndicate, to which
in ’Ol S. Australia foolishly granted the lease, for
42 years, of 6234 square miles of country on the
Roper River (N.T.), for an annual rental of £3BO,
and which nursed its “mineral, pastoral and all
other concessions” for four years, has at last
persuaded somebody to “take up” a part of its
lease. The somebody is a Glasgow company,
which is understood to have paid a deposit of
£3OOO cash, and to have offered the syndicate
30,000 paid-up shares in the new co. Whether
the canny Scots propose to raise buffaloes, or
export beef, or develop the mineral resources of
the “ province ” is not known—probably all three;
but the one obvious fact about this precious N.A.
syndicate is that, for all it has done to develop its
concession, S’Australia had better have hawked
its lost province round Britain itself. As to the
labor question, whether the Scot reckons to
import Hindus, Chows or whatnot, nobody seems
to know anything ; but the deal is one that wants
watching very closely indeed.
THE RETORT COURTEOUS.
PADDY: “ What breed may he be?”
DUDE: “ A cross between an Irishman and a
skunk.”
PADDY: “So he is related to both of us.”
According to a Tariff Commission witness,
much inferior spirit is imported into Australia
and used indifferently for making whisky or
furniture polish. A man in fact may use it for
polishing his sideboard or his nose.
* * * *
In a Hebrew mansion you never meet a
Hebrew servant, or a Hebrew butler or footman.
You may meet Isaac in the counting-house or in
the store, but never in menial service.
Until one of Melbourne’s coroners com-
mits someone for murder, the casual infant will
continue to be starved to death. Dr. Cole,
sitting lately on the usual infant, spoke firmly
about the “case’s” mother. The child had been
under three nurses, the second of whom hired a
wet-nurse, at some expense, and the infant thrived.
But the cost was alleged to be too much, so the
baby was sent back to artificial food again, and
died. It was shown that, since the child’s birth,
the mother had married well, and was quite able
to afford the wet-nurse expenses, but the infant
was an undesirable, and artificial food is quite as
efficacious as, and a good deal less dangerous than,
strangling or drowning. The bench only “spoke
firmly.” It didn’t issue a warrant.
A LESSON FROM THE PUPPY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I, by a wayside water standing, saw
A wild parabola of plimgipg pup,
With eloquent, vain protest of the paw,
Flung wide into the weir and swallowed up.
Thrice, struggling to the bank, was he thrust down.
Could he have heard, I would have whispered him :
“ ’Twill be the same to-morrow, why not drown ?
What matters it whether you sink or swim?”
Am Iso wise, then ? I have seen strong lives
Sucked down and stifWl in life’s foulest mud,
And yet in weakling me a something strives
Not to be quenched. What is this in the blood,
T muse, that will not let a puppy sink
tVhile he has strength to drag him to the brink ?
C.S.W.
GOD’S GOOD MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ God’s good man ” stood at Heaven’s gate ;
Assured he spoke : “ I am known here,
You labor under some mistake.
Go and inquire, sir, I shall wait.
My li e has been a life austere,
And not for me the brimstone lake.
Peter looked pity : “ Man, come in ”
But sounds of revels bright and gay
Burst through it when the gate stood wide ;
Gasping “ This is some den of sin,”
To see the merry crowds at play,
“ God’s good man ” dourly kept outside.
“ My brethren would not join that throng ;
Those dancing crowds they would condemn.
Where are my brethren ? Tell me, pray !
They sing good hymns the whole day long.
No trifling, Peter ; show me them I ”
Quoth Peter : “ They’re across the way !" '
Sternly he passed the gate and gained
A groaning black-clad crowd, who scowled
At “ God’s good man ” with fiery eyes ;
But by no doubting was he pained.
i nd when in agony they howled,
1 e said, “ Ah ! this is Paradise ! ”
F. HAMER.

S.A. Supreme Court has just shut down —
on the application of Paris Nesbit, K.C. —on a
strong attempt by lawyers to undermine the pro-
visions of the Local Courts Act for cheap law.
The Act provides a carefully graded scale of fees
for solicitors (every solicitor is a barrister as well
inS.A.). £1 has to cover the whole case for a
claim under £2O. Over £2OO the schedule grows
more generous, and*allows £7 for getting up the
case and £3 for “attending trial.” A recent hard-
fought case ran to seven days in court, and the
winning side tried to recover the £3 seven times
over; but the higher authority decided that the
single fee had to cover the whole calamity.
* * * *
Said that steps are to be taken to
regulate the admission of domain dossers and
others to the Free Public Library, Sydney. Some
regulation is wanted, or, if it exists, wants
enforcing in regard to supervision of the readers
at the library. The nation’s property should be
preserved from spoiling, and no man should be
admitted to the book collection who is in a filthy
state. Even the poorest can be clean if they
choose.
* * * *
Stated in the press that the underwriters
are instituting inquiries into the rumor that the
bulkhead doors of the Orizaba could not be closed
when the vessel struck off Rottnest Island. This
revives the old difficulty over bulkhead doors.
It is surprising how careless of this vital point
many vessels’ crews are. The wise law that every
iron steamship must be divided into sub-divisions
by watertight partitions is universally adopted,
but no one has yet found a way to ensure that the
doors between the several compartments can
be easily and properly shut when occasion de-
mands that they be banged-to in a hurry. It is
not so long ago that a well-filled passenger ship
in collision on the Australian coast was only saved
from appalling disaster by the proximity of a
friendly beach. So swiftly did the water engulph
the ship that experts readily saw that the water-
tight partition doors could not have been shut, as
demanded by regulation, though the deck officers
swore that their orders to close them were in-
stantly obeyed. As the company was an influ-
ential one, and no lives fortunately had been
sacrificed, the matter was not probed to the
bottom. So serious is such a, lapse regarded that
it might vitiate some of the insurance policies.

and obey? ”
WIFE: “ How do I know what I did ? I was

Out of 1501 candidates for positions in
Vic. police force, only 121 were found to be abso-
lutely fit and proper men, another 58 are doubtful,
who have still a chance, but the rest are not
wanted on the job. And yet the Melbourne
policeman is blamed for assuming the haughty
airs of a superior person. There is an unwritten
rule that good looks shall tell in applicants’ favor
too. The idea is to make it the irresistible force
evidently.
The present monopoly by
one firm of meat-purvey to in-
coming vessels to Sydney has
killed the old competition,
which kept several “butcher-
boats” down at Watson’s Bay
to book orders. They were
specially constructed for the
work—being long, narrow,
light, and very fast. The other
day a survivor of the fleet left a
few minutes before the pilot
boat (steam), and arrived at a
ship, two miles out, first.

“ C.N.” : Remark in re-
cent Bulletin about Anglo-
Indians desiring colored crews
on mail-boats, in order that
they may bully them according
to custom, recalls the fact that
on almost every voyage to
England there is a row on
leaving Colombo, owing to the
newcomers calling loudly
“ Boy !” instead of “ Steward !”
The white steward, though his
ranks are already being invaded
by the Indian, declines to
answer to that call, and ructions
almost invariably take place.

At a recent Sydney school
teachers’ meeting one speaker
referred to the studied absence
of the Australian poets from the
local University’s exams. He
challenged the authorities to
show that the work of Kendall,
Dales', Quinn and others was
not as worthy of study as much
of the matter put before the
local youngsters for examina-
tion purposes.

Maoriland Government
deals strenuously with the
population problem. A railway
employee, a porter, got five
weeks’ holidays, recently on
the plea that his girl lived be-
yond the horizon in backmost
N.S.W. and he wanted to
marry her. Leave to a porter
for such an unheard-of length of
time is unobtainable on any
other pretext, even the death of
a parent. A short while back
an out-of-work asked Seddon
for a job as temporary clerk.
“Are you married?” roared
the boss. “No,” replied the
applicant. “Well, get mar-
ried and I’ll give you a job,”
thundered Seddon, and the ap-
plicant withdrew. Four days
later he returned, accompanied
by a lady, whom he introduced
as Mrs. Out-of-work. The as-
tounded Seddon after a few seconds found his
tongue—and the job. #
While the Sydney Council is endeavoring
to hand the key of the street to motor omnibus
speculators, and proposes to license certain char-
tered libertines to kill old, deaf, and slow-footed
citizens at sight, it is interesting to read the
speech of John Burns in the House of Commons,
wherein he declared that motor omnibuses were
not only larger, dirtier and dearer, but also more
dangerous than tramcars because of their “roving
commission.”

The patience of at least one of that long-
suffering tribe, the country doctor, has given out;
and he talks as follows to Gundagai (N.S.W.) per
local advt. :
A battalion of make-
believe impecunious
rogues, who can find
ways and means for
every source of costly
apparel, entertainment
and amusement, with
a trip or two to either
Sydney or Melbourne
thrown in, find always
a plague-rat hole in
which to escape from
the doctor’s fees. The
honest ever - grateful
poor have always had
the services of the pro-
fession throughout the
whole world, and will
continue to have them
while, and as long as,
the poor continue to
exist; but the dis-
honest vagabonds who
endeavor to cheat the
Almighty God Himself
with their whining
tongue and hypocritic-
al promises, can hardly
be expected to be either
grateful or sincere and
honest to those who serve
them in times of sickness and
distress. To all such (and I have a
squadron of them on my books), I say,
“ Go to the practitioner who prescribes your deceit, your
lying, and your dishonesty. Go to him, he will write
your prescription in indelible pitch, and knows well how
to supply the actual cautery, an application much needed
by your dishonest souls and bodies.
