Recent Melbourne beer tragedy is an
almost exact replica of an incident of 20 years ago.
In that case three mates took a trip to a Hobson
Bay port in a small coasting steamer, and sampled
a bottle of beer qn the journey. Immediately all
three became violently ill, and one died before
they could reach a port. That beer had been
bottled by a Melbourne brewery, which got the
bottle in the ordinary course from a gathering
yard; but analysis discovered strychnine crys-
tals in the bottle, which had been imperfectly
washed.

CONCERNING SAPPHIRA'B HUSBAND.
Victoria’s Insolvency Judge Molesworth
lately investigated the affairs of an extra-saving
couple—a pattern pair of thrifty souls. They
married in ’97, when the wife spent her savings of
£lO on furniture and hubby made over to her all
his property, a house on which some £5O was
owing. She cleared that off, then set about pur-
chasing two other properties on time-payment.
Since marriage she had earned on an average 10s.
a week as a white-worker. Hubby also had been
in steady toil, and had regularly handed his wife
all his earnings, from £2 to £2los. weekly, and she
put the savings into property. He neither drank,
smoked, nor gambled; his only amusement was
to take his children to the gardens, and then “ he
never bought them any fruit or lollies.” Latterly
his earnings had fallen off, and since, presumably,
the time-payment properties did not prove suc-
cessful, he failed, liabilities £626, assets £257.
What a lesson on “thrift”! Never smoked, nor
gambled, nor drank ; never even bought the laddies
a ha’porth of lollies. Poor kiddies ! And poor
thrifty man and wife ! They can be sure of but
the one life, and what have they made of it ?
Yarra Bank (Melb.) asylum was greatly
fluttered, the other day, by the reception of a
’phone message from the detective office, which
seemed to point to the Waranga Basin murderer
being within the walls. A hasty interview with
the suspect (who was under another name) pro-
duced promising results. “Did he know Waranga
Basin?” “O yes!” “Did he work there?”
“My oath!” “Did he know a man named
Skinner?” A cheerful “Yes, well!” made the
asylum hum with gladness. When the party
admitted he had gone under the name of James
Edwards, there was soul-satisfying joy. In other
matters the man was obligingly corroborative.
He said he had sold a horse and dray at
Murchison about the time Edwards is supposed to
have sold a horse and dray at the same place. Also
he had sent a postal-note to his brother, and the
time of his admission was handy to the beginning
of the hue and cry after Edwards. But he wasn’t
the man wanted. His self-identification with
Edwards was merely a natural freak of his lunacy.
He was having a gcod, hilarious time at the
expense of the law.
Thomas Bent, last week, let daylight into
one of the North Melbourne hotel cases. The
Arbitration Court awarded £1494 as compensation
to the owner of a doomed pub., and £491 to the
occupier, a woman. Then the woman’s creditors
started in pursuit of her compensation allowance.
The landlord, the brewery, and the lawyers put in
claims amounting altogether to £550, and finally
they got a judgment and sold her furniture for
£25. She says it was worth £250. Anyhow, the
disposal of the furniture left the creditors with a
full claim to the whole of the woman’s compensa-
tion —when she gets it. She called upon Bent,
whose heart loveth all lady hotel-keepers, and he
suggested that the hungry creditors should give
her £250 if he passed the grant of £491. They
refused; so Bent, in his amusing character of bene-
volent despot, refuses to pay any compensation
unless the lady gets her “whack.” Declaring that
he is not compelled to carry out the law’s com-
mand in these cases, he says that the Premier who
would not battle for a pub-keeping female in dis-
tress is unworthy of the name of Bent.

YESTERDAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Little sea-maiden with eyes so blue —
Blue as the waters of inshore bays—
With your clinging arms, and rebellious hair, and your
wonderful, Innocent baby ways—
Little sea-maiden, tell me, pray,
Have you forgotten our yesterday ?
Little sea-maiden, you seemed so true—
True as the steel of the tempered sword—
With your warm, ripe lips, and your laughter low, and a
breath of remembrance in every word.
Little sea-maiden, demure or gay,
You called me lover but yesterday.
Little sea-maiden, I would that I
Could forget the kiss that you said was play,
For its memories sting, and its memories stay, and the
future looms lifeless, and cold, and grey.
Little sea-maiden, you went your way,
And left me lonely that yesterday.
E. J.
THE VOICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From wood to wood remote
The sweet voice swells along
With rich, clear, purple note
In a pagan song.
’Tis the viola : Hark
To the music deep and bold 1
Dawn rising from the dark
With a voice of gold.
It thrills and fills the air,
Then, like a lover’s lute,
Beneath a Seville stair
Is tragically mute.
V.J.D.
THE PROMISED LAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I met a sage beside the road
(My staff and scrip in hand)—
“ Young stranger, bound for what abode ? ”
“ I seek the Promised Land.”
His tongue was passionate with truth—
“ You know not what you say ;
The land you seek is that of Youth—
You left it yesterday ! ”

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Mat 16, 1905.
My Dear Arini.—
Cabled news of Lady Rawson’s health is of
good quality, though meagre in quantity. After
she had left Fremantle she began to improve, and
got through the tropics without relapse. Gov.
Rawson, with private sec. Wilson, returned last
week, after an awfully stick-in-the-black-mud trip,
from Broken Hill. They drove 485 miles in
buggies, and the only members of the party that
didn’t enjoy the trip were the horses. Eight
horses to a buggy. The Governor, who had the
back seat to himself, and was always glad of
camp-and-boil-the-billy time, made the ac-
quaintance of several delightful men of the Dead
Horse Bob and Greenhide Harry type. At
every township en route the Governor held a re-
ception, while all the inhabitants—often to the
number of thirty souls—attended. Majority of
these townships had never previously seen a
Governor—were probably seriously disappointed
at his lack of resemblance to a circus. In the
baokblocks the circus is the gauge and standard of
magnificence.
State Governor Rawson is at present staying
with the Northcotes in Macquarie-street. That
is, he sleeps there, but his daytime address is
very uncertain, by reason of the numerous charity
meetings he is attending and hospital wings that
he is unfurling. Private Sec. Wilson has left for
Coolangatta, Dr. John Hay’s place, for a month’s
holiday and quailing. Messrs. E. Wood and
Heathcote Amory (what a sweet, Mrs. Humphry
Wardish name !), who abode with the North-
cotes a few days, are fellow-shootists. Mr. Wood
is son and heir of Viscount Halifax.
Justice Edmund Barton and family are house-
hunting. Their home at N. Sydney was sold last
week to the Prestons, fortunate Westralian specu-
lators, who have a motor car made from the design
of one owned by King Edward. The price given
for the house was £6OOO. It is a beautiful artistic
home with an unsurpassed view of the harbor, and
was at one time the property of the Prosper
Williams family.
The early mayoress catches the first mite. It
was very smart of the mayoress of Mosman to
“get there” first with a ball in aid of the
Alexandra Hospital for Children. For the next
few months there will be an outburst of suburban
dances in aid of the same charity. A big meeting
of the Central Committee, held in Sydney Town
Hall last week, was smile-provocative from one
point of view. A crowd of amateurs in drama
wrote expressing their willingness to make melo-
dies to the moon in aid of the charity if the com-
mittee would pay the rent of theatre or hall and
incidental expenses. At an early date Mr. J. C.
Williamson will give a matinee in aid of the fund.
Mrs. Hubert Kelly, sister of the late Mr.
Patrick Jennings, and squatteress in the New
England district, has thrown the Warrigal Club off
its centre by giving a fluffy at-home in its pre-
mises. Members hid their flasks and portraits of
ballet girls, and surrendered themselves to elevat-
ing influence of tea and scandal.
“Like a Parisienne.” When a woman is so
described you are supposed to be quite informed,
and to see a mental picture of a woman who is
an epitome of physical symmetry, elegance in
dress, and intellectual charm. With very few
exceptions, the French women who visit Aus-
tralia are very like prosperous, middle-aged mem-
bers of the Chosen race. Their dress is generally
above reproach, their intellectual charm may be
the same, but grace of outline and coloring (if ever
they were existent) must have taken very early
flight. Persons who have travelled much in
foreign parts say that they never saw elsewhere
prettier girls than in Sydney. Go to a fashion-
able gathering, ball or race meeting, go into
cafes and shops and see the waitresses and shop
girls, go even into Sydney factories, if you are
m search of the Girl Beautiful.
But do not look for beauty in the restaurant
where the attendants are chosen by the proprietor’s
wife. The coffee-and-angel-cake establishment
run by a public company is the place to seek it.
A public company cannot be talked blind by its
wife, or kept awake with curtain lectures.
Miss Edith Kirkwood, the handsome soprano of
the Watkin Mills concert eo., struck a sparkling
mailboat match on her way out here. A Mr.
Gray, of Bombay, is the prize-winner. Again:
the husband is a marine product.
. Mrs. Haigh Jackson, wife of the musical comedy
singer, gives a recital, vocal and instrumental, to-
morrow at Nicholson’s. The lady, who is tied up
to Melbourne Uni. Conservatonum, must hurrj
back to classes and lasses in the southern city.
Mr. Abraham Hoffnung, senior member of S.
Hoffnung and Co., is, with his wife, on a visit to
Sydney. Mrs. H. is a lady with a large number
of grandchildren, and not a furrow on her face.
Also with huge diamond solitaires in her ears.
The week before she left London Ada Crossley
and her young man lunched at her flat. The
young man is described as very tall and fair, and
wasn’t backward in declaring his intention of
taking care of Ada.
Miss May Summerbell, the last of Sydney’s
women composers whose music has : echoed in
foreign parts, is about to visit London to arrange
for the publication in book form of somewhere
about 90 of her compositions, and try to sell the
European and American rights in an Australian
opera. She will return immediately the business
is transacted. Miss Summerbell is the girl who
brought to Australia the gold medal offered by the
Chicago Exposition for the best original musical
composition. At the age of 12 she had a compo-
sition published by a hard-headed musical firm as
a paying investment; and since then the same
firm (Palings’) has published many others. Her
: music is played nightly by orchestras in Sydney
■ and Melbourne, and the ballets she composed
for the Players’ presentation of “A Runaway
Girl ” in Sydney recently, raised considerable
enthusiasm. Between songs, waltzes, marches,
an occasional cake-walk, a Mass, and an opera,
she finds time to swim and to write fiction. With
her music she takes to London a novel.
Mr. Arthur Adams, Maorilander, author of
many verses and a book or so, is on his way back
to Sydney from London.
Sydney girls will suffer heartaches over the
departure of M. Nettement, the bijou young
Frenchman who “did something” at the Con-
sulate. He had a good dancing connection, was
within easy reach of the best society and possessed
the neatest moustache in Sydney.
Another departer will be Mrs. Mark Sheldon,
who goes by way of America.
Baronet Rupert Clarke returns from Melbourne
presently in his yacht to make a trip along the
Queensland coast. Talking of yachts : Mr. Alfred
Gollin wants to know when Sydney will build a
racer that will try to recover the laurels he tore
from here last season.
If you take tickets for that worthy charity-hop,
the Dental ball, it will be a deed that Will crown
the day and help to gold-fill you with satisfaction.
State Governor Rawson is to be present. Hon.
secs., Mrs. W. T. Beckett and Mrs. Donald Smith.
Date, June 7, at Paddington Town Hall.
It was rather “pretty” of Ada Crossley to have
the music at her wedding made by Australians.
Mr. George Clutsam, formerly of Melbourne,
composed a bridal chorus for the occasion, which
was interpreted by Nora Long (of M.L.), Meta
Bfiring (of Adelaide), Ivy Ansley (of Auckland),
Eva Mylott (of Sydney), and the Misses E.
Florence and Mary Conly (of Melbourne).
A local doctor, widower, an authority on the
laws of psychic phenomena, is about to be married
to a young widow.
Overheard on city tram. “That crystal-reader
is a fraud. When me husband was at the war
with the Boers she said he’d come out of it alive,
but ’d die of Brike’s disease three months after.
That’s three year ago, and he’s got no sign of
ennybuddy’s disease as yit.”
A symposium of London tailors has decided
and declared that the fashionable color for men’s
attire shall be green in all shades —from violent
cat’s eye to Brussels sprouts. Olive green Hom-
burg hats, and motoring caps, dark green bowler
hats, greenish tweeds and flannels are seen in all
the leading tailor shops. Trousers are to be
much tighter. The general tendency is tp make
the thin man a green lamp-post and the stout man
an over-grown cabbage.
Every now and then an attempt is made to
make man a picturesquely clothed animal. It
always fails, because the poor thing is a worker.
Even if he’s a peer and rich he’s got to work or
make a good pretence of so doing, else heads
will be *• shaken at him, and other peers
will give him the frozen eye. Gone for
ever the picturesque knee-breeches, ruffles,
and clouded canes of the leisured beaux of
the Georgian era. All the portraits of famous
men of the knee-breeches period show the originals
to have good calves and straight legs. Painters’
padding, no doubt. There must have been G. H.
Reid figures inside those satin cylinders and velvet
coats, as well as those of the B. R. Wise and
Francis Suttor build. The great bar to the
picturesque dressing of Him is lack of leisure to
keep knees from growing “ knocked ” and bodies
from becoming triple expansion. Besides, She
couldn’t spare Him space in the wardrobes for
beruffled clothes.
Which reminds me:—
The great tragedy ot the average man’s life is that
Nature refuses to conform to the cylindrical ideal, and
when the marks of his knees and elbows appear in his
cylinders he is filled with shame.— Bernard Shaw.
New French Consul-General, in a pair of bright
orange boots, a very British suit, and decollete
collar, like Federal Justice O’Connor’s, is now an
addition to the early forenoon city traffic as he
goes to work. He makes the mistake of wearing
his mouth slightly open. Statesmen’s catchment
areas should have a locked-and-lost-the-key ex-
pression.
Miss Maud Jeffries is another early-bird, alight-
ing at florists, drapers and photographers,
dressed in dark blue, piped with crimson, and cul-
tivating a very small waist inside a wide leather
belt.
An American litterateur on the symphony :
The most profound truths, the most blasphemous
things, the most terrible ideas, may be incorporated
within the walls of a symphony, and the police be none
the wiser. Suppose some Russian professional supervisor
of artistic anarchy really knew what arrant doctrines
Tsehaxkows'ky preached!
With these few remarks I lead gently up to a
correction about a symphony. Awhile ago I
wrote that Tschaikowsky’s sym. (the Pathetique)
was presented to the Amateur Orchestral Soc.
by Lady Mary Lygon. In a note as nice as bee-
jam (as the little boy called honey), the hon.
sec. of the Society informs me that Miss Woolley
was the donor of that sym. The “information”
had been given me by a former hon. sec., and when
I confronted him with his error he behaved nobly.
Set all his worldly affairs in order, made provision
for all those dear to him, even to providing milk
for the cats of his bachelor household down to the
third and fourth generation, then drank a hem-
lock cocktail.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“We have a bustling, breezy Mayoress in Mrs.
Pleasance. She likes gadding, and does it with a
cheerful countenance. She knows, too, the way
to do things without nervousness or stilted dig-
nity. Mayoress Strong was an example of the
rigid housewife and absorbed mother, being lifted
bodily from the home life to the Mayoral dais. It
took her breath away, and when she recovered it
with a certain amount of sweet patience, her term
was done. In the case of Mrs. Gillott there was
the school-teacher reserve force to arm her Mayoral
person. She is a strong, self-reliant dame, who
can show fight, and she stood square and fair on
the dais, ready for any Social emergency. Mrs.
M'Eacharn was tempted a second time into the
sphere by a spouse who loved office, but the
nervousness that attacked her first appearance
never left her. The poor lady visibly shrank from
the palms and palaver of the multitude. She
never grew used to it. But Mrs. Pleasance is the
typical plump, pleasant gad-about; and that’s how
she gets over so much ground and is so buoyant
after a day’s drag round to shows, meetings, and
conferences.
“ The Mayoral Pleasances |have fixed their chil-
dren’s fancy-dress hop for the second of June.
Close on two thousand are being invited. Mrs.
W. Greene is to supervise the special dances. The
idea is taken from Mansion House customs, don’t
you know ! Melbourne is coming on.
“ More Osbornes for this State ! Prof. Osborne,
who has stepped into the shoes of Prof. Martin
up at the ‘Shop,’ has a youthful phiz and an
elderly brain, ’tis said. So has the other don,
Prof. Skeats. There seems to be quite a number
of scholastic and scientific authorities of young
and innocent appearance about Victoria. Prof.
Harrison Moore is a leading example. Dr. Svme,
the little man with the big saw-skill, is another.
Headmaster Blanche, of C. of E. Grammar School,
is a third. Also, one of the wisest and most expe-
rienced clergymen of Melbourne has a round,
frank, unknowing face, and looks positively just
twelve.
“The most fetching member of a Concert Co.
only recently landed on Western soil became en-
gaged to an old flame on the voyage out. The
man who tells of the romantic happening travelled
on the same ocean-rocker, and says a meeting of
the kind on board ship is fatal. There’s no escape
for either. The disposition to follow the easiest
line of resistance settles on one shortly after
leaving Suez, and thereafter demoralisation (in a
sense) advances steadily. One sleeps through the
sunny noon, or lolls with a whisky or the girl
close at hand, and nothing matters. It is the
place, wound up the man, for estranged couples to
patch up the old tears in their affection. What
a groat idea for a philanthropist to work on ! He
could collect all the disunited, the unhappy pairs,
together, pack them on board (a sailing ship,
for preference), and leave them to come to amic-
able terms. The notion deserves consideration.
It has greater possibilities than Swift’s Hospital
for Incurable Bores, Fools, and Liars.
“ The Alston Wallaces are forward with a motor-
brougham—the first of the kind in this State.
The young people who benefited in the large for-
tune left by John 0. Wallace are spending it with
a splash. The two brothers built the most expen-
sive yacht to fly a pennant in Hobson’s Bay of
late years. Both had motors. One built a Toorak
mansion. Now comes the motor-brougham. The
two wives do their share of spending cash—but
entirely on clothes.
“Dr. Jones—the imported medico for the Mad —
is youngish, musical, and, for all that people know
to the contrary, unmarried. He’s an acquisition.
Already he has been entertaining and singing
serenades (Schubert’s in particular) to his assorted
guests. He is housed at the Grand.
“ There’s a bunch of Bananalanders at Menzies’
just now finding this southern hub disgustingly
cold. The smartest of these is Mrs. E. J. Met-
calfe —the Toowoomba dame. The last very
interesting person from the land of sugar-cane was
a grass-widower, who went at a hot pace in these
quarters, and spent as much time and money on
his fair friends as a whisky agent.
- “The change wrought in the style, manner and
address of that clerical Apollo Rev. E. S.
Hughes—by travel and matrimony is one that
astonishes most of his old worshippers. He still
wears an inverted, soup-plate kind of hat, but his
eyes are dancing in a regular two-step manner,
his mouth is in a constant stretch of humorous
smile, his face is luminous, and his walk, talk and
manner jaunty. But I wonder will the girl
parishioners like the rollicking shepherd whom
they used to call ‘ Father ’ Hughes and resort to
as their confessor in time of trouble.
“The precocious child pianiste—Miss Rita
Hope—made music for a few appropriate souls on
Wednesday, at the Alexandra Club. Miss E.
Goode (a Marshall-Hall emissary) was there with
the offer of wide glad smiles and a brisk hand-
grasp to greet the numbed arrivals. It was a
drab day, with lots of moisture in the atmosphere.
And Miss Rita Hope—clever as she is—has not
the piano art of raising the temperature any. Her
three gems from Chopin, Beethoven, and the
time-honored Gluck were fingered faultlessly and
with ease, yet they lacked the flame-play of soul.
But little Rita is just in her teens, and the biggest
prize-taker for her years in the State. Also, she
is gazelle-eyed and modest. Under the circum-
stances, her concert on the 30th should draw.
“ There’s a novel glove-cleaning chalk out, and
Collins-street girls are busy with it. Light
gloves acquire a look ot general smudge early in
their career, and dainty misses find the new glove-
chalk an easy and effective remedy. The other
day in Mueller’s quite half-a-dozen girls were dust-
ing their gloves over, preparatory to joining
tea-parties. But how the feminine accoutrement
spreads ! _ Presently we’ll be carrying on our per-
sons, besides powder and puff and fringe-pins,
palm mirror and ’kerchief, his last billet doux,
tram tickets, a book, small change, glove and boot
polish, a change of complexions, sweets, and a few
hairpins. And all without a pocket !
“By the way, among girls the mo. is not so
much in favor of late years. The kiss that tickles
has had its day. Girls seem to prefer the idea of
a lip that conveys a soft feminine itripression with
a hard masculifie earnestness.
“The Stonington petrol-car driver has been
once more fined for going the pace that kills.
The imported servant complains bitterly of
injustice and persecution by the Force. But the
local motor-pushers of Mr. W. Kelly and Dr.
Officer have been fined as often, and there’s no
police prejudice alleged by them. Tram em-
ployees, fated to travel in snail-fashion and see
the fliers on the road flash past, are the principal
witnesses for the Crown in all these motor cases.
The thing is to moderate speed on the tram track,
and go full-petrol ahead where only meditative
cows inhabit the view, and there’s no law in
sight.
“ The marriage of Mr. Albert Gran with Mrs.
Nicholas is the one romantic incident of a chilly
week. On Saturday the pair quietly went-off and
had the knot tied. The widow has lost most of
her fortune, and the match is undeniably one of
love. The lady has three large sons. Conse-
quently the Alexandra Club and most other smart
sets declare it is absolutely touching to know that
a love match is still possible.
“Mr. John Cooke, the foreign-meat authority
who has been opening depots for tinned bunny and
chop in aspic all over the globe, has just returned
from his business gad. He had seven months’
residence in Buenos Ayres—the Spanish centre of
South America—and tells astonishing; stories of
life and fashion there. A race meeting in that
city, says Mr. Cook, is the most gorgeous sight
in the world a grand opera first night, a
a scene of dazzling brilliancy. The dress and
beauty of the carefully-protected women are of an
amazing order.
“ A whole host of Melbourne women broke out
into lip or cheek or ear eruption last week, owing
to the alleged bite of a peculiar May-fly. The
Mayoress was a sufferer, so was Mrs. Marsden,
wife of the Government Architect, and ever so
many others. .Some said it was mosquitoes, others
had a theory that they were suddenly going bad in
spots, and were waiting to see the blister turning
green. All were in a mortal funk till one and then
the other slowly got better.
“ The farce of the She Political League inviting
the Falstaffian Bent to address its meeting the
other night proves that the political agitatress has
no sense of the eternal fitness of things.. In the
first place, Tummy Bent doesn’t believe in female
suffrage. Secondly, he is not the sort of ideal
stained-glass St. Thomas to pose as an oracle to
the sisterhood who craves for the ‘ good ’ politi-
cian. The absurdity of the wobbling Premier
talking the higher, better, and more aesthetic
politics to the league became promptly evident.
The interjectors raised the Bent spleen, and he
exploded at them in vulgar speech. The scene
was disorderly. Before the meeting was half oyer
all the feminine toupees were up on end in indig-
nation, and the Purity Leaguers prayed for a fall of
cotton to pack into their offended ears.
“There’s a new way of the sex escaping cus-
tody—stepping out of its clothes, and leaving the
Force to take its wearing-apparel into, custody on
its own. On a recent Monday a policeman had
a violent man in his grip on one side, and a
wrangling woman on the other. She unhooked
her clothes, slid out of them, leaving a pile of
garments in the Law’s fist, and flew on the wings
of her liberty.
“The Indian prince, or half-prince, whom the
sentimental Miss has been romancing about, turns
out to be a dark-complexioned Yank who travels
with a case of merchandise samples at hand. All
the stories arose out of one hysterical girl’s yarn
of an encounter with a dusky prince at a swell
hostelry. The Yank looked noble and aloof and
brown enough for the romantic Sullap, Mid he has
been pointed out as that person t v sr since.
“ A dame who had been preparing a political
speech for six months, and had taken elocution
lessons for extra fascination, failed to appear on the
night advertised for her attack. She probably
took fright at the last moment, having trained
herself too fine for the ordeal.
“ A pair of golfers who had gone the rounds of
the Yic. links as a mixed couple for two or three
seasons made the tie a permanent one last month,
and, true to the sport, went off on a golfing honey-
moon, probably minus the caddy.
“Mrs. Scanlon (of the Slattery case), with her
two daughters, is at present in Bendigo. Her
two daughters are likely to loom importantly as
heiresses in the eye of that centre. ”

The American papers are already discussing
whether it will pay the States better to fortify the
Philippines and to maintain a large fleet in Asiatic
waters, or to sell out to Japan.

MARTYR TO NEURALGIA.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills Cure All Blood Diseases.
“ WAS such a nervous wreck that I could
not stand the least noise, ” said Mrs. Mar-
JL garet Purkis, of Thorn and Forsjth-streets,
Wagga Wagga, N.S.W. “If the children
even walked across the floor my head thumped
until I thought it would break.”
Fortunately there are few women who have suf-
fered so dreadfully from neuralgia as Mrs. Purkis,
of Wagga. Usually the sufferer feels now and
again a few sharp stabs of agony or nagging
twinges at tender, irritable nerves. Even in
those common cases neuralgia is serious, because
it shows that the blood is poor and the system
run down. The nerves give no trouble when
the blood is pure, rich and red. Nervousness,
neuralgia, sciatica, hysteria, and St. Vitus’ dance
appear only when the nerves are starved by weak
and watery blood, or irritated by blood that is im-
pure and poisonous. Set your blood right with
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and you
will strike at the one cause of all these nerve com-
plaints. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills do one thing
only, but they do it well—they actually make new
blood. That is the sole and simple secret of their
success in curing all nervous disorders, from sleep-
lessness to locomotor ataxia. No one can doubt
their value after reading these words of Mrs.
Purkis: —
“The sharp piercing pain seemed to stab my
brain, and then run to every nerve in my body,”
went on Mrs. Purkis. “ The attacks used to
come on periodically, and I dreaded them like
death. My whole life was miserable. Every day
I grew thinner, weaker, and more nervous. I
could neither eat nor sleep. My health seemed
broken up in every way. Of course, my blood
was to blame for it all. I tried all kinds of medi-
cines, but nothing was of any use. Ordinary
treatment failed, even to ease the pains, and my
case baffled everyone. In fact, I found that
after years of martyrdom, the only thing that
could cure me was Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People.
“I got the genuine kind,” Mrs. Purkis con-
tinued, “ and after the first box or two I felt them
doing me a little good. Gradually my blood grew
stronger, and I could feel it racing through my
veins. I gained rapidly in strength, appetite, and
color. Soon the new blood soothed and steadied
my nerves, and I have never had a twinge of
neuralgia since. lam cured for good, and I owe
my splendid health tc*day to the great blood-
building nerve tonic, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People.”
Mr. Purkis’ case is only one of hundreds;.
Among your own neighbors you will find cases
just as wonderful in which Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills have struck at the root of other blood
diseases like anaemia, indigestion, rheumatism,
sciatica, backache, kidney disease, liver com-
plaint, headaches, and the special secret suf-
ferings of growing girls and women when the
regularity of their blood supply is deranged.
Remember Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills do not act
on the bowels. They do one thing only, but
they do it well—they actually make new blood.
If you are not sure whether your special trouble is
caused by bad blood or not, write for free medical
advice to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Syd-
ney. From the same address you can order the
genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills by mail —
2s. 9d. a box; six boxes 15s. 3d., post free.

will stick to Maoriland, and has no intention of
becoming that State's High Commissioner in
London.
It seems that Dr. Muecke, Ada Crbssley’s
husband, is a throat-specialist. A handy thing
for a lady vocalist to have in the house.

Commissioner is editor Reay, of the Melbourne
Herald. Presumably on account of the close
connection of the force with journalism.

of Toorak, Kew, and St. Kilda, explain now in
public that they have to vote for “the likes of

The Presbyterian Church in Victoria has
enriched itself spiritually by ordaining brother
Tong Way as a Minister. The new Presbyterian
mrson has a full-blooded Chinese wife, whom he
imported to the vineyard from Canton.
Died at Perth (W.A.) the other day, aged
62, Maitland Brown, once leader of the elected
members in the old W.A. Legislative Council,
and afterwards Government Resident at Gerald -
ton —the author of the “My God, I am proud of
you ” wire which Bigjohn received what time he
retired in great disorder from the then excited
goldfields at the point of an umbrella.
Chief Justice Way has grown suddenly
modest, and he declines to allow his social engage-
ments to be published in the Press, because “he is
only Acting-Governor and not Lieutenant-Gover-
nor,” during Gov. Le Hunte’s absence among
the Chinamen at Port Darwin.
There is a set of four brothers in Maoriland
who are, respectively, dentist, doctor, parson, and
undertaker.
Mr. Charles Griffiths, retired superin-
tendent of the N.S.W. Government printing
office, was in the public service 53 years. Ha had
served under five Government Printers.
Mr. Justice Real, of Q., years ago a car-
penter at Ipswich, was reminded recently of his
early struggles by the discovery, during the demo-
lition of an old house, of some of his tools.

.000 sovs., which he promptly donated to help
long country churches and curates. Dr. Harmer
■ent to school with the English Premier.

rwed with Cambridge against Oxford in his Uni.
da's, has not lost his enthusiasm for the pastime.

n a skiff.

u-ual brand of politician uses his influence to get

SUPERINTENDENT BOOKER, head of
S.A.’s Fire Brigades.
Christian Paech, dead, aged 84, the other
day, was one of several German refugees who
came to S. Australia in the emigrant ship Zebra
in the 40’s, and settled in the Hahndorf district.
He received the Government bonus as discoverer
of the old Kamnantoo copper mine.
H. Engelbrecht, the last of the Boer
prisoners at Ceylon, is now living at Hambantota,
denied permission to return to S’Africa because
he refuses to swear allegiance to Britain. He
says—“ I know myself, and could never be true
to the King. How can I make myself a false
man?’
Burnt lately, in the Learmonth (Vic.) dis-

Roach, farmer, and once the residence of “Big’
Clarke, father of the late William and Joseph
Clarke, and grandfather of Rupert. It was some-
what different from the present mansions of
Rupertswood and Cliveden.
Bishop Green, at Ballarat (2/5/’O5): —
We would rather see our dear land of the Southern
Cross ruled by Christian aliens than by heathen white
men.
That is doubtless the strictly orthodox religious
point of view." And if he is logical, the good
bishop should also prefer to give his daughter (if
he has one) in marriage to a converted Chow or
nigger rather than to a white Australian who
doesn’t subscribe to a church platform. But
would he ?
“What about the mace ?” was the ques-
tion hurled at Bent the other night when he was
slangwhanging a Melbourne meeting on behalf of
the Women’s National League. Bent is open to
so many pertinent interjections that it would
be a pity to give him any further chances to
score off a misplaced query. He wasn’t the Vic.
Speaker whose mace scandalously disappeared
from the House. The missing mace was Matthew
Davies’ trouble.
THE PARTICULARITY OF CHILDHOOD.
“ What are you doing , my dear?”
“I'm sending a letter to auntie, mother .”
“ Oh! And what are you telling her?”
“I’m not saying anything, I’m just showing her my
writing.”
W. A. Archer’s system of registration for
legal and statistical purposes, put into operation in
Victoria in 1853, was the first Australian attempt
at scientific statistical compilation. The Regis-
trar-General of Engl ni l published his schedules
as models, and c mfinental countries adopted
many of his arrangements.
Maoriland has a population of 903, G7G
(including 43,143 Maoris and 232 J Chinese).
Figures produced by the Wellington Law .Society
show that there are 710 practising solicitors in the
islands (an increase of 49 since last year). This
works out at one solicitor to every 1258 inhabi-
tant or one to every 317 male adults.
Next month the synagogue, Sydney,
will receive its long-expected chief minister—
Rabbi L. F. Cohen. Mr. Cohen was selected
nearly a year and a half ago, but he remained in
England to obtain by examination a diploma in
special rabbinical subjects. He will be the first
fully qualified Jewish minister to officiate in
N.S.W.
The average travelling evangelist has the
assertiveness of a bull-ant, the enterprise and push
of a commercial traveller, the sincerity of a
tortoiseshell cat, the humor of a clown, the
manners of a larrikin, the conscience of a pick-
pocket, and the humility of a peacock. Probably
he has other good qualities, but he hides them
under his bushel of chaff.
Died, at her residence near Glenelg (S.A.),
on May 6th, Mrs. Morphett, aged 91 years.
Deceased was a daughter of James Hurtle Fisher,
the first mayor of Adelaide, and with her father
came to S. A. in 1836. She was an intimate friend
of Colonel Light, for whom she often acted as
amanuensis. She married late John Morphett in
1838, and has lived near Glenelg since 1842.
Yarn told at the Q. Anglican Conference
by a backblocks parson—a member of the Bush
Brotherhood. An archdeacon was obliged to
leave his handbag on the bank of a flooded
river to swim across. It contained his vest-
ments. Not to be beaten, he appealed to the
publican’s wife for a skirt, and wore it round
‘his shoulders. The congregation had no idea that
it was not his surplice.

Hughes (he was the predecessor of George Howe).
Anglican parson, Richard Johnson. First “ man ” hanged,
James Barret, aged 17, a prisoner for life, described as a
“ terrible villain.” His crime in the colony was stealing
a small quantity of food. He perished six weeks after the
foundation of the country.
W'hen “General” Booth arrived in
Sydney, he wasn’t driven to the Salvarmy’s
“ People’s Palace ” and given a cup of dreadful
tea and a threepenny doss. The profit from that
doleful doss-house helped to house him in sump-
tuous chambers, and provide him with something
warm. Nor were the dead-beats, upon whose
class Booth has made himself a mighty capitalist,
given a free feed and doss in honor of his arrival.
It is the profits wrung from these poorest of the
poor that provide the sinews of war for the
Salvarmy officials.
Queensland’s first Premier,
It jbert Herbert, who died the other day
in the Cold Country, came out as Pri-
vate Secretary to the first Governor
under responsible government in 1859.
George Bowen, under orders from
Downing-street, immediately instructed
him to form a Cabinet, although an in-
experienced young man of 25. There
was general public revolt at this Im-
perial impudence, but, the franchise
being limited, there was sufficient of
the imported influence to throttle the
opposition movement. The result was
that Herbert was able to hold the
post for six years, but there were many
changes in the personnel of the Cabinet.
In 1866 the imported Premier went out
of office. But the succeeding Macalister
Govt, lasted but a few months, and
Herbert again assumed office. He was
only a few days in power when Macalis-
ter was given another chance. Herbert
then returned to England.
Even Peacock’s laugh, rarely
heard these times, has its uses. He was
with a party near Beaufort (Vic.), the
other day, taking a trip through the
forest in connection with the timber
royalty business. Oman, M.L.A., was
also of the party ; and a pub. being-
reached, he suggested that as it was in
the Kookaburra’s constituency he ought
to shout. They all entered, but no one
was to be found. After waiting a while,
Peacock played barman. He had
served his friends and was drawing a
“long-’un” for himself, when a large
female burst in with assault in her eye
and battery in her arm. Anything
might have happened if Peacock
hadn’t broken out into his screech. “Why,”
said the mollified missus, bowing—“it’s Mr.
Peacock, ain’t it?” And they bad to have
another.
Dan. O’Connor, the picturesque patriarch
of N.S.W. politics, who has cleft the classics
with his tongue these many years, has decided to
visit Ireland, and incidentally England and the
rest.of Europe, after an absence of 50 years from
native Tipperary. He goes in search of a pub-
lisher for the book of his life, and will be adjacent
when the Imperial general elections are on. It is
not improbable that he will allow hi rnself to be
nominated for a seat in the House of Commons as
a member of the Irish Nationalist party. Dan.
O’Connor in the historic British Parliament would
certainly mean a sensation. Nothing like Dan.
has -ever been heard outside Australia.
When Alec Morrison, recently-deceased
musical critic and all-round journalist, first went
to London, he called on the editor of the Daily .
Telegraph, and, presenting a mass of influential
letters of introduction, offered his services as an
expert on Australian matters. “Yes, Mr. Mor-
rison.” said the editor, “ I have no doubt that you
are very well qualified to speak with authority on
Australian affairs, and to write up your copy
attractively. But really, England doesn’t care a
ha’penny about Australia or anything that hap-
pens there. Consequently, I don’t think there
will be much scope for your ability as far as we
are concerned.” Morrison thus learned in two
minutes wbat it will take Australia years to learn.

Mr. Hugh R. Reid, the brother of Yes-
No, is fond of telling how he and Tommy Bent
wielded the willow together in a Melbourne
cricket club. At the opening of the club’s
ground, the first ball was bowled by George
Turner, Bent wielding the bat. The ball struck
Thomas on his Tory policy ; at the second, he
made a wild swipe, but did not succeed in holding
on to his bat as tightly as he does to office, as it
nearly killed a constituent near by.
A contributor to a recent Melbourne
Argus says that Andersoi Inlet was named
after a south-west Gippsland squatter. The
discoverer, in ’39, was the late Samuel Anderson,
of Western port, who died in ’62, aged 60. In
early life he was a member of the staff of London
Times. Samuel Anderson also discovered the
Tarwin river, mountain limestone at Cape Lip-
trap, and coal at Cape Paterson.
Of the N.S.W. politicians who participated
in the three Conventions which led up to Federa-
tion W. iVl'Millan is the sole survivor. He
appeared with Parlies in Melbourne in 1890, with
Parlies, Abbott, Dibbs, W. H. Suttor, Barton and
Jennings in Sydney in 1891, and with nine, who
still survive, in Adelaide in 1897. Of the seven
N.S.W. men who sat in the Convention of 1891
only two are alive ; the ten who sat in 1897 are all
on deck.

All Australians are not born tired. A.
sample of splendid energy is the correspondent
who writes from North Queensland :
Neither of my parents could read or write, and they had
a dead set against those who could. They brought me-
up in total ignorance. When I should have been going
to school I was acting as scarecrow to keep the cockatoos
off the wheat, and when I reached 12 years of age I was
promoted to the pig-minding industry. At this time I met a
neighbor’s boy, who taught me the alphabet—all he knew
himself. The following year we went to the Lachlan district.
At this time bushrangers were very rife all over the South-
ern part of N.S.W., and everybody was anxious to hear
of their doings, but not two men out of every ten could
read the papers. Those who could, used to read out the
news to a little crowd of people who stood or sat around
with their mouths open at the doings of Johnny O’Malley,
Dunn, or Gilbert. Gardiner was captured at this time.
Also, Bertrand, the murder of Kinder, was just then on
his trial, which made me very anxious to learn to read.
We hadn’t a book of any kind except a prayer-book, and
I often stole away to an old shepherd to get a lesson. He
had no books either, but he taught me to spell words of
three letters. Next, an old travelling schoolmaster, who
stopped at our place one night, made me a present of an
ABC book which I had to keep concealed, as my father
would have flogged me and confiscated the book if he
had seen it. With the aid of an old knock-about hand I
mastered it, and one day I found “ Sinbad the Sailor "
and an old torn copy of “Robinson Crusoe,” and these I
read and read until I wore them out. After that I could
struggle through the newspapers, and after 12 months’
practice I ventured to read-out in public. A neighboring
squatter’s wife offered to take me and pay for my educa-
tion, but my father told her that I was becoming useful
to him now and he could not spare me. The fact was
that he was prejudiced against education, and thus he
lost me the chance of my life. The following year the
Weddin Mountain rush took place, and I helped to take a
mob of cattle there. I became fascinated with the life,
and I returned to it again after going home. I was very
lucky on the One-Mile and the Quandong, where I worked
all day and studied half the night. When I was leaving
Grenfell I sold £6O worth of books for £5. Since those
days I have read Plutarch, Homer, Shakspeare, Byron,
and the history of nearly all the world. I have studied
the first four books of Euclid, and read nearly all the stan-
dard novels, both ancient and modern ; and now I am
going to try and learn to write articles.
Seems as if the motor ’bus would solve the
Adelaide tram business. Why doesn’t Adelaide
Council promptly set going a motor ’bus service ?
“ Red hair is either loved or loathed. Its
admirers are almost hysterically enthusiastic, and
the artists call it golden, though it is not truly of
that shade. A red-haired woman is always sure of
attention, no matter how plain her face or insig-
nificant her figure. For those who dislike red
hair say it is a sign of bad temper, or of immor-
ality, or of both. So they will watch the red-
haired woman in order to convict her.” — Helen
Forbes.

Aboriginalities.
“ Lak ” : Re strange meats. In New
Guinea once, out of curiosity, I tried infant croco-
dile. Resembled chicken in appearance and taste,
but was a trifle tough. The natives eat the adult
“ croc.”—impossible to a white man’s “ tummy.”

MRS. MARGERINE (after a few neighborly
words): “An’ I’d have yer know, Mrs.
Mangles, that me husband is so well thought
on as he takes the plate round in church.”

of it, mum. Which you wouldn’t be able to

“M. 8.” : “P.M.’s” amateur vet. friend
(B. 27/4/’O5) is wrong about the warts on the inner
side of horse’s legs being scars of pre-natal “ liga-
tures” (ligaments he means). Scars contract as
they grow older, but these excrescences are con-
tinually growing and being shed. They are com-
posed of the secretion of a modified scent gland,
which has a strong musky odor. Somewhat simi-
lar glands are seen on the leg of the pig.
“Stranger” : Those warts on inner side
of horses’legs B. 27/4/’O5) are vestigial
remnants of the great toe which was present in the
protohippus—the palaeontological ancestor of the
horse. The hoof of the present-day horse is the
specialised middle toe of the ancestor aforesaid.
“ Gymp ” : Re “P.M.’s” inquiry about
warts on the inner side of horses legs. These are
what are called “caps,” and are joined at the
roots to two tubes, which draw the oil to and fro
through the shin bone. If these scales are cut or
torn, and not looked after, weakness of the knee-
joint will follow.
* # *
“ D.R.” : Re that snake-bitten filly
(“P.Q.F.,” B. 4/5/’O5). It is remarkable that
nearly all domesticated animals, when moribund,
seek human company. In Walgett (N.S.W.), not
long since, there was great mortality amongst
horses, and almost every afflicted animal wandered
into the town and tried to get into dwellings.
Three or four had to be shot by the police. Dogs,
too, when poisoned, always come home to die, and
a kangaroo-hunter’s dog that gets mortally
wounded in battle generally crawls to his master’s
camp before he surrenders the ghost.
“ W.M.F.”: Re snake-bitten filly (“ P. Q. F.,
4/5/’O5). Once knew a horse staked in the fetlock
and suffering from tetanus take possession of a
tent, frightening the women and children who
occupied it, almost to death. Getting no help
there, he raced a quarter-mile to a hut. Found no
one there, he did a half-mile to a corner of the
paddock, whence he could see the homestead.
Still getting no aid —poor brute was past it—
started back for the tent, and died on the way.
Have also known sick sheep and other animals
enter houses. Fact is, many sick animals instinc-
tively turn to man for aid.

When the police are carting niggers long
distances in Kimberley (W.A.), they feed them
on flour-and-water, boiled and flavored with black
pepper. This pleasing mixture is known as “Hell-
in-a-billy.”
* • * *
“ S.M.W.” : Once saw an emu, when
threatened by an eagle, throw itself on its back,
and remain in that effective kicking posture until it
thoughtit safe to sprint a bit further towards shelter.
As more eagles soon came along the alternate sprint
and drop became very quick and lively, but the
emu eventually reached the shelter of the scrub.
Another time I saw an emu caught by a kangaroo-
dog, which ran alongside and grabbed it by the
neck. Nothing so utterly helpless as that emu
was ever seen —it stood perfectly still while the
dog tore its neck. Almost looked as if the poor
bird was enjoying the treatment.

All its sorrows, pleasures, cares,
Useless suffering, why endure ?

“Prooshan”
on aboriginal
trial by com-
bat :
Forty - eight
years ago a fine
young black of
the Tappitilla
tribe named Sam
Condywight
worked for a
farmer named
Livingstone at
Broughton Creek
(Berry, N.S. W.),
where old King
Tappatilla flaun-
ted his cherished
copper crescent
of royalty. Sam
stood 6ft., and
was a fine, manly
fellow. He and
one Billy Dixon, a
half - caste, were
rivals, and Julia,
the dusky object
of their affec-
tions, surren-
dered to the
streak of pipeclay
in Billy. One day
Sam was inform-
ed in the potato
field that Julia
and Billy had
bolted. Bending
the welkin with
a yell, Sam sprang
off to hisgunyah,
and, arming him-
self with spear,
nulla, and shield,
he rushed after
the absconders,
who, however,
eluded him.
Next day he re-
turned, looking,
as the boys
thought, fit to
leave alone. In a
week he jibbed
on work and went
off to Jamberoo
on a “jamboree.”
A few days later
Dixon returned from
his “ honeycomb,” bor-
rowed a huge horse-pistol, and
proceeded in the direction of Jam-
beroo also. In a week’s time Sam’s dead
body was found behind a log near the Seven Mile
Bsaoh, and although the authorities took no action,
Sxm’s relatives took Billy, and he was tried in abori-
ginal fashion and taken to the scene of the murder,
where he was bound by the waist to a post. A
shield was allowed him, and then two of the deceased’s
male relatives were informed that they might throw seven
spears at him. If he withstood all seven he was adjudged
innocent, and, upon his receiving the first wound, was to
b 3 set free. Two of Oondywight’s sisters were allowed to
lie at accused’s feet and' rap his shins with yam sticks
whenever a spear was hurled, to divert his attention.
The spears were thrown with accuracy, but Dixon stopped
four. Then Sam’s sisters hit his shins extra hard, and
the fifth spear pierced his shoulder. After that the
ordeal was over. Dixon recovered and bore his Julia
beyond the reach of the Condywights.

‘ ‘Pag”: Be water-finding (“Tip,” B. 4/5/’O5),
I know a place on the Barwon where thousands of
pounds have been sunk in wells, which at most
have yielded a bucketful or two of salt stuff. A
water-finder went to work with a twig and traced
a line for miles, and wells sunk anywhere along
this give good water. Also, at a place on the
Upper Hunter, wells had persistently failed ; but
on the owner following the directions of a water-
finder, he sank 80ft. (last 20 through solid rock)
and struck a splendid supply.

B. 4/5/’O5) : Had a stiff job once trying to prevent

loaded with wool-packs, was crossing some flooded
§ round, and got bogged. The shatter—a vicious

“ beat me ” for his head. Every time his napper
was yanked up out of the water —once or twice
when he was pretty far gone—he would grunt

“ C.X.Gr.” : Bill had seen a jibbing buggy-
horse induced to pull by fastening his tail to the
vehicle; so next time he w r ent out for a load of
wood, when the old horse stopped on the home-
ward journey, Bill hitched him on by the tail to
the dray. But, unfortunately, he didn’t take into
account the difference in weight between a buggy
and a heavy dray with a load of wood on. The
old horse pulled his best, and after an heroic
struggle, the cable parted ; unfortunately at the
horse end. Next day Bill had an opportunity to
sell his horse, but when he produced the poor old
moke minus a tail, the deal was off, the buyer re-
marking that he did not mind a cob, “ but
this was cutting it a bit too fine.”

“H. J.L.” gives an interesting bit of
personal experience:—
It was when I was beche-de-mer gathering at Flinders
Island (N.Q.). An aboriginal young man wanted a wife,
and he interviewed the chosen one’s father, who put on
full war paint. The prospective bridegroom, also fully
armed, stood facing him, about 200 ft. off, and seemed to
poke borak at future pa-in-law, who pretended to get
wild, and suddenly launched at him a fighting spear
(stingaree-tipped), which the aspirant smartly parried
with spear and woomera. Other members of the family
then had a shy at him, in vain, and one of the nigs told
me that “ bin draw-em blood, no more catchem Mary"
(betrothal busted). Next ceremony took place at night.
Bride lying quiet behind a screen, in front of which
were posted two bodies of aborigines in full corroboree
costume—bride and bridegroom’s respective relatives—
who were going through some awful contortions, when
suddenly out of the darkness rushed the bridegroom and
his brother and seized the bride, who fought like a fiend,
using nails and teeth freely on carcase of proposed loving
husband, whilst the united camp howled like Hell let
loose, and the corroboree crowd engaged in tierce mimic
warfare till mother-in-law appeared, armed with a healthy
waddy. Son-in-law (a fine powerful nigger) meekly bent
his head while 111 a smote him a real “ deather,” making
the blood stream from his pate. But he merely swayed,
shook his head, and took two more awful swipes from
bride’s sisters. Then he and his brother gave a trium-
phant yell, and fled into the night with the bride. Next
morning I asked the n gs why the bridegroom let ma-in-
law smash him without retaliation, and they said,
“Sposem bin get wild and fight, no more catchem Mary."
“L.H.” : Mound-builders do not directly
assist their chicks to escape from the mound after
hatching, but the parent birds keep near the nest
during the incubation and daily scratch over the
surface of the hotbed of rotten vegetation to pre-
vent it caking. This admits the air necessary to
incubation, and also renders the hatched chicks’
egress easy. The experiment has been made of
covering a nest with wire-netting, to prevent
access of the parents, and the result was—no
chickens.
“ J.T.8.” : No need to go so far afield as
some Bulletin correspondents have travelled in
search of the derivation of the term “barracker.”
I well remember its origin in the angry application
of the designation by one footballer to another,
who belonged to a rough lot that used to play on
the waste ground behind the Victoria Barracks,
St. Kilda road, Melbourne. The word cannot be
found in any football report before that time.
“M.Gr.” : My old father
pioneered the Wimmera 50
years ago, and tells a tale of
native cats in millions. They
came up out of the cracks in
the earth, they fell into the
house water-casks in the night,
and crawled out in the morn-
ing over the dead bodies of
drowned companions. Twice
the old man hung his mutton
(a whole sheep) under his
verandah to cool, and found in
the morning bones (the eats
having climbed up the veran-
dah posts, and sprung from
them to the carcase). Out
of a waterhole at the end of
the house he took one morning
ten cats, next day thirteen,
next day nine, and so on,
while the plague lasted. They
ate everything before them,
and the forward ones starved
the hinder right out, so that
the smell of “dead cat ” be-
came almost unendurable.
Teams hardly dared camp, as
the cats would be up on the
loads before the horses were
unharnessed, tearing into
everything eatable their claws
could reach, and eating every-
thing not in cases or tins.
Coming down from the home-
stead once my father stopped
at the men’s hut. The cook
was sitting in the doorway
with a big milk-dish full of
chopped suet on his knee.
“See me feed my chickens,”
he said as he scattered the
mince about. Before the dish
was empty the cats were
swarming. The earth seemed
to open and vomit cats. There
were hundreds dead in the
morning. The suet had been mixed with arsenic.

“ R.” :Be star-seeing by daylight. No
mention made hitherto in B. of seeing stars from
the bottom of a 90-foot well. Does the well-shaft
act as a kind of telescope, or a sight-concentrator.
[The well-shaft simply shuts off nearly all the
daylight, which keeps the stars invisible.]
A WORD FOR THE COCKATOO!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now, you boundary-riding rhymesters, working poets of
the station,
And you droving, shearing singers of the road and of
romance,
Won’t you let your glance indifferent wander where that
cultivation
Breaks the deadness of your paddocks ? Give the cockatoo
a chance!
When you sing of breaking bullocks, brumby-chasing in
the ranges ;
When you stock-horse, range, and paddocks with such
memories endow —
Won't you spare a little notice for the man who’s making
changes
On your timber-covered hillsides with the horse that
draws the plough ?
Early morning—before daybreak, ere the jovial kooka-
burra
Wakes the stillness of the night-time with his, cookie-
calling cries—
Sees the draught and cuddy plough-team yoked and
standing in the furrow,
While the cockie eats his breakfast, waiting for the sun
to rise !
Midday smoke-oh 1 finds him spelling (in the garden
planting “praties”),
While his ill-matched pair of hat-racks gets its grass-and-
gum-leaves feed ;
And at night before the cockie goes to bed, however late,
he’s
Baking bread or boiling mutton to supply his next day’s
need.
Home, a bark-and-sapling humpy; logs for chairs and
slabs for table ;
Ceiling, just a piece of hessian ; bed, a hammock made of
sacks ;
Walls rough-papered with the Worker, or the Bully (if
he’s able —
Illustrations shed their brightness on a life that bright-
ness lacks);
Food an endless repetition—tea, rice, mutton, bread and
honey—
Same at breakfast, same at dinner, same at lunches, same
at tea ;
Nought to change the deadly dullness save when chance-
got ready money,
At the pub, oblivion offers in a drunken “ jamboree.”
Thus his daily round continues, lonely, toiling, monthly,
yearly.
In his timber-covered, bushfire-threatened, wilderness of
scrub,
Never troubled by events that shake the outer world, but
merely
Strike his ear as being mentioned by some “ skiter at the
pub ”;
Till the day comes when he’s missing from his usual
haunts; his neighbors
Note his absence from the shanty, or the howling of his
dog,
Take a ride to search his paddocks, find at last the
cookie’s labors,
Ended by the work he’d lived in—find him pinned
beneath a log.
Axe in hand and pipe half-empty, stretched beside his
work they find him,
With his body torn by dingoes and his eyes picked out by
crows ;
Not a soul to claim his kinship, not a mourner left behind
him —
Just a cockie killed bush-falling. How it happened no one
knows—
No one cares so very deeply. So he’s planted 'neath the
wattles
That had caused him many a swear-word in the struggling
years before ;
And they fence his grave with split-rails and a fringe of
empty bottles—
And the station buys the paddock to become the run once
more.
So, my boundary-riding poet, when, well-horsed, you
canter through the
Paddocks of the old selection, and the grave-rails come in
view,
Pay a passing tribute to the man who, given the horse,
could do the
“ Man from Snowy Biver ” business with the first and
best of you;
When you sing your virile verses of great horses and their
riders,
Of the racing, droving, station life that is Our Land’s
romance,
Try and spare a simple stanza for the cuddy-borne out-
siders,
Who are making homes from nothing—give the cockatoo
a chance!
VIC.

the stations
maintain small
gardens by dint
of having niggers
continually
douching the
soil. Not a gar-
den of any kind
is to be seen in
D e r b y—not a
f'o.ver or a fruit-
t ree. The only
tree that grows
v. ell is baobab,
which reaches
sometimes 25ft.
in girth, but th«
timber (or, ra-
ther, pith) is
quite useless. I
have seen a
W,i nchester
bu'let go right
through one, at
]OO yards, and
kill a horse on
the other side.
Nothing will
ever be grown
in Kimberley,
except with an
enormously ex-
pensive irrigation
scheme, and the
place is a Sheol
of malaria, white
ants, scorpions,
centipedes, mos- .
quitoes and sand-flies. Yet men continue to be
allured here by reports of high wages. The country
is essentially a cattle-country and nothing else ; and, cut-
up and improved, would carry a good population. Now
one man or a syndicate owns half a country-side, and one
beast runs to 1000 acres. The almost unvarying diet in this
wearv country is canned stuff, with a tew onions and
potatoes occasionally. Here a man 25 looks at least
49 and grey and baid-headed at that. Kven the childien
appear old-fashioned and worried. Amusements and
relaxation there are none, except drinking fiery whisky.
The heat from November till April is broiling , and,
altogether, there’s no place out of Gehenna to compare
with Kimberley.

“Eiddal”: “Kolonga” (B. 4/5/’O5) has
fallen down the wrong hole. Harvesters easily fill
85 bags daily, on even crop, round the Kyabrarna-
pootra fringe, and the bags are lifted off the
machine, and sewn , in the paddock. Only an
idiot would think of carting in unsewn bags of
grain. * * *
“ Vrai ” • Be ants dying to save their city
(“ Brandar,” B. 4/5/’O5). The case described was
probably merely a matter of being jammed m the
nest-entrance, but, for all that, indifference to in-
dividual safety in comparison with that of the
community is strongly characteristic of the
workers and the fighters of all the social hymo-
noptera.

“J.T.G.”: A notable feature of the
jackass character that I have not seen mentioned
is the calmness with which he endures the perti-
nacious “ chiacking” of scores of smaller bush-
birds. His expression on such occasions is a
capital study in philosophic indifference.
NOBLESSE OBLIGE.
CITY BARMAID (frigidly): “ We don't serve pints
of ale heah."
SWAGMAN (awed by his surroundings): “ Then
I'll take a quart.”
“F.R.W.” : A tame jackass which I had
for years, used to catch and eat sparrows. He had
one wing clipped, so he used to do the confidence-
trick by sidling gently up to a party of feeding
sparrows and then making a sudden dash at the
nearest. t .
“S.R.”: We boys respected the jackass
to the extent of never using a gun on him, but we
•practised freely on him with shanghais. But Jack
is a tough subject. With a new catapult and
leaden pellets I’ve slain a fair-sized hawk but
could do Jack no serious damage. When a dozen
or so of him would be ranged on a small branch,
holding an evening concert, we would send up a
pebble whizzing from a catapult. A sounding
whack from above, and over would go one of the
performers, drop a few feet, recover himself, and
fly to a distant tree. Then the ranks would close
up and the racket would start afresh; and this
would go on till the whole orchestra had been
shifted, one by one.
* * * *
“ M.L.” : In several M.L. dairying dis-
tricts I have dropped across the expression (used,
sav by a slighted lover), “She’s gone sour on
me.” Have wondered if it is of merely local
origin, and arises from the state of the milk after
a thunderstorm, or when it is troubled by senile
decay ?
The bullocky’s heaven must have bul-
locks in it, or there’ll be language. One of
him brought his whiskers into the township just
as the big mine had a clean up. Thebig director
was there—the man who owned about halt the
shares—and he passed while Wiljames was hold -
ingupa post at the pub. “Whiz, said James,
“if I had that bloke’s gilt, and a clippin team of
bullocks, by , l wouldn’t care for nobody !

“ Ikey B.” : Duck shooting on the Darling
once I ran out of shot, and, coming across a
shanty, I asked the landlady if she could sell me a
pound. She brought me a heaped-up pannikin of
]No. G and explained that she hadn t a lb.-weight,
but knew that the pannikin held exactly a pound,
as she always weighed out Hour that way to swag-
men. There must have been a good 81b. of shot,
fit’s a curious example of the evil having rou-
tine ” knowledge of facts. Fancy the paralysing
effect on that woman’s natural intelligence caused
by the acquisition of the fact that a pannikin
held a pound of Hour !]
“Manuka”: Re the “slow” growth of
forest trees. In ’7B a kauri was measured-ldlt.
high and llin. in circumference. In 1904 the tree
wa°s about 40ft. high, and 23in. in circumference.
A totara, measured at same times, had increased
in circumference from sin. to 18in.
When other lips and other hearts
With flattering vows allure.
Remember that vour truest friend
Is Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.
When frosty stars gleam overhead
And earth’s in wintry moods,
And cold attacks in throat and head,
Then you’ll remember Woods. "
“ Alec Z.” : Re red-backed spiders I
have seen no mention of the fact that within
24 hours the bitten person develops a heavy cola
sweat. In my own case I had to discard the
sheets (upon which the sweat left a yellowish
stain) and roll myself in a blanket. I have known
several cases of spider-bite, and, personally, 1
would as soon be bitten by a black snake as ny
this venomous little creature.
“D.H.R.”: To “San Remo” (B. 27/4/’O5).
There is no record of recovery from effects of bite
of a death-adder—Old or young. As to never
knowing a dog to live more than ten minutes attex
being bitten by a tiger snake, I have known dogs
to survive three hours, and some to recover. Mos
dogs when snake-bitten rush for the nearest water,
remain in it for a considerable time, and often re-
cover. A man I know had two dogs bitten by the
same snake. Both had an aversion to water but
he caught one, and tied him neck-deep to a log.
This one recovered, after three hours immersion,
but the other, which couldn’t be caught died.
The blacks say that a human being may thus re-
cover from any snake-bite, except that of brown
or black snakes or death-adder.
* * * * n
“ V. 8.” : On Newstead Station (Invereli
district), some years ago, the little daughter of a
selector was bitten when playing near the house*
The mother killed two death-adders at the spot
(one 2ft. Gin. long), and then sent for the father,
who was working some distance away. Horses
had to be caught and harnessed and the chiia
taken 1G miles to Invereli for medical treatment.
She recovered, and is now grown up.

“ Rollo ” • Re recovery after death-adder s
bite (“San Remo,” B. 24/4/’O5). I knew a man
(name herewith) who was in his youth bitten first
by a brown snake and then by a death-adder, y
recovered completely in #
“ j B ”• To “ San Remo’s ” inquiry about
death-adder bite (B. 27/4/’O5). Kangarooing near
Quirindi (N.S.W.), some years back my mate was
bitten on the third finger of his left hand by a full-
grown adder. He immediately placed the finger
over the muzzle of his rifle and blew it off. Walked
three miles to camp and drove lo to the doctor,
and next day was out of danger, owing, doubtless,
to prompt action.
* * * *
“H.J.H.”: I knew a one-armed Malay
who used to make a living turning over hawksbill
turtle on sandbanks on the N E. coast of Queens-
land. He had a 22ft. cutter, and his only mate was a
terrier, which he had trained to hang on
to light ropes. The dog used also to watch at
night for incoming turtle. Small ones he would
turn over himself, but when a big fellow came
along he would bring his master. I badly wanted
to steal that dog,, but the Malay was a lot too
handy with his knife.
JAMES A. CRISP, a young Sydney artist, who
is going to try his luck in America.

- Pocre’y ” : Shark’s Bay (W.A.) is divided
into two parts by Peron peninsula, m no place of
Greater width than 20 miles. On the Eastern
shore of the peninsula the shags, a species o
cormorant, build their nests in the low mangrove
trees and lay their eggs in April. On the
Western shore the same species of birds do not
build until August, and their nests are not in
trees but on the ground amongst the rocks
Seems rather extraordinary that the same kmd of
birds only 20 miles apart, should differ so con-
siderably in these particulars.

There was a time when the man who was going
• f ldintnfrnnhv either as a pastime or a means of
livelihood, was worried whether he should go to the Baker
and Rouse Proprietary, or to Kodak, Limited. Lach
establishment, had attractions that were to be found m no
other and were not common to both. Intending pur-
chasers M-e no longer called upon to wrestle with such a
problem? Kodak,'Limited, and the Baker and Rouse Pro-
urietarv Limited, are now amalgamated, and the best
features’of both establishments are to be found under the
same roofs. Two of the finest shops in Sydney and Mel-
bourne hold the combined firm’s large and vaned stoc ~
which includes such specialities as the goods of the
■Rochester Optical Co., the Century Camera Co., the Blau
Camera Co., the Seed Dry Plate Co., and many others.
Tn addition to importing the best foreign-made goods, the
firm runs a factory of its own at Abbotsford, in Victoria,
for the production of the favorite Pearl and other bromide
Papers and mounts of every description ; and the Aus-
tralian-made goods, fresh from the factory, can be relied
upon to give ssxtisfaction every time.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, udiether sent by post or handed m; and m
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.
