POLITICAL
POINTS
The dengue fever outbreak has frightened
Mr. G. H. Reid off his Queensland tour. There’s
a silver lining to every cloud.
From his latest speech, it would seem that
Mr. Deakin will not connive at the gross inter-
ference by the Reid Cabinet with the indepen-
dence of the High Court.
Westralian Government has decided to
re-cast the regulations dealing with the blacks
on the lines of Dr. Roth’s recommendations.
One of G. H. Reid’s metaphors : “I ob-
ject to a man putting himself in the position of a
machine and following a rule like a sheep-dog.”
That delightful old humorist, Melbourne
Argus
The enormous burden of a high tariff has not been able
to keep the United States back.
When the Argus sees smoke it knows better than
to guess fire ; it guesses a waterfall or an iceberg
straight off.
“Fool ” : Why immigration ? I can do 48
different kinds of work, but can’t get any. And
can’t get land either in Vic.
Premier Carruthers’ announced resolve to
stop the abuse for private pleasures of N.S.W.
Government steam launches counts to him for
righteousness. Some of the State steam launches
had become practically the private property of
various official persons.
“It is not Mr. Reid’s forte to say anything
very original, but what he does say is uttered
with the air of an oratorical Columbus.”—W. M.
Hughes.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers has, during
his Ministerial wanderings, .so far discovered
three Paradises, five Edens, and seven Gardens of
New South Wales. If he were also to be the
Columbus of a colonial Little Hades, it would
agreeably diversify the monotony of his slobber-
some eulogies.
THE GATHERING.
“I call upon the whole body of persons opposed to the
Socialistic Labor party to meet together against a com-
mon enemy.”—G. H. REID.
Significant sign of the trend of secular
opinion. The three candidates at the Wellington
(M.L.) by-election each answered “Nay” when
asked to support the Bible-in-schools movement.
Two (Hutchison and Fisher) were prohibitionists,
too.
Asked before N.S.W. Lands Commission if
he had ever seen the departmental papers iri~re
Sims’ leases, Mr. W. N. Willis replied : “ No, I
did not. I did not want to see them. What is
the good of papers to people who are starving?”
And the “starving people” in question had just
put up £6OO to try and get an easier title to 15,862
acres of land !
N.S.W. Parliament has been further pro-
rogued till June 13. What chance then of
dealing with the year’s estimates before the
expenditure is incurred?
Are there other odors about, only requir-
ing an opportunity to make themselves felt ? A
number of North Coast (N.S.W.) settlers want the
Justice Owen Lands Commission extended to the
forestry and mining departments.
‘ ‘ The wealth of this country can simulate
the pangs of poverty more successfully than even
the professional mendicant.”—G. H. Reid, in
N.S.W. Assembly (in defending his land taxa-
tion years ago). He doesn’t say things like that
now.
One of the Yankee trust tricks in trying to
cut out the local manufacturer is to buy a couple
of local machines at the full price and then hawk
them about the State at something much under.
And farmers “be sich fools ” as to believe that a
machine offered at less than the ordinary rates
must be bad. So they pass the local sort and go
to a Yankee brand —invoice price, £26 ; customs
valuation, £34; selling price, £9O.
The half-yearly returns from the State-
owned railways are the only items to cheer Q.
Treasurer Ividston in his revenue troubles. He
cannot understand how it is that there is a falling
off elsewhere while the railways indicate splendid
business.
The Mohawk and Archer, two late ships
of the Australian squadron, were recently sold in
London for £4850 apiece. Now seems probable
that we might have bought the whole late squad-
ron outright for an amount no higher than we
paid as one year’s naval subsidy. Is it the same
with our present “first line of defence ” ?
Even Maoriland’s comparatively high
tariff isn t sufficient, seemingly. Chairman of the
Kaiapoi (M.L.) Woollen Co. recently told a meet-
ing that local co.’s were quite incapable of com-
peting with the enormous quantities of shoddy
still coming in, and, to meet the demand for
shoddy garments, they had themselves to import
the shoddy.
M‘Kay, the big agricultural implement
manufacturer of Victoria, is pompously told by
the Argus that the great Harvester Trust of
America is a creature of Protection, and that it is
to be destroyed by lowering the CJ.S.A. tariff,
not by raising that of the Commonwealth. W 7 hat
the Argus shirks is the almighty fact that the
Trust is an omnivorous living thing, against which
Australia has to be protected. To argue as
the Melbourne Argus does is like trynw to
console the man in the alligator’s jaws with an
assurance that the animal should have died of
measles when it was a pup.
Still Yes-No. George Reid had said Yes
to the invitation to visit Queensland, and all
arrangements had been made to show him round.
Then, at the last moment, he wired No. And, by
a nasty trick of luck, the Yes-No is reported in
the same column of Sydney D.T., in which tne
complains of “the” horrid Yes-No title”
which has been given him.
The other Allan M‘Lean,
tootling his little “ anti-Socialistic ” toot at the
gaud Mayor’s quarterly feed in Melbourne, was
specially severe on “ the levelling down of
Socialism.” Contemplate the dead level, say, in
Railway Commissioner Tait’s salary of £3500 a
year, and the porter’s ss. 6d. a day. And the
railways represent Socialism, absolute Socialism.
Mr. Reid’s humor and logic (scraps from a
report of his Lismore meeting):—
Mr. Reid : The Socialist party
A Voice : The best party in the world.
Mr. Reid : That is all right. There are a few lunatics
about wherever I go. (Laughter.) I don’t mind meetino-
one occasionally, and I always humor them, because they
are dangerous. (Laughter.) Thanking my friend for the
great assistance be is giving me
The Voice : That's not argument.
Mr. Reid : Oh, I never argue with people in your state
of mind. (Laughter.) . . .
The Voice : You did what was right. i
Mr. Reid : Poor chap ! He will only get excited, and ;
then become more dangerous still. (Laughter.)
Then a sarcastic bishop got up and thanked Mr. i
Reid for his “intellectual treat.”
A resolution carried by the recent Vic.
State School Teachers’ con-
ference “ That the
scale of salaries be
a maximum of £2OO
for the fifth class
and £432 for the
first class ; and
that no certificated
teacher be paid
less than £llO per
year.” The de-
mand was unusu-
ally moderate, for
it is on the capa-
city, and wisdom,
of these men and
women that the
future of the coun-
try depends. But
how Bent will bellow
. at this request that the
teacher should have a minimum wage of £2 2s. 4d.
a week—less than the legal wage of a bootmaker
or a baker!
A Lismore (N.S.W.) speaker, Bishop
Doyle, explaining why they liked Reid
They all wished that Mr. Reid would come among- them
oftener. When he was there, years ago, he gave them
the fine bridge across the Tweed, and he had also loaned
the municipality of Lismore £lO,OOO without security.
(Laughter and cheers.) The pity was they had not pressed
him to forgive them the debt. (Laughter.)
Exactly. Mr. Reid is the man of concessions and
gifts—from the public purse. But as Federal
half-Prime Minister he can only give away post-
offices, and that limits bis powers of attraction
seriously.
Before the Sydney Lands Royal Commis-
sion Mr. W. N. Willis, beaten by 307 votes at
the State elections last July, stated that he
lost the Darling seat “ through people voting
three or four times on the same elector’s right.”
How was it, if this were so, that Mr. Willis did
not enter a protest? The popular impression is
that Mr. Willis was defeated through Mr. Sleath’s
failure to effectively split the Labor vote; Mr.
Sleath only polled 259.
Mr. Thomas Bent met an adverse audi-
ence at Prahran the other night, and Tommy is
seen and heard at his best when his audience is
casting gibes at his enormous bulge, and jeering at
his dray-horse intellect. On this occasion Tom-
bastes threw restraint to the wind, broke through
the thin skin of civilisation in which he sometimes
disguises himself, and ramped and roared like a
distracted savage. He bit, he glared, gave the
atmosphere awful blows, and rushed his speech
into the absurdest incoherency, tearing insanely
from one theme to another, and showering sen-
tences that had no proper beginning and were
never finished, but which were mainly splutter,
and barking, and abuse; and meanwhile he
sweated so that you could hear him slosh in his
boots as he cake-walked the platform in his foolish
anguish, and every time he threw up his half cwt.
of head he gave the chairman a shower-bath.
He darted at every subject tossed at him by the
hilarious crowd, like a terrier in a barrel of rats.
Bent was screaming that he had led as moral a
life as any man in the community ; and was
merging into a condition of dementia that threat-
ened apoplexy,, when the chairman intervened to
prevent fatalities, and eased him into a chair. It
was a weird example of the possibilities of a
democracy.
THE BAPTISM OF
AUSTRALIA.
“Of all the countries
of modern times Aus-
tralia was baptised with
liquor more than any
other.” —MR. DEAKIN, re-
cent speech.
It is announced that Maoriland Govern-
ment will, within the course of a few weeks,
acquire land in the Hutt-Petone district for the
establishment of workmen’s homes. The present
purpose is to lease the land at the rate of 9d. per
eighth of an acre, or Is. 6d. per quarter acre, but
it is probable the Government will house the
whole of the Petone (Government) workshops
employees, charging a rental commensurate with
a 5 per cent, return upon capital invested. The
extension of the principle to all workers in the
colony is merely a matter of time. Fat, who has
bought up all land near the cities suitable for
workmen’s homes, is already beginning to roar at
this gross infringement of the rights of private
enterprise.
A partner in the Sydney firm which
carried through the sale of Myall Creek estate to
the N.S.W. Government for closer settlement told
the Lands Commission last week that the com-
mission on the business amounted to £6715. Ex-
M.P. Willis’ little cheque was for £4232. That’s
the sort of thing the closer-settler is expected to
pay interest on ! And, apart from that, and out-
side this case altogether, what a lot of things may
happen when £5OOO cheques are in the air ! Even
a £lOOO cheque would cover a multitude of weak
spots in an estate offered for sale. That £6715
commission, which, of course, the selectors will
have to pay (or the general taxpayer, if the
selector can’t), amounts to 6715 good reasons for a
stiff graduated land tax.
One of the particular and
peculiar advantages of the Imperial
connection to Australia is in the
circumstance that the opinions of
any numbskull wandering from
this country become weighty, sig-
nificant, awe-inspiring and respect-
able when aired in London.
Some exasperated money-grub or
infuriated Conservative tick writes
to the Times that the tyranny of
Labor legislation is whipping the
Commonwealth to the dogs. The
Tory influence at the other end of
the cable seizes greedily on this,
and it is sent back to us with ail
the tremendous significance and
weight of Imperial recognition—
in the shape of the Times’ edi-
torial approval. Had the afore-
said grub or tick been restricted
to an Australian paper in criti-
cising Australian poliejq his words
would have been properly appreci-
ated as those of a dull, angry indi-
vidual, who imagines that his
country is crushed under an iron
heel every time his own corns are
trodden on. But we are blessed
with an Imperial connection, and it is not what he
knows about himself, but what his “mother
country ” is willing to think about him, that is
supposed to matter to the Australian.
N.S.W. Agent-General Coghlan reports
that Q. Agent-General Tozer’s Stinking-Fish yarn
in connection with the landing of his wife’s maid
is being widely spread in the English press
as an argument against Australia. Queens-
land Govt, should compel Tozer to eat the
leek without further delay. Where’s the sense in
the Govt, spending money to attract immigration
whilst its principal outside official is semi-officially
spreading a silly fiction which makes such
endeavors abortive ?
Vic. Premier Bent, in his recent Prahran
speech, when not uttering mad, meaningless
bovine noises, was flattering the female electors of
the Commonwealth with the finesse of a hippo-
potamus writing a billet-doux. “I trust to the
women,” he cried, “to stem this flood of Socialism,
of collectivism, of Yarrabankism.” But Bent
won’t give the women of Victoria a vote. Tommy
has said that his admiration of the women is
reciprocated, but he is evidently afraid that, if the
chance is given them, they will dissemble their
love to the extent of kicking him from the Trea-
sury benches.
G. H. Reid, in July, 1898 :
So far as the Labor party was concerned, although he
was not a member of it, he regarded it as one of the
greatest honors that had ever fallen to his lot in political
life to know that the Labor party found it consistent with
its public principles to give him a loyal support. He
could say to that great meeting that he was proud of his
connection with the Labor party. As an indication of his
gratitude for the support of that party during the last
four years, it would be noted that he had not allowed a
single candidate to go into the party’s electorates and
wear his colors in opposition.
And at that time one plank of N.S.W. Labor
party’s platform was: “The nationalisation of
land and the means of production, distribution,
and exchange.” Apparently George didn’t recog-
nise any “menace to the country” in the proposal
then.
There was a possibility in Victoria that
some of the working-women would vote against
the Labor party, owing, in some respect, to the
indiscretions of the party itself. But, wher-
ever the Female National Ass. has penetrated,
the result has been to thoroughly close up the
Labor ranks, and to ensure a fine, solid vote at
the coming elections. Nothing emphasises the
inequality of present social conditions better than
those meetings, where the society women gather
together and attempt clumsily to patronise their
social inferiors. It was a bright, warm day
indeed for the Labor party when the Tory
invented the Female National Ass.
JUST SO.
“ I’m going- to place myself right across the path of Socialism.”— REID.
The Anglican Synod and the Presbyterian
Assembly of Q. both sparred with the Kanaka
question last week. But after a few of the mis-
sionaries had beaten the air about the imaginary
cruelty and injustice of Kanaka deportation, both
bodies sheltered behind the gag of the “previous
question; ” it was pretty manifest that the
White Australia policy had a majority present.

(With the curtains gather’d and drawn),
With a hacking cough and a burning head,
Vainly waiting the tardy dawn.
When close to my bedside an angel came,
With a phial of liquid pure,
And I read on the label a magic name—
It was Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*

Legal Notices.
Statutory notice to Creditors
Re THOMAS CLARKE CHRISTMAS
DECEASED.
PURSUANT to the provisions of “The Trustees and
Executors Act of 1897 ” Notice is hereby given that
all creditors and other persons having any claims or
demands upon or against the estate of Thomas Clarke
Christmas late of Fortitude Valley Brisbane in the State
of Queensland Pianoforte Maker deceased and carrying on
business as a Music Dealer at Celtic Chambers George-
street Brisbane aforesaid who died at Brisbane aforesaid
on the second day of May 1905 leaving a will whereby he
appointed Annie Christmas of Fortitude Valley Brisbane
aforesaid widow sole executrix thereof are hereby required
to send in in writing particulars'of their debts or claims
to the executrix at her undermentioned address on or
before Saturday the tenth day of June 1905.
And notice is hereby also given that after that date the
said executrix will proceed to distribute the assets of the
said deceased in Queensland among the parties entitled
thereto having regard only to the claim of which she shall
then have had notice and that she will not be liable for
the assets or any part thereof so distributed to any person
of whose debt or claim the said executrix shall not then
have had notice.
Dated this twelfth day of May 1905.
NICOL ROBINSON & FOX,
Solicitors for the said Executrix,
272 Queen-street, Brisbane.

Sydney D. T. is not only unabashed and
unashamed at the severe reflections on its “stink-
ing fish ” policy,. contained in Acting Agent-
General Coghlan’s dispatch to Premier Carruthers,
but it has the green-hide hardihood to comment
smugly:
Australia has many unscrupulous critics in England,
against whose apersions its credit requires defence, and
there is undoubted need of some competent person to
take up the cudgels on its behalf.
The most “ unscrupulous critic” of Australia, and
the circulator of the most injurious aspersions on
Commonwealth “ credit ” and legislation has been,
and still is, the D.T.
Mrs. Gannon, who used to provide such com-
fortable quarters at Petty’s Hotel, Sydney, has, since
recuperating her health at her home-at Port Hacking,
taken over the well-known Grosvenor Hotel. The
Grosvenor has a fine site which, from the top of Church
Hill, looks down on harbor and suburbs on one side and
the heart of the city on the other.*
One interesting point of law was settled
bytheN.S.W. Full Court (4/5/’05) in the appeal
case of the Commissioners of Taxation against
Palmer and others, as to the rights of the Crown
as a creditor in bankruptcy. In this case the
Commissioners had proved for a debt in the ordi-
nary way in a bankrupt estate, and claimed
preference over all other creditors. But the
Judge in bankruptcy held, and the Full Court
supported his decision, that the Crown haying
taken advantage of the Bankruptcy Act placed
itself in the position of a subject, and lost its
inherent privileges thereby. This judgment,
however, does not affect the Crown’s right to sue,
by an old-fashioned process, by a “ writ of extent ”;
and it seems that any Crown debt so established
must be satisfied in full out of the estate of a
bankrupt before any other creditor can claim a
farthing.
All good smokers will be glad to hear that the
firm of Gallagher, Ltd., whose tobaccos beat the world’s
exhibits at St. Louis Exposition last year, and secured the
gold medal, are about to launch their leaf on the Aus-
tralian market. Gallagher’s is an independent Belfast
(Ireland) firm, and is not connected with any ring or trust. *
It was to have been expected that as soon
as Mr. G. H. Reid got into office, financial smells
would obtrude. Now transpired that one of the
first acts of the (who ought to have
taken a warning by theNeild case) was to appoint,
at £400 a year, an old familiar to write useless
articles to useless foreign papers about the Com-
monwealth. As State Premier, Mr. Reid first
appointed this gentleman to write articles about
the State. The See Government retired him
and appointed somebody else. The Carruthers
Government abolished the useless job. Now Mr.
Reid revives it for the Commonwealth !

Thousands Owe
Their Lives to the
Skill of This
Wonderful Doctor.
♦
Seems to Possess Strange
and Mysterious Power over
Disease.
Offers His Services Free to
the Sick and A fflicted,
Distance no Barrier.
For the past three years accounts have come from
America of the most' astonishing nature, regarding
marvellous cures that were being performed daily by the
famous physician and scientist, Dr. James W. Kidd.
At first these stories were received with considerable
doubt, but there continued to come reports of men and
women being restored to health after years of suffering.
Chronic and so-called incurable invalids were brought
back to perfect health. There seemed no affliction or
disease but what he might cure. Thousands of patients
were treated by the doctor until his name and the fame
of his marvellous remedies spread all over the civilized
world. Investigation has proved every assertion made by
Dr. Kidd. Some of the stories told and letters received
from grateful patients seem to indicate that this man’s
remedies possess a mysterious power over disease. No
matter how severe the case, how chronic, or how long
standing, what other men have said or failed to do. These
are the cases he especially solicits, because they give him
a chance to prove his superior ability.
When asked the secret of his success, the doctor
modestly answers that it is partly due to the wonderful
remedies which he uses and partly to the careful attention
given each case. He treats patients all over the civilized
world by mail, and seems equally as successful as though
they were present in his office. His greatest ambition is
to give every sick, afflicted and suffering man and woman
in the world a chance to try his wonderful treatment. In
an interview recently the doctor said: “I believe that
I have discovered a way to relieve most of the suffering
from sickness and disease in the world, and I believe that
it is my duty as far as possible to make this fact known to
all. For this reason, lam offering to send a free treat-
ment to every applicant, rich or poor, no matter where
they live, no matter what their disease. lam not a rich
man. I cannot afford to always give my medicine away,
but I am going to continue as long as possible or until
every afflicted person has had a chance to try and satisfy
themselves without expending one penny.” Do you
mean that you will send treatment absolutely free to every
one who applies? was asked. “That is exactly what I
mean. Any sick or afflicted person who will write to me
and describe their condition may have a trial of this treat-
ment entirely free. There are no conditions, no restric-
tion. Distance is no barrier. I cure as readily thousands
of miles away as in my office. A letter does just as well
as a personal visit.”
The above statement appeared in all the leading
American papers and from there to the English press. As
a result Dr. Kidd’s mail has been flooded with applications
for free treatment. All have been answered promptly,
but still they continue to come. For the benefit of his
thousands of patients in the British Empire the doctor has
been compelled to establish an office in London. To
secure a free treatment, it is only necessary to address Dr,
James W. Kidd, 486 Saracen Buildings, Snow Hill, London,
E C beino- sure to describe your case. Remember that
the ’doctor treats all diseases from the simplest and
easiest cured to the so-called incurable and chronic
afflictions. There is no disease that he may not cure.
In view of Dr. Kidd’s marvellous success, his standing
amono- America’s most famous physicians, his reputation
for honesty and integrity, this offer affords a remarkable
opportunity to all who are sick and in need of medical
help It means a chance to get well. It means the
opinion and advice of one of the world’s most famous and
successful physicians on your case absolutely free.

State enterprise. Also, within the past six days,
it has had articles advocating the curse to this
extent: —
(1) Calling upon the State to organise a weather
bureau, and see to “ the regular and punctual transmis-
sion of weather messages.”
(2) Calling upon the State to fight German trade in
Australia and the Pacific.
(3) Calling upon the State to do more and better in the
wav of technical education.
(4) Calling upon the State to “ earnestly insist on fair
trade in butter,” Private Enterprise having proved itself
a rogue.
(5) Calling upon the State to open up roads and provide
better means of communication to the Dorrigo (N.S.W.)
lands.
(6) Applauding the State for developing and advertising
Australian timbers.
Six days, six demands for Socialism. And yet
the good old soul is aghast at the Queensland
Labor party’s demand (which it doesn’t make)
for “Socialism in our time ” !

evidently counts for nothing unless the means for
shifting it are available. The race was between the
lightest of the crews and the . second heaviest. The
Tasmanians pulled a splendid race, but they were not
the finished oarsmen the Victorians were. The Victorian
stroke was machine-like, and the boat seemed to rise like
?u -j a ? d over the surface of the water. It was
the ideal of “ the temperate order in so fierce a cause,”
and against that cool, mechanical action the frantic

This was the 29th inter-State race. The first
was in 1878, between N.S.W. and Victoria. In 1885 Tas-
mania took a look in, just once in a way, and then the
two original competitors had it all to themselves till 1890,
when Queensland entered and beat N.S.W. for second

dl i ‘lS** beat Queensland for the second place. 1898 saw
only Victoria and Queensland compete. N.S.W. resumed
operations next year. Of the 29 races Victoria has won
r 4 ’ j. which have been successive victories since and
including 1894. The silly snobbishness of amateur row-
ing clubs m barring manual laborers, for many years stood

account facilities and proportion of water-edge popula-
tion, N.S.W. ought to hold the rowing supremacy.
The sculling championship was a long stern-
by Tasmanian T. H. Bourke, the champion since and
, including 1903, after Ivens, the Victorian. Four started,
but Edwards touched Evans’s (Tas.) boat, and had to slow
down to avoid a bad foul, and practically put himself out
of the race. Ivens shot away and led right down to
Tennyson, where Bourke spurted, and Ivens seemed too
pumped to respond. Bourke won easily, while the other
Tasmanian (Evans) came with a great rush and secured
second place. Since 1892 Queensland has held the cham-
pionship four tunes, Tasmania three, N.S.W. three, and
Victoria and Westralia once each.

The troubles which are now bobbing up over
the Towns v. Stanbury match were noted before the
articles were signed, and made one of the reasons why
Towns was emphatic in demanding three-quarters of the
boat receipts in case of defeat. It is scandalous that
private enterprise should jump the best positions
on the course ; but the agreement to row on the Hunter
. 18 a ac , er f ? r . the boat trust. Boat-racing is one
of those sports which the public patronises by squat-
ting on the water’s edge, and cheering the leading sculler
as he passes it is so free that there isn’t even a collec-
tion ; therefore, the action of Towns and Stanbury in seek-
ing a spot where proceeds are likely to be better isn’t to
be wondered at. By the way, the frequent statement
cnat old Stanbury is going to row “ young ” Towns is
.overdrawn Stanbury is only a year older than Towns ;
the chief difference between the two is that Stanbury is
about four stone heavier than Towns.

Darling’s decision to keep to Australian times
of play during the English tour will have a bad effect on
the gate. The populace will get tired of the monotony of
drawn games, and a great majority of the fixtures are
sure not to be finished without a Turner or Spofforth on
the visiting side. Australian method of adjournments
and delays on all manner of pretexts from a drizzle to the
loss of a trouser button, are all right when this week and
the next are available for the match, but are out of place in
three days contest. The idea of getting stale should not
frighten the team. It has 14 men and a playable
manager. r J
The Australian XI. match with Surrey ended in

which defeated the Australians. The time wasted when

haye j ust about given time for a victory. On some future

Charles Nugent, an English authority on race-
horses, seems rightly to have predicted that Moifaa, the
ex-Maorilander bought by King Edward to win the
Grand National, would be quite unbalanced at his iumns
by the fact that Richard Marsh, his new trainer, had
considerably shortened his tail. At all events, Moifaa,
deprived of his rudder, jumped very badly, and eventu-
ally fell. Tail docking is an ugly, cruel, and barbarous
practice, which ought not to be permitted. It is making
its way in this country among the Anglo-maniacs who
think it must he right because the English swells doit,
and who never consult the fly-worried horses.

race under V.R.C. rules and the “ ponies ” that don’t.

* * « *
The North Sydney-Balmain Rugby match at
Sydney Agricultural Ground, on Saturday, was an in-
teresting display of language and scrap. There were only
a few hundred people present, but had the teams adver-
tised the curious exhibition they were going to put up
the gate would probably have been valuable. It wasn’t
exactly a free fight, and it was still less like Rugby or any
other recognised brand of football. When teams get out
of hand and commence using language to one another,
and kicking and punching in the loose, it is time to close
the match and disqualify the offenders. With the British
Association and Australian games growing in popularity
every week Rugby cannot afford to have such displays.
* * * *
The new Sydney District Rugby team is a
strong one, and on Saturday it astonished the University
crowd considerably. The Sydney forwards were deadly,
and dribbled all over the Uni. forwards, and were only
brought up by the excellent combination of the ’Varsity
backs. Sydney backs were sound in their defence, but
lacked dash in attack, and could not make use of the
superiority of their forwards. It was by a wonderful
piece of play that the University at last scored, well on in
the second spell. The Sydney forwards immediately
evened things by rushing the Uni. team right on to their
line and bursting through. Most of the time after that
the Varsity was jammed back on its goal line and playing
a defendve game ; but had the Sydney backs shown more
alacrity the Uni. would have got the beating of its life.
The baby is a vigorous infant.
* * * *
N. Rugby Union, not having received anv
official news of the abolition of the field goal and the
reduction in the value of the marked goal by the English
International committee, has cabled for particulars In
the meantime the Cornstalks will play as before. As no
Australasian body has any say in the appointment of the
English council, it seems unreasonable that it should
impose its decisions on Australia without question.
Imagine the shocked groan that would hit the heavens if
Australia cabled that it had decided to increase the
cricket stumps to four.

* • * *
About the most exciting football match played
m Sydney on Saturday was the Soccer game between
Balmain and Glebe at Epping. From these two teams
will be drawn a large proportion of the Sydney team that
is to oppose the Maorilanders, who arrive here on the 17th
of next month. Although they attacked very solidly and
played with beautiful combination, Balmain was unable
to force it home ; but after each repulse they came again
with grim persistency, till at last the sustained effort was
rewarded with a goal. Then Glebe got possession, and
after a pretty struggle, managed to even up the score.
As the game went on each side redoubled its efforts. They
played with frantic zeal, and scored goal and goal about,
and after Glebe had secured the odd one, the Balmain
team failed to do before time ran out.
* * * *
The ex-vice-president ef the Yic. Football
Association says it would be better to have straight-out
professional footballers than the underhand business of
the present time, which is professionalism hypocritically
masquerading as amateurism, and trying to disguise its
identity by all manner of lies and deceit. As most of the
footballers have become professionals in all but name,
perhaps the best course would be to insist that the thing
should walk out in the daylight in its own clothes. A
disguise always suggests criminality.
* * * *
French motor-car builders are experimenting
with a workable machine for the use of kerosene instead
of petrol and naptha. Once an absolutely reliable kero-
sene-burning automobile has been invented it will, in all
human probability, become the freight-carrier of the in-
terior of this droughty country. The outlook for the
horse is getting decidedly gloomy.
Road-covering by bike is a strenuous occupation
in Maoriland. The distance between Wellington and
Napier is about 213 miles, and the journey includes a
climb over the Rimutaka ranges and eminences of lesser
altitudes, through about six different climates, and 18
different systems of prevailing wind. The record until
the other day was 18h. lmin., when O. G. Smith, winner
of last Christchurch-Timaru Dunlop road race, arose at
3 a.m. and battled out of one of Wellington’s gales and
into the fastnesses of the ranges, where assorted cyclones
happened to be playing football, and deposited Smith
on the doorstep of eternity. Smith pulled up at Carterton
(Wairarapa) for repairs, after covering 20 odd miles with
one pedal. The 50 minutes delay did not prevent him
reaching Eketahuna (where it rains eight days out of the
seven) in record time, but a severe storm—to which the
district is also addicted—compelled the plucky rider to
give the attempt best. A few days later, however, he
crossed the Rimutakas in the moonlight, and although
his spills were disconcerting at a spot where a train was
blown into a gully a few years ago, sufficient of him was
left to carry him to Napier and clip Ih. 37min. off the
record. Smith is only a'handful—stands five-foot nothing,
and weighs Bst. 61b. Fifty pounds, a bike and the glory
were the inducements.
The worried motorist sees another bogey rising
in his dusty track. At present his tyres, the set of four,
cost him, if they are made of the best Para rubber, any-
thing between £2O and £4O, according to thickness. And
the tyre is part of the machine that will not stand
economy—if it is not of the best material its life is briefer
than a shearer’s drunk. Even when made of the best
Para, the tyre is a comparatively short-lived article, and
a motor car without rubber pneumatic tyres would be
like a jaunting car running over the roads which are paved
with good intentions. There’s no substitute for the real
thing. Within quite a recent period the price of Para
rubber has increased by £220 per ton, and, as a further
rise is impending, the man who goes a-carring or wheeling
will have to face an increase in the price of his punctures.
* * * *
Two miles a minute is the record which the
automobilists of the world are striving for. So far, 110
miles an hour is the maximum.
* * * *
The proposal of the N.S.W. League of Wheel-
men to withdraw from the Federal Cycling Council is an
empty threat unless it can induce at - least the Victorian
League to also secede. If N.S.W. alone withdraws, it will
be N.S.W. League against all Australia. Which means
that the other States will provide more racing, more and
bigger prizes, and as they will have the money to import
the foreign crack riders, and the power to disqualify any-
one having any dealings with the N.S.W. League, the
Cornstalk crowd will not retain many racers. Of course,
the Council might take it lying down—but it is not likely’
and it’s better to remain in the Council and have a say in
its management than withdraw from it and live by its
grace only.
* * * *
The French motor firm of Serpollet is making a
special steam whizzer with which it hopes to establish a
new world’s record of somewhere about 130 miles an
hour.

The Australian cycle ‘ ‘ promoters ” have
announced “ no more appearance money for even foreign
cracks.” They say the extra cash will be distributed in
prizes. If the foreign rider will come all the same,
attracted by the big money prizes attached to the great
handicap races, well and good. It will give the Australian
riders a better chance to make a decent living without
“ cutting it up.”
* * • *
The hog in the car raised a considerable dust in
England recently. Somewhere in the midland counties a
child was knocked down and run over by a motor car, and
the driver fled on at top speed without deigning to
trouble about the victim. Popular indignation was
raised, and London Daily Mail offered £lOO for the de-
tection of the miscreant. • Then it was discovered that he
was the varlet of Hildebrand Harmsworth, one of the
owners of the Daily Mail. The Mail lost its head, with-
drew its offer of a reward, generally played the fool,
and sort of laid itself open to a charge of tacitly
claiming for Hildebrand’s driver the right to run over
children who get in the road. Meanwhile the child has
died, and the driver is committed for manslaughter.
Buttan Singh and Bert Woods, Russian
Lion Haokenschmidt’s discarded wrestling assistant
(alleged premier of the British navy), decided their
frequently postponed catch-as-catch-can match at Mel-
bourne Athenamm Hall last week, and the Englishman
showed poorly against his wiry ancient antagonist, who
is in the forties. Twenty minutes’ground work—Singh
atop mostly—saw the brown man bring the dreaded
hammerlock into operation, and as he added to the
agony by forcing the limb up towards the shoulder,
Woods writhed with pain till his second, Graham, cried
enough for him. It wasn’t possible for Woods to get out
of the difficulty, and further resistance meant a dislo-
cated shoulder or a broken arm, but all the same he
kicked vigorously at Graham’s interference, insisting
that he could have released himself, but refused to face
the ordeal again.

* * » *
“G. 8.” : “ Old Timer ” misapprehended me. I
compared Miss Kellermann’s times with those of the world
beaters to discount the nonsense which appeared in soin?
papers, and said that she had not the stamina to swim the
English Channel. As he raises the point of speed in that
connection, let me say (hat she is neither sprinter enough
nor stayer enough for the task. Experts agree that the
endeavorer must either have speed enough to get through
in daylight and with one tide, or must have stamina
enough to last through two tides and the extra dangers
and cold of the night. The slow swimmer leaves with a
favorable tide and nears the other shore to find the tide
running so strongly that landing (especially in the dark-
ness) is impossible. The swimmer must either cave in or
hang on till the tide slackens. All recent failures have
occurred through these causes, and the cognoscenti
select Jarvis as the man to dash through in daylight.
Now on figures Jarvis could swim on such a journey about
five miles to Miss Kellermann’s three, and the slower
the swimmer the farther he or she has to swim in conse-
quence of the tide, in such a journey.
♦ * * •
The international lawn tennis match in England
is fixed for July 8 and the week following. France,
Austria (a late and unexpected entry), and Australia all
have byes, Belgium playing the United States to start
with. The four left in then play two semi-finals and a
final (Australia playing Austria), the ultimate winner
having to meet England afterwards.
•■ » * *
Ping-pong has come absolutely to the end of
its tether. From a foolish game to amuse idlers and
British Army officers, it had a rocket-like career up to the
heights of an inter-State association, whose representatives
swaggered round the streets. Last year an effort was made
to resume “practice,” and a roster of matches was drawn
up. The effort was abandoned when a quarter of the way
through, and this year Ping-pong has not been men-
tioned.
* * * *
Mr. N. Joubert, proprietor of Lane Cove
(Sydney) ferry, has in his shed a launch of new and
original design. Her special feature is an arched bottom,
the open side of the arch being the lower, gradually sink-
ing to a flab bottom aft. The notion is to cause the least
possible displacement of water, and, lifting the boat on
the volume that rushes into the curve, to increase the
speed. The unsolved problem is her behavior in a sea-
way, but the designer feels confident she will keep on top
there too. Her dimensions are, roughly—Length, 30ft.;
beam, sft. ; shape something like the boats' at Manly
water-chute ; propelling power, oil.
“Edyson” : The fact that Australia is always
ready for a gamble makes the Eight Hours and the
Druids’ art unions immensely profitable, and apparently
the Australian likes his odds long. Two hundred and
fifty odd thousand Eight Hours tickets were sold, which
pans out at something considerably over £12,000, allowing
for commission and expenses in connection with the
lottery. For this the purchaser gets a chance of prizes
valued at £2OOO or so, and an afternoon’s or evening’s
sport. To the vast majority of the purchasers, who do
not go near the ground on the appointed days, the thing
is a straight gamble, and the odds against the investor
are enormous. Still, the tickets sell like hot cakes, the
devil-me-care Australian’s motto, “Take care of the
pounds, and let the shillings take air of themselves,”
accounting for the fact that so many invest. A well-
conducted national lottery might be made to wipe out
Australian debt in a few years, and it may not be wholly
foolish to make use of a national vice to remove a
national evil. Of course, the hallelujah party will
strenuously oppose any project that gives the wicked the
chance of sinning with the very best intentions, but the
unregenerate statesman cannot overlook the fact that
the people who get furious over the rape of a lock in the
shape of taxation come cheerfully to be fleeced in a
gamble.
* * * *
Bruisers Hiam and Eraser met again at Sydney
National Sporting Club last week. Both remembered the
former unpleasantness, and seemed to be imbued with a
most thorough-going- respect for the other’s offensive
artillery. Their previous fight was a thing to make tigers
glad. In comparison this was a mightv cautious re-
approachinent, but some fearful blows were struck, and
the men began to sag early. In the seventh round
Fraser lost his balance, and in a moment the marine had
jolted him on the jaw, and down went Fraser.
* * * *
The cheerful sports who shouted 4 to 1 on
Walcott, when that colored brother met Nicholls at the
Gaiety on Saturday night, fell in badly. They were
treated to a boxing match of 20 more or less lukewarm
rounds, at the end of which Nicholls was declared winner
on points. The only excitement was when a series of
rather sharp uppercuts dazed Walcott so that he hung
on and hit, and an excited individual foamed at the
mouth and tried to climb into the ring, only to be
hauled back ignominiously by his hind legs. Otherwise
there was a painful absence of comic relief about the
show, which was dull.
* * * *
“0.K.”: Speaking from memory only, I would
say that the Oxford and Cambridge crews have lost bulk
within the past 20 years. The Oxfords this time were
small at both ends of the ship, for whereas No. 5 scaled
13.12 or thereabouts, the four lightest men averaged 11.2,
which was the exact weight of the stroke. The stroke of
the Cantabs, a week before the race, was a mere trifle of
10.5 J, nearly 2st. lighter than No. 7. I call to mind some
very successful University stroke-oars who went higher
than 12st., and I’m wondering whether big men are less
suited to modern racing- conditions or the supply of them
has diminished.

Better and Brighter.
Miss Margret Raven, op Sydney, Writes
a Letter Teli.tng now .Toy Overcame
Her Despair.
“ There is no joy like the joy of being again in
perfect health after you have been disabled, so to
speak, by the pains and after effects of a long,
tedious illness.” That is the spirit of a letter
written on December 7tli, 1904, by Miss Margret
Raven, of 127 Quay-street, Ultimo, Sydney, in
which she most graphically describes the pains and
the wearying effects of the disease —indigestion—
that tortured her for years.
In her letter she says : “I don’t think anyone
in the world has suffered more cruelly from indi-
gestion than I have. For years it blotted all
happiness out of my life and brought me almost to
the grave. It made me weak, thin, pale and
nerveless, unable to eat, sleep, work or enjoy any
of the ] Measures of society. I was then residing at
Hyde Park, Adelaide, South Australia, of which
city lam a native. My case baffled all the skill
and knowledge of my doctors. In the end I could
not retain food on my stomach, and the mere sight
of food often made me retch and vomit till I was
exhausted. The straining, from this cause fre-
quently brought on bleeding from the lungs, which
alarmed me lest it might be consumption. About
two years back, on the faith of advice from a near
friend, I began to take Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
“ To my great joy Mother Seigel’s Syrup proved
to be quite as good as my friend had represented
it to be. From the first it enabled me to retain
my food, and within a couple of weeks I regained
my lost appetite. Day by day there was a marked
improvement in my health and appearance.
“I felt like a better and brighter being. Instead
of moping about the house, dejected and listless, I
was soon able to take active interest in the affairs
of life. After a few months of perseverance with
the medicine my health was thoroughly re-estab-
lished, and I still remain quite well and happy.”
The strongest testimony in Miss Raven’s case is
contained in the seven words at the close of her
letter, “I still remain quite well and happy.”*
AT POVERTY POINT.
The late Antoinette Sterling had been a pupil of
Manuel Garcia, the great singing teacher, but Antoi-
nette’s soulful, songful “ suspirations of forced breath”
when she visited Australia gave no idea of her early vocal
culture. She was then suffering from fatness and reli-
gious hysteria, and she did some of the things that
Garcia had formerly taught her not to do in voice
production. With Santley, another of Garcia’s pupils,
the case was quite different. His songs didn’t appeal
particularly to the multitude, but his method was an
education to local singers.
Ada Reeve has sued an English paper for saying she was
drawing £250 a week. She pleaded it would frighten
managers off if they thought she wanted so much. She
got an apology and her expenses.
Declared now that even “ Yankee Doodle ” was “ made
in Germany.” Johann Lewalter, an eminent authority on
Polk Songs, has traced the tune to Wasenberg, which in
1770 was the central depot of the Hessian troops engaged—
on the wrong side—in the War of Independence. Even
to-day many tunes survive in the neighborhood of
Wasenberg which very much resemble it.
The late Charles Arnold struck Australia for the first
time at Melbourne Bijou in 1888, when Brough and Bouci-
cault were running the theatre. He brought “ Hans the
Boatman,” a precocious kiddie, and a St. Bernard with
him, and played the name-part in his novel show on
“ sharing terms,” the rest of the clever co. being organ-
ised by B. and B. Myra Kemble was the leading lady,
and Pattie Brown (who didn’t spell her name with an “ e”
in those days) made a particular success as Jeftie Thursby.
Three of the men who supported Arnold were “Bill”
Leake, H. C. Sidney, and Lash Gordon—dead long ago, all
of them. “ Hans the Boatman ” carried Arnold trium-
phantly through a long tour, and gave him the Australian
name which, in after years, enabled him to make a fortune
with the bustling Yankee farces —“ What Happened
to Jones” and “ Why Smith Left Home.” Meanwhile,
he had paid a second visit to this country in partnership
with Prank Thornton. Thornton wanted to bring
“Charley’s Aunt” as his own piece, but Penley, the
original “ Aunt ” in London and the owner of the English
version of the comedy, wouldn’t let him have the Aus-
tralian rights, so Arnold got the rights and formed an
alliance with Thornton. In the first place they produced
“Captain Pritz,” a comedy built on “Hans the Boat-
man ” lines in which Arnold hoped to repeat his previous
success. “Pritz” scored a failure; “Charley’s Aunt”
hit the mark. That was in ’93. Arnold returned to Eng-
land richer in pocket but somewhat dimmed in reputation,
partner Thornton having put him into the shade. The
funny little she-inale impersonator came back to Aus-
tralia a couple of years afterwards with his own co. No-
thing more was seen of Arnold until he started to coin
money by means of “Jones,” likewise “Smith.” He
had astounding luck in his last venture. Coming at any
other time he might have done no better with “Jones”
than the merits of the piece deserved. It was not a
brilliant farce, nor was Arnold a remarkably bright come-
dian, but he happened to catch the right moment, and
that was all about it. Por twenty years or more he had
lived upon his other successful piece, “ Hans the
Boatman,” which possessed the grand business virtue of
an attraction for women and children. Arnold will be
remembered for his Hans when many cleverer artists of
his time are quite forgotten. The performance was
likeable, good enough in its decorative way, and distin-
guished by a certain manly femininity that seemed more
or less natural to the actor in private life.
Charles Panning, a Sydney man, well-known to patrons
of Rickards’ shows, writes from Johannesburg, where
he has lately been performing:—“ The charge for
admission to the theatre here is much higher than we
have been accustomed to in Australia. Front stalls and
circle, 10s. 6d. ; stalls, 7s. 6d. ; pit and first gallery, ss. ;
top gallery, 2s. 6d. ; weekly promenade tickets, one
guinea ; yet the house is crowded nightly. The theatre-
goers here are ‘ dead-struck ’ on show-folk, and are lavish
with presents. Our little girl had a birthday surprise-
party recently, and was loaded with enough toys and other
articles to start a small shop. But some of the women
performers are very ‘ strong.’ One woman was given '
four diamond rings, besides bangles, necklets, silk dresses,
and other ‘articles of bigotry and virtue,’in four weeks.
I am doing a parody on the ‘Holy City’—Jewhannesburg
—with a local bearing on the Chinese question, in bur-
lesque Kaffir lingo. It caught on wonderfully. The
bars do a wonderful trade —the cheapest drink,
such as lemonade or soda-water, costs a shilling.
Johnny Coleman’s sister, Nelly, opened here on 27th
February. She is his living image, but only about half
his size ; you may guess she is not very big. She does
male comedy business, very much after Johnny’s style,
but far ahead of him—so you can understand she is
clever. She had heard a report of her brother’s death, of
which we were entirely ignorant and could give her no
information. Her husband is also with the company ;he
models in clay. Saiii Austin and his wife, Rae Cowan,
both well known to Sydney audiences, arrive here in a
day or two. Billie Barlow was appearing successfully at
this theatre when her husband died in England.
The most notable Garcians that Australia has heard
since voice production begad to be talked about were
Charles Santley and the younger Hall 6, who gave lectures
on singing with vocal illustration. But Garcia must have
been a very ancient man when he took Charles Halle’s son
for a pupil.
The “Silver King,” the most popular English melo-
drama of the age, is one of the very few melodramas that
don’t include a titled person among the characters.
There isn’t even a majah or a captain in “ Silver King.”
No dude, no low-comedy man-servant or parlor-maid, no
heavy father, mother, aunt or uncle. Its success is due
as much to the unconventional plan of its characterisation
as to the strength and “ improving ” nature of the plot.
Cleanliness, both political and physical,
often depends upon the inducement to purification.
And the increased popularity of the Turkish
Baths in the Royal Arcade, Melbourne, may be
the result of the splendid improvements in that
desirable institution. “ I was always here pretty
often,” says a prominent habitue in the Parlia-
mentary line of business, “ but since Daly has made
the old place into a palace, I’ll be hanged if I can
keep away !”*

bed, attired in a silk robe of the Rose du,Barri hue, which
she changes in presence of her admirers prior to chocolate
being served. Another scene is the f&te at Louveciennes,
with a Bacchante dance. . . , ,

“ The Pierrot ” : Wm. Anderson is the luckiest manager
in Australia. Everything he touches turns into money.
At the time of writing he has four companies going, and
all bringing him in good money. It is only five years ago
since he made a start at Ballarat. He made a bit of
money, I believe, Out of a speculation, and with it ran
Charlie Holloway at the old mining town. Then he went
into partnership with Holloway, and, since, nothing he
has touched has failed.
A handsomely got-up volume is the
“ Tourists’ Handbook of Australia,” just issued,
gratis, by the Howard-Smith S.S. Co. as a souvenir
of the firm’s jubilee. Mr. Donald Macdonald,
who is responsible for the letter-press, deals graphi-
cally and picturesquely with the five Australian
States, and their principal cities, enterprises and
industries.

“F.C.”: When some Australian Premier
hasacquired nous enough to inaugurate-a system
whereby prisoners can be put. to useful and re-
munerative work, they should be credited with
what they earn. Then when the cost of their own
and their keepers’ keep—including police, niain-
■ tenance of gaols, etc.—has been deducted, they
should be discharged when they have accumulated,
say, £5 over the damage they have done. ’ Thus a
burglar would be sentenced, not to two or three
years’ hard labor, but to earn the assessed amount
of damage and injury he had done—perhaps £2OO
or so. A man who assaulted another and broke
his jaw would be ordered into confinement till he
had earned the amount of the loss occasioned by
his unruliness—the up-keep of himself and his
keepers,- his wife and family, the .victim, till he
i was able to go to work again, the latter’s wife and
' family, and a solatium for the pain and inconveni-
ence occasioned the victim. It might take him a
■ good while to do.this, but that would be his own
funeral. At any rate, it would.be a vastly more
; reasonable system than the present, under which
a man who breaks another’s jaw may get any-
thing from one month to five years, according to
the temper of the jury and the liver of the judge,
and the man with the broken jaw gets nothing.

A Letter from the South.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thursday Island is just the same as ever. Pilots in and
pilots out. Jack has gone south with a 8.1., and Frank
arrives here to-morrow with the outward-bound Jap.
Remember me to Old Broad-street and the Flea-pit—l’d
rather you be there than me.— From Jim J ’s Thurs-
day Island letter.
The fog folds up before mine eyes, the sky is
strangely light,
The smells of Soho, like the half-spent fumes of
dynamite,
Are changed to tropic scents of fruit, and sea, and
Mitchell grass,
Of paw-paw and the isles of pine that wall Whit-
sunday Pass.
The blue and gold of Torres Straits, and Thurs-
day’s white bleached sand ;
The thunder on the Reef drowns the reverberance
of the Strand ;
And the Plea-pit and Old Broad-street are hidde»
by the day;
And I am south of Thursday—twelve thousand
miles away ;
The squalid streets of Manchester, the gloomy
halls of loot,
Forgotten with the Clyde side—and the misty
Kyles of Bute—
My homesick heart outleaps the poles of
space and sea!
The Memory flies unto the places where the Man
would be.
So “Frank steers north the Japanee“Jack
takes the 8.1. south ” ;
And Jim is going west—the lust of New Tracks
in his mouth;
And Elder’s streaming every month his half a ton
of tin;
And I am here in England, hunting dross that
fools do win.
But yet, for that I am a man, Ambition spurs me
on
To leave the great and golden plains whose suns
for me have shone —
To follow to my Destiny, whose pains do never
cease—
To flee the silent haven and the snowy hill cf
peace.
For that some men are dull as bees; for that some
men are born
To gather color to their lives, as poppies in the
corn;
For that the wheat—the bread of life—is baked
for all by fire.
For some also the poppy grows—the symbol of
desire.
And, ineradicable, while my leaping pulses beat,
Love doth my life incarnadine as poppies in the
wheat.
The love of life, the love of love, the love of
land and home!
The love of doing, that made once the world all
rose and chrome—
The love of Splendore—the South wind’s daugh-
ter, strong and kind,
Who bore me north to Torres Straits, and left the
towns behind.
The raven days of London go, I see a Southern
night;
A ship inside the Barrier Reef—a bay of mobile
light;
The eyes of brown and hazel —the smile upon her
lip—
My lady looks unto me and gilds for me the ship;
Gold light upon the silver, from the ship-lights’
changing bars;
The sea a fecund meadow-land sown carelessly
with stars.
The long-drawn days upon the plains—the star-lit
sandy camp ;
The creek parched by the sunlight making night
soft, sweet and damp;
The one clean love that matters spurring me unto
the chase !
Here—miles of shame, and every one with the
same harlot face.
The summer nights Australian, tropic scents of
fruit and flower—
And ended grudgingly because too short is every
hour;
The midnights in the garden, when every voice is
inute,
And fancy made the night deceive with fancy’s
Lover’s Lute.
Here —for your lute, there is the stolid, squalid
drunkard’s song;
The voices of the night repeat themselves in
horror long;
The icy wind’s shrill shrieking as the house-eaves
take the shock;
The woman’s cries in labor; and the thief who
breaks the lock.
The sounds of war and hunger in this hell of slate
and stone —
And lonelier than a lonely sea the man who lives
alone.
Down at Cairns, and up the Barron, where the
mighty cedars rise,
There is half-content for me, although not there
the loved one’s eyes ;
At Thursday, where the pilots land and sail down
to the South —
Although not there the name that is as honey to
the mouth ;
The meanest rock of all my land holds almost all
content,
And here—are streets, and usury, and life as
naught is spent. . .
Back from the Old World sail I South as
birds fly home at night,
To be done with the foolish tramps to see, when
lifts the Borda light.
Ah ! to do it again for the joy of it all—the track
that was ever new.
God love me ! the track that was rose and carmine,
and the sea of mobile blue.
But never again the barren plain—the seas whose
woes increase—
Back to the haven of silences and the snowy
hills of peace.
LONDON.
Randolph Bkdfop.d.
A London fashion paper has secured Bernard
Shaw, the well-known novelist and playwright,
as a contributor to write on prevailing fashions.
And he objects to starch. He will not “ wear a
shirt smeared with disgusting white mud.” Shiny
white tubes on the wrist repel him, and the rain-
pipe trouser leg arouses his wrath.
At an electioneering meeting t’other night
in S. A., a budding but nervous politician rose in
turn, blushed, and said with much confusion that
he supported everything his leader supported,
and sat down. After all, it was a sensible speech,
or substitute for a speech. But it didn’t taka on
with the crowd.

Horatio Bottomley and Tanqueray Todd are
sueing certain members of London Stock Exchange for
“conspiracy and slander” in connection with a concern
called Hannan’s Trust. This wild-cat was floated in 1896,
but, according to Todd, did no business till 1904. It then
took over Bottomley’s Market Trust, and its litter of wild-
cats, and also some alleged goldmines held by Mt.
Yagahong Exploration. “Bears” of Hannan’s Trust
shares got cornered, and said nasty things of Bottomley
and Todd. Hence the row.
* » * *
April crushings, Charters Towers, totalled
18,090 tons for 15,4090 z. Cyanide treated 16,354 tons, for
82240 z. Principal yields—Brit. Central 2350 tons,
10440 z. ; Phoebe 805 tons, lllOoz. ; St. George 1965 tons,
151 loz. ; Queen Cross -.'981 tons, 4337 oz. ; B. Extended
2024 tons, 8430 z. ; Victoria and Queen 104 tons, 208 oz. ;
B. and Wyndham 1930 tons, 13840 z. ; Mills United 895
tons, 5320 z. ; Carrington’s P.C. 51 tons, 400 oz. ; Q. Central
449 tons, 511 oz. ; Marshall’s Queen 517 tons, 2500 z. Some-
thing better was expected from Marshall’s ; mine reports
said—“ Shows gold freely.” Twenty co.’s made calls
totalling £14,175, while six co.’s declared divs. (including
one liquidation) amounting to £16,078. Brilliant St.
George with buyers at 15s. for paids, holds first place
in the market; then Mills United 10s., Queen Cross 9s. 6d
Carringtons 4s. 9d. Phoebe, with a credit balance of
about £14,000, is on the 4s. 6d. mark.
* * 7T *
Unless something new is discovered or more
economical work is done, the Towers will be in a bad way
shortly. Brilliant St. George and B. Central are barely
earning their divs., and, with the Queen Cross, they con-
tribute £13,600 out of a total of £16,078. Mills United
and Phoebe threaten to come to the rescue, and possibly
will for a while. Average cost on the field is about £2los.
a ton. It needs to be a high-grade show to stand that for
long. No attempt is usually made to grade the stone or
sample the mine, so as to work on scientific lines. Argu-
ment used is, if the gold’s not there, you can’t get it. So
the crushing-s are gouged out—there’s no other term for
it. When it goes 3oz. a ton they are good men ; then
when the ioz. stuff is reached it is hard luck. Nothing
less than £oz. is any use, and even on that some will make
calls. The managers are not entirely to blame for this
sort of thing. All they have to do is to report how much
sinking or driving has been done, mention whether they
have put a new clack in the water bucket or not, and keep
count of the number of trucks hauled. “ The office ”
manages the mine—and supplies the unemployed with
letters to the figure-head.
* * * *
Day Dawn Gold Mines, Charters Towers, is in
trouble. Total output for six months was 516 tons
valued at £1195 18s. 2d. Of this tributors got out 227
tons, worth £491. Reef found by the drill is supposed to
be the Talisman. Directors are considering how to test
it, for it means 400 ft. of sinking on calls. Co. has £lOOO
on fixed deposit, but there is £1129 against it.
* * * *
Carrington’s P.C., outside the Towers, has to
date crushed about 100 tons for 7700 z. Show is 30 miles
away, and (some say to keep shareholders off) is wire-
netted. No information is supplied except through the
manager’s fortnightly report, and that mav be censored
for all the public knows. Main shaft is down 60 feet
When the public learned that crosscut had located the
lode, there wasn’t a share to be got—market cleared.
Worth noting that lip paid-up shares are changing hands.
A Huntingdon mill and winding-engine are to be erected
on the claim. Late Gov. Geologist W. H. Rands is
inspecting. Show may be equal to Mount Morgan or the
Vesuvius for all the Towers really knows.
* * * *
“Old Fossicker” offers a suggestion -.—Re the
extraction of gold from sea-water, what is wrong with
this: Let £oz. Premier Reid coat the bottoms of the
ocean-going steamers with quicksilver, have a clean-up
on arrival in port, report-the yields weekly, and apply
the proceeds to pay the mail subsidy ? If the battery"
man’s salary is made good enough, I might be persuaded
to take the job.

Tin mining is, to a very large extent, in the hands of
Chinese fossickers ; and very little capital is used in the
business. The alluvial fossicker, working on the same

every important mining centre and cities of Common-
wealth. Correspondence invited. *

Constant R. : Return would be about 6f per
cent. ; but risk is very considerable.... W. : (1) If it cost
£3OO to build the walls of a house and £2OO for woodwork
&c., and the whole is insured for £2OO, the insurer would
get the full £2OO in the event of a fire which left the walls
uninjured, but destroyed the rest; the condition of aver-
age would not apply. (2) Under similar circumstances if
the house were insured for £5OO, the insurer wou’d get
£2OO, the amount ,of the actual loss. (3) If a house had
been insured for 20 years, and was then destroyed the
insurer would-receive-the amount of his loss,df it were
within the amount of policy ; bonuses are deducted from
premiums and are not Cumulative. (4) If a house is worth
£5OO an’d is '.insured for £lOOO, and is totally destroyed
the insurer receives £500; a fire insurance policy is an
agreement to indemnify against actual loss only, and the
insurer could not give the necessary statutory declaration
that he had lost £lOOO.
Now stated, on very definite, authority,
tfiab N.S.W. Premier Carruthers has decided to
abolish the Arbitration Act, giving, as a poor sub-
stitute, a Wages Boards system, such as rules in
Victoria. This explains why he is now weakening
the Act by refusing to administer it.
ihe political situation in Bananaland grows
uncertain. Treasurer Kidston has offended
-Minister for Agriculture Denham because he is
too radical, and also the Labor party because fib
is not radical enough. It is questionable whether
the Government will live long enough in the
coming session to carry any popular legislation
Many of the Morgan' party are disaffected,
lvidston s strength lies in the fact that neither
party can do without him. A split eventuating
he is the one man in the present Parliament fit to
load a Labor Government. • - • -
In London Daily News, John Burns
favorably compares the crowd at the late Oxford
and Cambridge boat race (in habit, temper
dress, and behavior) with that of 20 or 30 years
ago. He heard no bad language, saw no betting
or gambling, and met no drunkards. He puts the
difference down to 30 years of free education,
larger schools, concerted play, collective drill]
methodical diversion, the diffusion of knowledge,’
and to the wider opportunities for outdoor and
indoor amusement.
A monument has been erected in London
to the Jews who fell in the Boer war. But what
about a monument to the Jews for whom England
fell into the war ?
Proposed re-distribution of Federal elec-
torates in N.S. W. will take from the rural districts
one seat (which happens to be that which the ex-
Premier, Mr. J. C. Watson, holds) and adds that
and the new seat to the city and suburban repre-
sentation. It is more than possible that the
redistribution scheme will not go through Parlia-
ment, as there will be strong Victorian opposition
to the proposal to deprive that State of one of its
members.

Sydney, that home of the blatant Bulletin, which

A gentleman is seldom a colonial success, and it takes a
colonial more than a generation to become a success as a
gentleman.
An amateur pottery competition at the
recent Adelaide Chamber of Manufactures’ show
was won by one of the blind men engaged in
basket-making. The wheel and the method of ma-
nipulating the clay were submitted to him for the
first time the night before the contest. He made
quite a presentable basin, and won on his merits,
no special points being allowed for his sightless-
ness. Some of the things made by the amateurs
who joined in the struggle were of the weirdest
character, and no one dared to give them a name.
G. K. Chesterton puts the matter forcibly
from the White Man’s point of view in February
Fortnightly, in a New Year’s Eve political tria-
logue, which ends thus
Dr. Paul: I can’t understand your pro-Russian business
and you a Republican. The Japs have Parliaments,
education, the best modern art and science. What have
you and the Russians in common ?
Mr. Desmond (throwing open the shutters and letting
m a flood of pale light) : New Year’s Day.
Again, re Gladstonian magnanimity—from the
same article:—
The concessions made by the Gladstonians have almost
always been made to small Powers, such as the Boers, the
Greeks, or even the Irish. And it is obvious that this
kind of concessions cannot really raise any doubt of the
nation’s military power. The Tory concessions were
nearly all made to great nations. In a word, I like the
Gladstonian magnanimity simply because it really was
magnanimous. But I dislike your Torjr prudence towards
1 ranee or Russia, because—well, because it is really
prudent.
Q. Treasurer Kidston on some land ques-
tions :
During my northern trip I visited the Atherton district,
near Oairns, where there is some of the finest land in the
State. The soil in some plaoes is 60ft. to 70ft. deep.
Climate is temperate and healthy, situated, as the locality
is, on a sub-tableland. It is curious, however, that most
of the cultivation is done by Chinamen. They secure
leases from white settlers, who seem to be well satisfied
with the Chow’s rent as a return. This Asiatic-alien
settlement question calls for attention at the hands of the
Government. At present there is no power to prevent
the leasing. Consideration of the whole question is press-
ing and urgent. * f
In sugar cultivation a remarkable development has
taken place.* I admit that this was undertaken by the
small farmers, and I do not believe that white labor will
be a success on large plantations. But whereas 20 years
ago people scouted the idea of growing cane by white
labor as far north as Mackay, now two-fifths of the
growers were employing white labor only.
So white labor will have another good effect—
burst up the large estates. Australia wants
small farmers. It can do without the large sugar
planter.
“M.J.C.” writes
. Re “Westralia’s Crying Need” (B. 20/4/’O5). I have
just arrived at Albany from Perth, and have completed a
tour of inspection of lands on the great Southern Railway.
My object is to advise a brother in Rhodesia whether
after his engagement with the British Chartered Com-
pany expires, it would be to his advantage to take up land
or not. From the trend of your article it would appear a
settler with small capital would not have much show in
obtaining a fair selection accessible to the line. Exclud-
ing the districts of Beverley, Katanning, Wagin, and per-
haps one or two others, my experience is that good land
near the line can be obtained at a considerably less cost
and with better prospects than in any of the other States
It is only within the last few years that Westralia has
realised the extent and .the fertility of its soil, and the
climate is the best in Australasia.

Bttt t fttn correspondent •
A common notion amon/old bushmen is that they have
a legal right to camp and graze their horses, &c., on river
banks, up to two chains from the level of the stream. Is
there any foundation for this idea 7
-yyhich a law-authority answers with this
i dea se ems to have sprung from the almost uni-
yersally accepted, but erroneous, belief that the cus-
tomary Crown reservation of 100 feet fronting the sea
and arms of the sea cannot be disposed of by the Crown
for an estate in fee simple. Nevertheless, such disposi-
tions Qf dai]y occurr |’ nce . and altho ugh all navigable
rivers and arms of the sea are common highways, subject
j. Q unfettered use of the public as far as the waters
are concerned, the banks and beds thereof are in
no way distinguishable, legally, from other Crown lands.
The law governing riparian rights in Australia is the
ancient Common Law of England, modified by various
State statutes to some scarcely appreciable extent. And,
generally speaking, throughout the Commonwealth, a
landed proprietor whose holding is bounded by a river
possesses half the river bed; while, if the river passes
through his holding, he owns the whole of the land
over which the water flows. His rights, however,
as to the water itself have in some States,
and notably in N.S.W., been legislatively restricted.
The travelling bushman’s position is therefore: Unless
he is on a stock route or protected by some special local
statute, he has no legal right whatever to camp or graze
his horses on any river bank. If the river is a navigable
one, he may row or sail or swim through the riparian
owner’s land without committing an offence, but imme-
diately he touches a private-owned bank he is a trespasser,
and similarly he trespasses if he crosses the riparian
owner’s land to gain the water.

Health Notes.
(By W.S.C.)
In order that we may keep the body in the best condition to withstand the daily stress and strain
of mental and physical wear and tear, it is essential for us all to understand something of the Various-
processes of nature which ensure the maintenance of life. Most people do not realise that it is as
necessary to life that the body should waste away as it is that its substance should constantly be
replaced by a sufficient supply of fresh air, water and food. The wasting of the body is accomplished*
by the production of a certain quantity of used-up material, and this used-up or dead material must
be thrown off from the body by natural means without undue delay, for if any of it is suffered to
remain in the system, it becomes actively poisonous, and must cause disease or death.
Upon the skin, lungs, kidneys and liver rests the main responsibility of extracting poisonous-
matter from the blood and removing it from the system. The moment one of those organs, through
weakness or disease, becomes incapable of performing its work efficiently, that moment we begin to
suffer from some illness, and if steps are not taken to restore to health and activity the organ at fault,
death from some form of blood poisoning must ensue.
The skin, as a general thing, needs but regular treatment with good soap and water, and it will
do its duty perfectly and without complaint. The lungs are vigorous organs, and we are soon
made aware of any interference with their action by a fit of coughing or a choking sensation. It
is the importance of looking well after the health of the kidneys'and liver which we especially desir©
to impress upon our readers. These organs are the most delicate of all those engaged in extracting
and removing from the blood the waste poisons as fast as they are produced in the body. Unless the
kidneys and liver are in a fit state to thoroughly perform their task, the particular poisons, which it is
their duty to make and extract, will remain in the system, and as the kidneys and liver are often
unable to tell us directly, by a sensation of pain, that there is anything the matter with them, we
are probably unaware of their failure until we begin to suffer from Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick
Heidache, General Debility, Gravel, Stone and Bladder Troubles. All these complaints are caused
by the presence in the body of urinary and biliary poisons, which, if the kidneys and liver were
acting efficiently, would have been duly removed, and we should have been spared from suffering.
The kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten or twelve -grains
in weight, of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly
an ounce. If the kidneys are working freely and healthily, all this solid matter lea,ves the body dis-
solved in the urine, but if through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their work
properly, a quantity of these urinary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins con-
taminating the whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning such as Rheumatism',,
Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, Gravel, Stone, and Bladder-
Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are healthy is to place some, urine, passed!
the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until next morning. If it is then
cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural color, or has particles floating about in it,
the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must be immediately taken to restore their vigor, or
Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many manifestations of uric poisoning will result.
The liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made
from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver takes
sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be affile to again supply it
to the blood, as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble,
into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles which have
lived their life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from some
form of biliary poisoning such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia, Jaundice, Sick Headache, General
Debility, and Blood Disorders.
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys and that done by the liver,
that where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys, the liver becomes affected in sympathy,
and vice versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this close union of the labor of those vital
organs which resulted in the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as Warner’s ■
Safe Cure. Certain medical men, knowing what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine
could be found which would act specifically on both the kidneys and liver, devoted themselves to an
exhaustive search for such a medium, and their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in .
compounding a medicine which possesses the required quality in the fullest degree. Warner’s Safe
Cure exhibits a marvellous healing action in all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys .
and liver, and restoring them, as it is able to do, to health and activity, it, of necessity, cures all com-
Elaints due to the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the
idneys and liver naturally eliminates the poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons
cease. Cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent simply because they are natural. [s.]

There in the golden afternoon «*- ..
Soft airs from belts of pine and palm
Wander, all fragrant-warm, to swoon
For sweetness on the still lagoon,
Faint with the balm -
Of breath of lilies, sinking soon.
There the still streams glide, calm and deep,
Flowing low-voiced by lawn and lea,
With languid murmuringS as they sweep ;
And silver shallows croon and creep
To meet the sea,
Whispering themselves all day to sleep.
No frowning heights, no mountains loom
Like shadows on the sunny day ;
Only the low hills, pink with bloom— IT
Only the hollows’ soft-blue gloom
Far, far away,
And breaths o subtle-sweet perfume.
Let me my weariness confess,
Gathered to some deep-loving breftiSt;
There let me pluck the flower and press
The fruit of fair forgetfulness,
And find me rest, ■: 7 ’
Clasped in a kind, calm, passionless caress.;.’.
There let me lie and so forget
The past, its passion and its pain j ‘
And all the thousand thorns that fret
The sad soul, rich with love’s'regret.
And grief in vain—
Let me forget! Let me forget!
There let my listless days be spent,
Untouched by passion’s restless tire.
The long years calm and indolent,
Will pass me by with kind consent,
And no desire
Shall vex my days with discontent.
But in this land of sun-drenched leas,
And changeless skies, blue over all;
Of deathless summer, balmy breeze
And languid streams and dreaming seas,
The heart must fall
Aweary of its endless ease ;
And sometimes, siqk withJouging,'"Turn

And heart of youth, the raptured sighs,
The old sweet pain,
And all the ecstasy of love’s surprise.
“0 for a keen live wind to wake
And scatter all the languid flowers.
O for a breath of storm to make
Confusion on the shores, and shake
The hanging bovvers, 1
Imaged forever'in the dreaming lake.”
Ah me ! the Lotus Isles are fair, Y.
