MAUD PEACOCKE.
The Serpent.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
ADAM was vaguely alarmed when Eve
told him. It was a possibility that had
never occurred to him. .
“Do you mean to say he has proposed to you ?”
he said.
Eve’s eyes had a pathetic shade. There was no
doubt this apple tempted her, but—with limita-
tions. She nodded.
“What did you say?”
“I —I put him off.”
Just then the cause of their conversation, the
Serpent, arrived and claimed her attention at the
piano. Adam, hitherto unsuspicious, watched
him bending sympathetically over her as she
dreamily played, with a quite new feeling of
restlessness. The Serpent was Adam’s friend.
None of the other beasts had his capacity of
twining around the affections. The lions had too
many notions. The pigs were not exactly sociable ;
and various crawling things Adam did not like.
But he had liked the Serpent—until now.
“Eve,” whispered the fascinating reptile, as he
leaned his elbow against the piano ; ‘‘ am I to
have your answer? Will you take what I can
offer ? It will be a life of happiness to you.”
Eve glanced backward at Adam, and flushed at
his sulky face.
“ I will tell you to-morrow,” she said.
* .* * *
The Serpent had gone, arid. Adam lingered,
declining an invitation to accompany him.
“Look here. Eve,” he blurted out, desperately,
“ I saw you whispering; you’re not going to
marry— him ! ”
Eve’s eyes were drooping.
“ I said I would give him his answer to-mor-
row,” she said. ’
“Eve, Eve, you must not marry him !” said
Adam passionately. “I want you to marry me ;
I love you, dear ! I love you ! ” \
• * * *
So Eve offered the apple to Adam that the
Serpent had offered her ; and the Serpent bit the
dust of the earth.. .
But Adam and Eye lost their. Eden they
married !

called out “ All right,”
When .the shot had fired, Jaek looked at his
' watch. ’ “Twenty to seven, Joe ! I’ll just go in
and have a peep and see what she’s done, while
you warm the Hilly.”
Joe lit two short pieces of candle, stuck them in
a dab of clay and placed them on the level.
Round the lights he built three large stones, and
on these he rested the billy. Then he spread a
bag over an empty dynamite-case, and made him-
self comfortable. Joe was a Cousin Jack, and
knew all the points of the game. Aldridge came
out presently,- in a hurry. His face startled Joe.
“ Damme, me son, what ails tha’ ? Didn’t she
? tear ?”
; “Joe, old man, get hold of that tail-rope and
pull meaip. This is no place for us to-night.”
Joe nearly choked.
“ Has the smoke gripped ’un, Jack ?Or has tha’
seen a ghost?”
“Get hold of the rope, Joe, damn you! I
haven’t seen a ghost, but I’ve seen the Peerless
Pebble reef in there —as strong as the Bank of
England.”
* * * *
Night-time in Kalgoorlie, and a bustling,
merry, drinking crowd in Sharp’s saloon. Sud-
denly Aldridge pushes through the throng, walks
-up to the bar, and'-says something in low tones to
Maud.
“Blow me,” said Sammy, “if Jack hasn’t
plucked up to speak to her.”
“Hello! Here comes the almighty Smart.
And she’s gone straight over to him—talkin’ dead
serious, too. Smart ’ll win, hands-down; he’s a
cert. Come an’ try something. ”
“I’ve a mortgage over her reputation, as she
calls it,” said Smart, as he smoked a fat cigar and
settled back in his chair. “ I lent her £2OO
to-night, on condition that if she doesn’t refund
within a week I’m to take her instead. Her old
man is in trouble over in Sydney, and she wants
to save him. . Some old gaol-bird, I suppose.
However; she’s as good as mine. She hasn’t a
hope of paying.”
Then Binns, the agent, came in, and whispered
to Smart; “ A bit of a move in the market to-
night, sir. Somebody’s nibbling at‘Hopes’. Three
thousand went out at sixpence, and then 2000
more were bought at sevenpence. Sellers want
tenpence now, but the nibble won’t go past eight-
pence.”
“ What crowd are buying, do you know ? ”
“Someone for a Sydney syndicate, I think;
they’re going on the blind. What about you,
sir?”
“Oh, roll them in! Get tenpence if you can.
If you can’t, take anything. Put ten thousand
in.”
Binns went out.
Tobacco-stnoke filled the room, and it was
becoming exceedingly hot. Smart suggested the
balcony, and the company followed him up the
stairs. He selected a deep armchair, and leaned
comfortably over the railings. The big open-call
Exchange was right opposite, and they could see
and hear quite plainly the transactions. Binns
came back. He had unloaded at ninepence.
Smart nodded, and continued to watch. He
listened carelessly.
‘ ‘ Buyers two-and-six, sellers three—any better
buyer or lower seller in the market ? ”
There was a pause, and a slight stir, and then
the broker’s voice again :
“Buyersfive—buyers.five-and-sjx-j sellers silent.
Buyers seven —eight—nine—buyers' ten.” Smart
sat up.
“What the has happened over there?
What are they selling ? ”
The broker’s voice again : “Maid of Hope,
buyers 10s.”
‘ ‘ Hey ! ” roared Smart, standing up suddenly,
“have they all gone mad ? You there—Smith—
what’s happened to the Maid ?”
“What, don’t you know?” answered a voice
from the street. “They cut the Peerless lode
in the Hope this evening. She’s a ‘ bute,’ too;
coinin’ right underfoot.
The ‘ King-Pin ” was looking so ill that Maud
brought him a drink unasked.
“Did you unload in the rush?” he asked,
grimly.
“No.”
“Then you’re a rich woman to-night, Maud ?”
She smiled meaningly at the company.
“I shall be able to redeem myself'now !”
As they laughed Aldridge came in. He walked
across to Smart, and placed a roll of notes on the
table before him.
“ What does this mean ?”
“Maud tells me that she owes you two hundred
pounds; lam responsible for my wife’s debts.”

Impressions of S’African
Politics.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOUTH AFRICA seethes with politics.
In the Transvaal, the talk is all of
“ Representative ” or “ Responsible ”
'Government; in Cape Colony, it is the Afrikander
Rond; in Natal, “ rebates ” and the iniquities of
the “freight ring.” (Why, by-the-way, does not
the Commonwealth join with Natal in establishing
a direct line to London, via Durban, of 20-knot
turbine steamers for mails and perishable produce,
which might also serve as naval cruisers ?) But,
underlying all this confusion of terms, there is only
one great matter in contest, viz. : “Shall South
Africa be Dutch or British ? ” The issues are
■everywhere extremely clear; but their solution
will be a test of statesmanship for many years.
* * * *
Note first the conditions of the problem. South
Africa is a country of black men. In the four
colonies of the Cape, Orange River, Transvaal,
and Natal, there are 3,228,000 blacks (not including
101,000 Indians and 30,000 Chinese) and only
1,189,000 of European descent. And the blacks
are all warriors, while in the Cape some of them
have votes. Next note that the European element
is divided by race-animosities. The Dutch have
never forgotten that the Cape was taken from
them by the British ; they still maintain their
separate language, are slaves to a Calvinistic
religion, and remain fixed in their own ideas.
The “ loyalist ” politician estimates the position
thus
“The Dutch” are always essentially anti-British.
Whatever appearances of goodwill and'friendship to-
wards English rule they may profess for English edifica-
tion, their “ predikants ” and political leaders preach an
unremitting race hostility. A Bond leader will make a
speech in English at a mixed gathering which is full of
sweetness and conciliation, and the next day write in
Dutch an article for Ossnand, breathing resistance and
defiance in every line. The predikants, who rule their
flocks with the meticulous, yet comprehensive tyranny
of a Paraguayan priesthood, may, for form’s sake, preach
moderation in a Sunday sermon ; but this will not pre-
vent the employment during the week of the boycott
and other forms of petty persecution, if the district in
which the loyalists are living is remote enough or Dutch
enough to make the use of such weapons safe. All the
congregations know that when a predikant preaches
charity to Britishers his tongue is in his cheek. Yet the
Dutch are so clever and smooth-spoken that every public
man, at one stage of his career, gets the disease of
believing that the two races can be brought together ; and,
as a rule, the better he is, the greater will be his difficulty
in shaking this off. For such a belief is a disease, and
comes from an ignorance of the Dutch character. Let
one broad fact be taken as a test. There is no teaching
University in all Cape Colony. Even the Bond Govern-
ment, when Mr. Rhodes was Premier, could not overcome
the opposition of the Dutch clergy, who were afraid that
a University would attract their students from the cleri-
cal seminary of Stellenbosch. Now, before Englishmen
can acquiesce in a Dutch supremacy, the Dutch must
move with the age and realise that they no longer live at
a Geneva in the time of Calvin. The first step is freedom
from clerical tyranny and abandonment of their attitude
of social isolation. The best friends of the Dutch, and
those whom the Dutch themselves most respect, are the
public men who speak quite plainly of their determination
to maintain English rule. Once let the Dutch realise that
there will be no yielding on this point, and they will
quickly enough accept the inevitable ; but the policy of
conciliation is as irritating and futile as it is dangerous,
because no concessions will appease a race-prejudice,
which may be suppressed by a policy of equal justice, but
can never be reconciled. Accordingly the best hope of
peace lies in a strong rule and a fixed determination to
maintain the British supremacy.
These may seem extreme sentiments; but it
must be remembered that the memories of the
war are still vivid, and that the Dutch, w r hose
loyalty is in question, were only three years ago
burning and killing within 50 miles of Cape Town
itself, while at the same time they were support-
ing the Transvaal Boers with arms, sympathy,
and money in every other part of the colony.
And if the language of the British party is
“extreme,” their acts have not erred on the side
of severity. These stirrers-up of. civil war—these
Cape rebels who broke their allegiance—have been
punished by the victorious loyalists for their crime
of treason by being deprived for five years of the
right to vote —that right which two-fifths of
Australians never trouble to exercise. Would any
other nation have shown equal leniency ? Would
the Dutch, if they had won ?
As a consequence of this disfranchisement of
rebels, the loyalist Government has now a majority
of six, which has enabled it to carry through a
re-distribution of seats, which, by making votes
more nearly of an equal value, will diminish the
electoral power of the more sparsely populated
Dutch districts. “One Vote One Value ” is, for
the same reason, a loyalist rallying-cry in the
Transvaal. The Cape Parliament has three more
years to run (Parliaments are quinquennial in the
Cape Colony); and the Government expects, by
that time, that the Dutch will have acquiesced in
the inevitable and learnt to recognise that British
rule means liberty with justice.
• * * *
Now for the Dutch side of the case.
First, like the Irish, they appeal to history
where all is m their favor. Beginning from the
time when they were cheated of the compensation-
money for their emancipated slaves, as they be-
lieve, by the British Government, but in reality
by swindling agents to whom they had given
powers-of-attorney to collect the moneys which
with incredible fatuity the Government of the day
had declined to pay except in London. Beginning
that is to say, from the first trek early in the 19tli
century down to the proclamation of the Boer
iiepublic after Majuba Hill, British dealings with
the Boers have been a record of inconsistencies
evasions and duplicity ; due, not to bad faith, but
to the lack of a coherent policy. Thus the Boers,
who are tenacious of their grievances, consider it
axiomatic that the British Government cannot be
trusted, because it will undo to-morrow whatever
it may do to-day. They see, for instance, that
many Dutch loyalists have received less compen-
sation-money . than some of the very rebels who
destroyed their property, and are convinced that
■“Loyalty does not pay.” They say, however,
that they will not on that account rebel again.
“ Out of the fryingpan into the fire ; ” and
they have a specimen of German rule in West
South Africa which satisfies them that, whatever
its faults, English rule is preferable. They declare,
then, that they are a much misjudged people;
that they are too sensible to provoke another war,
and desire nothing now but “ political equality ”
and “majority-rule.”
This is a plausible and attractive cry. But it
would require more knowledge of South Africa
than I possess to draw the line between “ Justice
to the Dutch” and “Injustice to the British.”
* * * *
I express no opinion. But I do not like the
refusal to allow a teaching University; and
Bulletin readers will recall that Milton found by
experience that “Presbyter is but Priest writ
large.”
The Transvaal is undergoing a transition from
Crown to Responsible Government, and the new
constitution may be promulgated any week. Its
main provisions are already known. There is to be
a Parliament on the British model, but the Cabinet
is not to bs responsible to Parliament alone, and a
certain number of Ministers will be Crown
nominees. Such a constitution is only intended
to lie provisional, and it is expected that full
Responsible Government will be given within five
years.
British opinion is divided on this question of
delay. One section insists that the Colony needs
rest and that it would be foolish and injurious to
plunge it into the turmoil of party politics before
it has fully recovered from the disorders of the
war. “Time,” they say, “ must be given to allow
the animosities of the war to simmer down and
accustom the Dutch to Parliamentary institu-
tions.” They urge, too, that there is not yet
sufficient community of interests or knowledge of
each other in the miscellaneous congeries of for-
tune-hunters who have flocked into the country
since the war, so that it would be specially
dangerous to give the control of a complex system
of administration, which is hardly yet in working-
order, to men of no Parliamentary experience,
elected by equally untrained voters. “Let us,”
they say, “ have one Parliament upon the mixed
system, which will give members experience and a
sense of responsibility, and the electorate an
opportunity of estimating the qualities of candi-
dates, and then we shall be ripe for Responsible
Government in its completeness.”
These are in effect the views of the British
Government, which appear to Vie approved of by a
majority of the British party. A section, however,
of the British, composed—strange alliance —of
Laborites and millionaires, object to any delay.
“ The colony.” they say, “is being run by
strangers—young men fresh from Oxford or the
army—who draw high salaries for little work.
These men know little of the country (one of
them, by the way, is Dr. Jameson, of W.A.), and
are starting the colony upon wrong lines, because
they are out of sympathy with the farmer and
worker.”
The Dutch, always acute to take advantage of
British divisions, have not as yet definitely
declared themselves ; but they are believed to be
in favor of immediate Responsible Government, if
they can obtain it upon their own terms of repre-
sentation according to localities and not to
numbers. On this point, however, all sections
of the British are united against them, “One
Vote One Value ” being in their eyes an essential
basis of representation.
The fight is really between city and country —
between the miner and the farmer. On
a basis of “One Vote One Value” Johannes-
burg rules the Transvaal. On any other basis
control must pass to the Dutch farmers, and the
great Rand industry will lie in a more parlous
plight than even in the days of Kruger, whose
laws and proclamations could be evaded by judici-
ous bribery.
Now, without the Rand mines the Transvaal
would be bankrupt, as it was before they were
developed, and the financial position of both the
Orange River and the Cape colonies would be
very seriously affected. Imagine South Australia
being suddenly deprived of both the Broken Hill
and the Westralian trade, and we can picture an
illustration on a small scale of what the conse-
quences would be to all South Africa if the mining
industry of the Transvaal were to receive a serious
check.
But the unmentioned issues of this contest are
of even wider import. The ostensible dispute is
over the form of an Electoral Act, but that which
is really at stake is British supremacy in South
Africa; because if the Boers could only obtain a
majority in the Transvaal Parliament the Dutch
in the other colonies would at once agitate for
Federation.
Natal, of course, is British to the core, but the
Orange River Colony could always be relied upon
as a set-off against Natal. Rhodesia, too, will
always cast a British vote. Thus, if the Dutch
could get possession of the Transvaal, a South
African Commonwealth would consist of two
Dutch and two British colonies, with the Cape
Colony to hold the balance. Now parties in tne
Cape are so nearly equal that the added weight of
Boer influence in the Orange River and the Trans-
vaal might at any time kick the beam in favor of the
Dutch, and thus the Afrikander dream of a Dutch
South Africa would be realised through the con-
stitutional agency of a Federal Parliament in a
manner at once more effective and more difficult
to deal with than .any form of quiet or open agita-
tion. For how long, for instance, would South
Africa remain a British country after its Federal
Parliament had passed a law making Dutch the
official language ?
Thus the Transvaal is the key to the situation ;
and the unanimity of the British party in that
colony in support of “One Vote One Value”
becomes easily intelligible.
* * • *
I found it, however, impossible to understand
in a short stay the reason for differences among
the British upon other questions ; but I am sure,
nevertheless, that the complaints against the Admi-
nistration are in the main unfounded. That mistakes
have been made may be readily admitted ; and
two of the Ministers appear to be unpopular. But
I am certain that no popular system of govern-
ment will give a more honest and efficient Civil-
Service, or deal in a more thorough and scientific
manner with the problems of the war. Still one
may hold this opinion—which, indeed, is general
—and yet insist that it is better for a people to be
governed by themselves with moderate skill than
by strangers with supreme efficiency. The success
of the present system is therefore not a conclusive
argument against a change.
It is certain also that the notion current outside
South Africa that the present Government is in
the interest of the millionaires is ludicrously in-
correct. The most bitter opponents of Lord
Milner whom I met were mine-owners; and the
story is that whenever stocks fall a point, the
Exchange will say it is Lord Milner’s fault.
Indeed, some of the mine-owners avow that they
want Responsible Government, in order to obtain
a squeezable administration, and get rid of the
taxes on their profits which Lord Milner has
imposed. For the rich man “squeaks ” when he
is taxed, just as loudly in South Africa as in
Australia. But how he would squeak in Australia,
if one of our “democratic” Governments were to
put a tax of 10 per cent, upon the profits of gold-
miners, or 65 per cent, upon the profits of a
diamond-mine ! No one, indeed, could have less
sympathy than Lord Milner with the aims and
methods of the modern millionaire ; and the two
measures of taxation to which I have referred
should convince the most incredulous that he
cannot be fairly charged with partiality to the
capitalists.
. “How, then, did he come to sanction the
introduction of Chinese labor ? ” This is “ another
story,” which may fittingly form the subject of
another article.
B. R. WISE.
